==PAGE:4==
Abstract
This thesis explores how makerspaces can be meaningfully integrated into Danish el­
ementary schools through the detailed design and development of the Gnist Education
concept. The project investigates three central research questions:
RQ1: What factors are influencing a school’s decision on integrating a mak­
erspace in the Danish elementary school and how is this affecting Gnist Edu­
cation?
RQ2: How might a detailed design of the Gnist Education concept be devel­
oped to integrate the makerspace in a Danish school setting?
RQ3: How can the developed solution(s) be assessed and validated to strengthen
the integration of the Gnist Education concept and what significance will it have
for the startup?
Through a research­driven design approach, including a systematic literature review, em­
pirical studies, and multiple design iterations, the study identifies key pedagogical, tech­
nical, and organisational factors that influence the successful adoption of makerspaces.
Central to this is a user­friendly makerspace that can adapt over time and implement
smart solutions to improve user experience and usability. Likewise, the role of teacher
confidence and self­efficacy is key to a successful adoption, which is shaped by both ac­
cess to adaptable curriculum materials and support structures for technical and didactic
implementation.
The outcome is a modular, mobile makerspace situated in a container, developed along­
side a professional development course featuring an adaptable curriculum, and a corre­
sponding business and implementation model. These components were integrated and
tested in a real­world school setting through a full­scale MVP test in collaboration with
Gladsaxe Privatskole. The test confirmed the usability of the physical makerspace and
its potential to foster student engagement in Technology Comprehension, while also high­
lighting the importance of teacher preparation and logistical planning.
While the MVP test provided promising results, further pilot testing across more diverse
school settings is recommended to validate broader applicability. The study concludes
that the integration of physical, pedagogical, and organisational design elements can pro­
vide a holistic and viable framework for expanding makerspace access in Danish elemen­
tary schools, with significant potential for both educational impact and business sustain­
ability.

Use of Artificial Intelligence
In the interest of academic transparency, this section acknowledge the use of generative
AI. In this thesis, ChatGPT and Microsoft co­pilot have been used to refine formulations,
and explore ways of structuring content in the report. Further, AI have been used to
improve grammar and sentence clarity to keep a consistent academic style.
All AI­generated content have been treated as a preliminary draft, not as the final output.
Any suggestions and corrections were carefully reviewed before rewritten and included in
the thesis.
Terminology and Aberrations Used in this Thesis
In this thesis, various terms related to the school system will be used. The table below
provides an overview of some of the most frequently used terms along with their defini­
tions.
Table 2: List of terminology and how it is used in this thesis
Term
Definition
21st century skills
Refers to a broad set of competencies students need to thrive in a
modern society, including critical thinking, creativity, collaboration,
communication, digital literacy, and adaptability.
CFU
Center For Undervisningsmidler
Common objectives or
National common objectives
A list of goals and objectives the students have to learn at specific
grade levels. Made by the Ministry of Children and Education.
Curriculum
The plan for what the students have to learn about a specific topic
and the activities they will go through to learn it.
DRM
Design Research Methodology ­ The structure the thesis follows.
DS­I
Descriptive Study I ­ The second phase of the DRM framework.
DS­II
Descriptive Study II ­ The fourth phase of the DRM framework.
Elementary school
Covering primary and lower secondary education (in Danish
“Folkeskole”), also known as K­9 in both public and independent
schools.
Experienced teachers
Teachers with more than 5 years of work experience.
In­service teacher
A teacher who is currently active in service and employed at a
school.
Continued on next page
vi
==PAGE:8==
Term
Definition
Makerspace
A space with equipment for fabrication, e.g. 3D printers, laser cut­
ters, podcast studio, etc.
Ministry of Children and
Education
The Danish ministry in charge of rules related to education and the
school system. Sometimes referred to as “the ministry”.
New teachers
Teachers who have less than 5 years work experience.
Pre­service teacher
Student in the teacher education
PS
Prescriptive study ­ The third phase of the DRM framework.
RC
Research clarification ­ The first phase of the DRM framework.
Scaffolding
When the teacher provides a clear structure and goals and supports
students in their learning process.
School board
A governing body responsible for setting policies, overseeing bud­
gets, and making key decisions for a school.
Special needs
Students with special needs cover the students that are extra tal­
ented and need a challenge as well as the students who have cog­
nitive, physical, or mental impairments.
STEM/STEAM
Science, Technology, Engineering, (Arts), and Mathematics
Subject
The subject in which the curriculum is taking place, e.g. math, biol­
ogy, english, etc.
Syllabus
What the students have to learn in each subject during a year.
Teaching material
Anything used to represent the content of a subject with learning as
the main objective (T. I. Hansen & Skovmand, 2012)
Technology Comprehension
The new test subject that will be mandatory from 2027. In Danish
“Teknologiforståelse”
UDL
Universal Design for Learning.
UFP
Ultra Fine Particles

==PAGE:14==
1
Introduction
In a world increasingly shaped by rapid technological change, the ability to understand
and engage with technology is becoming more and more essential. As a response to this
development, Denmark is introducing a new subject, Technology Comprehension, in ele­
mentary schools. This calls for new learning opportunities and pedagogical approaches.
Makerspaces offers a promising framework for addressing this need. However, imple­
menting makerspaces in schools also presents a range of challenges related to access,
usability, pedagogy, and interdisciplinary learning.
This thesis investigated how these challenges can be addressed through the development
and testing in collaboration with Gnist Education.
1.1
Overarching Problem
Beginning in 2027, Technology Comprehension will become an integral part of the curricu­
lum in Danish elementary schools (Børne­ og Undervisningsministeiret, 2025). As part of
this educational shift and transition, makerspaces aim to become a key learning environ­
ment that both support individual and collective creativity through advanced digital fabri­
cation tools. This provides students with hands­on learning experiences through complex
and technically challenging school projects that reflect industry practices and processes.
These learning environments empower students to engage critically and creatively in a
world driven by technology, by fostering innovation and promoting interdisciplinary learn­
ing.
Research underscores the value of makerspaces in cultivating creativity and innovation.
Soomro et al. (2023), finds that especially three overarching areas positively contribute
to this effect: Interdisciplinary collaboration, digital fabrication tools (e.g. 3D printers and
laser cutters), and student­centric maker­based pedagogy. Loertscher et al. (2013) fur­
ther defines and present makerspaces as a flexible physical or virtual learning environ­
ment, with creative and uniquely adaptable tools and materials, where students can ex­
plore, design, solve problems, and invent. In the Danish makerspace context, Hjorth et
al. (2017), has demonstrated, in a study with Danish students from age 11­15, that ac­
cess to makerspaces significantly enhances students understanding of digital fabrication
technologies.
However, a study by Buus (2021) on behalf of the Villum Foundation, show that there is a
lack of accessibility for Danish school children to experience makerspaces. This is shown
by mapping elementary schools in 83 out of the total 98 municipalities in Denmark. A
study by Pilgaard et al. (2022), shows that of these 83 municipalities, 62% of elementary
schools have access to at least one makerspace. The study further shows that 52% of the
responding municipalities are in the process of exploring possibilities to acquiring more
makerspaces. Despite this potential, it still shows an uneven access for school children
across Denmark, meaning that many are missing valuable learning opportunities that a
makerspace can offer.
Implementing and maintaining new makerspaces does however not come without its own
set of challenges. Equipment can become underutilised, due to technical barriers or limi­
tations, and varying user needs. Additionally, high initial cost and maintaining can present
financial barriers, impacting the level of implementation. Lastly, unsuitable curriculum and
teacher responsibilities, can lead to abandoned or misused equipment and tools. This can
1
==PAGE:15==
undermine the overall success of a makerspace, which negatively impact the learning op­
portunities within them. These challenges are resistlessly what Gnist Education ApS aims
to address.
1.2
Gnist Education ApS
Gnist Education is a start­up launched in the fall of 2024 aiming to spark curiosity, better
the understanding of technology, and its application among students in the Danish ele­
mentary school. This is done by providing a mobile makerspace for elementary schools,
thus being the prerequisite educational platform to the new school subject Technology
Comprehension launching in 2027 (Børne­ og Undervisningsministeiret, 2025).
1.2.1
The Five Pillars of Gnist Education
Five overarching pillars were created to guide the development of Gnist Education. These
included, Container Development, Curriculum Development, Software & IT backend, Busi­
ness plan & Logistics, and Community Development.
This thesis project will explore three of these pillars: Container Development, Curriculum
Development, and Business plan & Logistics. Some elements of Software & IT Backend
will also be explored and included in the combined container development. Community
Development will likewise be touched upon in the curriculum development. These ele­
ments were chosen and formulated into three focus areas, in a combined effort to create
a coherent concept that will allow Gnist Education to test and gain valuable user insights
of the detailed makerspace MVP.
1.2.2
Focus Areas
As Gnist Education originally was developed as an idea and turned into a concept, this
thesis project aims to bring the concept to life by developing a detailed design of the
makerspace platform based on three focus areas.
These areas will focus on designing and optimising a functional, user­friendly makerspace
container, creating customisable curriculum materials through co­creation workshops with
teachers, and develop a business model through engineering systems design. By em­
ploying a systematic approach, we will prototype, test, and refine solutions to ensure
seamless integration of the physical, educational, and strategic components to overcome
the implementation barriers. Figure 1.1 below shows how the three focus areas will allow
a common goal of the makerspace development to be reached.
2
==PAGE:16==
Figure 1.1: Overview of how the three focus areas will be used to reach a common goal.
1.3
Research Questions
Three research questions will guide the design process. They will be formulated based on
the Research Clarification phase, and answered during the Descriptive Study I, Prescrip­
tive Study, and Descriptive Study II. Here, the questions are presented to give a quick
overview of what will be covered during the thesis.
RQ1: What factors are influencing a schools decision on integrating a makerspace in the
Danish elementary school and how is this affecting Gnist Education?
RQ2: How might a detailed design of the Gnist Education concept be developed to inte­
grate the makerspace in a Danish school setting?
RQ3: How can the developed solution(s) be assessed and validated to strengthen the in­
tegration of the Gnist Education concept and what significance will it have for the startup?
3
==PAGE:17==
2
Related Work
As the Gnist Education concept has evolved from an early student idea into a functioning
startup, multiple academic projects have contributed directly to its development. These
include bachelor projects, special courses, and master’s theses that together explore key
dimensions such as container design, curriculum development, logistics, digital infrastruc­
ture, teacher onboarding, and inclusive learning. This section first presents an overview
of these parallel and preceding student projects, which have collectively informed and
supported the integrated makerspace concept developed in this thesis.
Beyond these project­based contributions, the thesis also builds on a broader body of re­
search into makerspaces, STEM education, 21st century skills, and the emerging subject
of Technology Comprehension in Danish elementary schools. These perspectives pro­
vide the pedagogical and contextual foundation for understanding both the opportunities
and challenges of implementing makerspaces in formal school settings. Together, the
practical insights from student collaborations and the theoretical grounding in contempo­
rary educational research form the dual basis on which this thesis is situated.
2.1
Previous and parallel Student Projects Related to Gnist
Education
Gnist Education began its journey in 2024 where a group of students took on a proposed
project in the DTU course: Holistic Design of Engineering Systems. Since then, multiple
teams of students have worked alongside the startup. The following sections are out­
lining both the previous projects to form the starting point of this thesis, as well as the
parallel projects. During the thesis period, there have been a tight collaboration between
the parallel projects, sharing insights and planning testing sessions together. Figure 2.1
illustrates the full timeline of Gnist Education, including the start and finish time for all
projects.

==PAGE:18==
Figure 2.1: Gnist Timeline
2.1.1
Holistic Design of Engineering Systems 2024 - Design Park
In February 2024 a group of students from Holistic Design of Engineering Systems under­
took the task to design and develop the first concept of Gnist Education school­oriented
mobile makerspace, under the alias Design Park (Larsen et al., 2024). The design team
consisted of Ebbe Bliksted, Emilie Østergaard, Jakob Hedegaard Poulsen, Johan Lindb­
jerg Skovbæk, Linnea Wedel Wiemann, Mads Hjortkjær Nielsen, Sofia Folmer and Philip
Wiehe Larsen. Together they investigated and analysed the makerspace situation at the
Danish public primary schools, mapped the influenced stakeholders needs and require­
ments and developed a set of recommendations and design proposals for future devel­
opment. The main takeaways from the project was:
• Wall mounted equipment for optimal use of space inside container
• ConceptModularised wall mounting system for a more dynamic and flexible mak­
erspace solution
• Taylored teaching material to accomodate the various use of equipment in the mak­
erspace container
• Identification of strategic enablers
