==PAGE:19==
2.1.2
Special Course - Innovation Container
The Special course of InnovationContainer focused on maturing the concept of the “con­
tainer based mobile makerspace”. The special course was initiated over the summer by
Philip, one of the original members of the DesignPark team. Philip saw the potential of the
concept and had an urge to explore it further and develop it to a higher tier. Together with
Mads Emil Oxholm Iversen, Marcus Nystrup, and Adam Fink Clemensen, three other De­
sign & Innovation master students from DTU began the special course in August of 2024
and came to an end in December 2024. Together they were responsible for these key
activities:
• Designing and conceptualising solution proposals for wall mounted equipment fitted
to the flexible Unistrut channels.
• Finalising the interior and exterior design of the mobile makerspace.
• Took the first steps towards better branding and renamed Innovation Container,
”Gnist”.
• Developing the visual identity and initial branding elements of Gnist.
• Developing initial business model
2.1.3
Master's Thesis: Transforming STEM Education - Designing School
Curricula for Modular Makerspaces
The Gnist Curriculum Design Guide is a comprehensive and structured tool designed to
support teachers in creating curricula for technology integrated learning experiences de­
veloped by Buch (2025). The guide was developed in response to a critical gap in Danish
elementary education, where teachers are often left without concrete and practical frame­
works for implementing maker­based learning. The guide consolidated pedagogical, le­
gal, and technological considerations into a user friendly step­by­step format. It includes
detailed templates, compliance checklists, and instructional strategies that enable teach­
ers to design engaging activities while maintaining alignment with curricular goals and
legal requirements. The guide serves not only as a curriculum development tool but also
as a means of professional empowerment. It bridges the gap between innovative edu­
cational ideals and classroom realities by providing teachers with a reliable framework to
integrate makerspaces and Technology Comprehension into their teaching practice. As
such, it contributes to both pedagogical innovation and systemic educational coherence.
2.1.4
Master's Thesis: Modelling the Digital Value Chain for an Educational
Makerspace
The master thesis by Lobón Trujillo (2025), focused on integrating and developing a vir­
tual network of a makerspace, intended for use in a school setting, to enhance resource
sharing, student engagement, and interdisciplinary collaboration. The project identifies
that virtual makerspaces improve access to shared resources, encourage peer­to­peer
learning, and support the development of STEM related skills. However, the study also
identifies challenges regarding digital accessibility and technical difficulties. Overall, the
study finds that there is a potential for a virtual makerspace to be a scalable and innovative
way for introducing and integrating digital fabrication to schools.
6
==PAGE:20==
2.1.5
Bachelor's Thesis: Makerspaces for Children - A User Centric Design
Approach for Gnist
The bachelor project by Dau et al. (2025) investigated how onboarding of teachers can be
structured to better support the adoption of the Gnist Makerspace. The project identifies
critical tension between Gnist’s pedagogical ambition and the practical realities faced by
teachers unfamiliar with makerspaces or digital tools. In the project, a modular onboarding
concept have been developed that emphasises experience­based collaborative learning.
The proposed template, guides teachers through scaffolded considerations relevant for
developing curriculum as well as configuring the container with accompanying equipment.
The projects contributes valuable insight into how physical and pedagogical flexibility can
lower the threshold for teacher adoption. It positions the Gnist container not as a fixed
solution, but as a dynamic toolset that must be meaningfully interpreted and localised by
teachers themselves.
2.1.6
Special Course: Designing, Prototyping, Test Procedure, and Testing
of a Transport Optimised Test Rig for Vibration and Shock Isolation
The special course project by Wiemann (2025) focused on the design and testing of a
transport­optimised test­rig for securing and protecting the equipment in the makerspace
during transport. The aim was to ensure that sensitive technical equipment could be
moved reliably within the container, when transported to a school without sustaining me­
chanical damage or requiring extensive recalibration. Using ISTA­standard vibration test­
ing at the Danish Technological Institute, the project evaluated how different configura­
tions of shelving, dampening materials, and weight distributions impacted the mechanical
stresses experienced by mounted 3D printers. Key findings revealed the important of
vibration isolation dampeners, shelf stiffness, and secure filament storage in mitigating
transportation induced wear and calibration drift. The project resulted in a series of con­
crete design recommendations, including structural reinforcements and dampener place­
ment. There outcomes contributes to Gnist’s broader goal of scalable, high­quality deploy­
ment of mobile makerspaces by improving the robustness and reliability during transport.
2.1.7
Special Course: Enhancing the User’s Sense of Belonging in Physical
Makerspaces
This special course project conducted by Nielsen (2025), investigated how to enhance
students’ sense of belonging in makerspaces through the lens of universal design. The
study addresses the often overlooked challenges faced by students with disabilities and
proposes actionable recommendation for creating inclusive and accessible learning envi­
ronments in makerspaces. The project presents a design framework based in three core
elements: accessibility, legitimacy, and confidence. These principles are operationalised
through targeted design strategies spanning four key domains: spatial design, teaching
practice, community building, and teacher mindset. By integrating Universal Design prin­
ciples with human­centred design methodologies, the project contributes valuable insight
into how makerspaces can become more equitable and welcoming environments for all
students.
2.1.8
Special Course: Minimisation of Adoption Friction
In this special course, O. Rasmussen and Rewers (2025) have investigated how to min­
imise adoption friction, when integrating makerspaces at schools. The course have been
tightly connected with the experiences from the centralised makerspace BUFX building
on their experiences as well as analysing platforms like MakersEmpire as a benchmark.
7
==PAGE:21==
Recognising that time, confidence, and perceived complexity are significant barriers to in­
tegrating makerspace pedagogy, the project focused on creating a digital resource bank
to support both teachers and students. The platform prioritise plug­and­play usability,
featuring short instructional videos and sequenced learning activities. Key adoption en­
ablers have been identified, such as gamification elements, onboarding modules, and
teacher support tools including co­teaching strategies and ambassador roles. By com­
bining UX principles with educational insights, the project contributes a practical and scal­
able concept for strengthening the Gnist ecosystem. The platform empowers teachers not
only with resources, but with the confidence and clarity needed to facilitate meaningful,
technology­integrated learning experiences.
2.2
Makerspaces and the Maker Movement
Makerspaces have emerged all around the world as innovative learning environments
designed to support creativity, problem solving, and hands­on engagement with materi­
als, tools, and ideas. Makerspaces are originally rooted in the do­it­yourself culture and
the global maker movement has gained momentum over the past two decades (Demi­
rata & Sadik, 2023; Hughes et al., 2022). At their core, makerspaces are collaborative
environments where learners of all ages can explore, experiment, and create using a va­
riety of digital and non­digital tools. These might include 3D printers, laser cutters, robotic
kits, coding platforms, or recording studios. Regardless of the tools, the activities re­
volve around the design process, iterations, and problem solving, typically tied to STEM
education (Demirata & Sadik, 2023; Hughes et al., 2022). This format allows students
to develop skills in areas ranging from electronics and programming to design thinking
and artistic expression (Demirata & Sadik, 2023). Importantly, the maker process shifts
students from passive recipients of information to active creators (Harron et al., 2022).
Makerspaces are not a one­size­fits­all model and can vary widely in both form and func­
tion depending on the context. In the United States, makerspaces appeared as “Learning
Labs” in public libraries, not only as an open source but as a place where people would
engage in activities.
At institutions like MIT more technically advanced makerspaces
emerged as “Fabrication Laboratories” or “FabLabs”, providing an environment for pro­
totyping and fabrication. In Europe, makerspaces were likely to be part of schools to
promote experimental and interdisciplinary learning (Demirata & Sadik, 2023). The role
of makerspaces in formal education has gained increasing attention due to their potential
to practice innovation and entrepreneurship freely which most formal education do not
offer (Demirata & Sadik, 2023; Hughes et al., 2022).
2.2.1
Constructivism vs. Constructionism
The pedagogical foundations of makerspaces and the maker mindset are deeply rooted in
the theories of constructivism and constructionism, both of which emphasise the learner’s
active role in building knowledge through experience (Hughes et al., 2022). Although often
used interchangeably, these two theories have distinct emphases, particularly in how they
inform educational design and learning environments such as makerspaces.
Constructivism, as originally developed by Jean Piaget, views knowledge as something
that individuals construct through interaction with the world. According to this perspective,
students are not passive recipients of information but rather interpret, transform, and adapt
new inputs in light of their prior knowledge and cognitive structures. Learning is a pro­
cess of internal organisation and assimilation, where conceptual change is gradual and
often resistant to external correction (Ackermann, 2001). Piaget’s theory is particularly
8
==PAGE:22==
valuable for understanding the developmental stages of children and the mechanisms by
which they make sense of their environment. However, constructivism alone does not
fully account for the influence of context, tools, media, and social interaction on learning.
As Ackermann (2001) notes, it tends to overlook individual learning preferences and how
meaning is shaped by external environments.
This is where constructionism, introduced by Seymour Papert, expands the construc­
tivist model. While sharing constructivism’s view of learning as the internal construction
of knowledge, constructionism adds a critical dimension; learning is especially powerful
when it involves building artifacts such as a physical model, computer program, or digi­
tal design (Ackermann, 2001). These artefacts are not only learning outcomes but also
reflective tools that help students externalise, iterate on, and communicate their thinking.
Constructionism emphasises student agency, creativity, and personal ownership (Kyni­
gos, 2015).
Papert’s approach also challenges conventional views that concrete thinking is inferior to
abstract reasoning. He argued that exposure to hands­on expressive tasks provides the
foundation for developing more abstract forms of understanding (Kynigos, 2015). Learn­
ing in the constructionist view, is not just a private, internal event but a social and expres­
sive activity, influences by the environment and the meanings that negotiated with others
(Kynigos, 2015). This view makes constructionism especially relevant for makerspaces,
which embody these characteristics by providing learners with a space to tinker, design,
and create with both digital and non­digital materials (Hughes et al., 2022). As students
design and revise physical or digital artifacts, they engage in a reflective process that
aligns with Papert’s vision of “diving into problems”, rather than abstractly reasoning from
a distance (Ackermann, 2001). This form of learning empowers students to take owner­
ship of their knowledge and develop deeper conceptual understanding through hands­on
inquiry and personal relevance.
In summary, while Piaget’s constructivism helps explain the cognitive mechanisms of
learning, Papert’s constructionism provides a model for designing learning environments,
like makerspaces, that foster agency, reflection, and contextual learning. Both perspec­
tives are complementary and together offer a rich theoretical foundation for understanding
how makerspaces can transform education from passive knowledge transfer into an ac­
tive, student­centred process of exploration and creation.
2.2.2
Access to Makerspaces for Danish Schools
In Denmark, the maker movement have also made its impact.
In a survey made by
Buus (2021) on behalf of the Villum Foundation, access to makerspaces for schools was
mapped. 85% of the Danish schools participated in the survey making it valid to gener­
alise for the entire country. A distinction between three different types of makerspaces
was made:
1. School­based makerspaces are placed on the school and the primary users are the
schools own students and teachers.
2. Centralised makerspaces are driven by the municipality and the users are all the
schools in the municipality.
3. Mobile makerspaces that the schools can book for the school for a limited time
period.
Overall, 65% of all schools in Denmark have indicated they have access to at least one
9
==PAGE:23==
type makerspace. This means that about a third of all schools in Denmark do not currently
have access to any type of makerspace.
2.3
Teaching in Makerspaces
Makerspaces offer rich opportunities for interdisciplinary, hands­on learning, but they also
introduce new demands on teachers’ pedagogical approaches, subject integration, and
classroom management. Understanding how teachers can facilitate meaningful learn­
ing in makerspaces requires attention to both theoretical and curricular perspectives. To
understand the educational potential of makerspaces and how they intersect with formal
schooling, it is essential to explore the broader frameworks that inform teaching in these
environments. To situate this within the context of schools, this section explores how
makerspaces can promote STEM education, the development of 21st century skills, and
subject­specific goals like Technology Comprehension in elementary education. These
perspectives provide insight into the broader educational relevance of makerspaces and
the evolving role of the teacher within them.
2.3.1
STEM Education and 21st Century Skills
Makerspaces align closely with current education goals emphasising 21st century skills
and STEM competencies. These frameworks reflect a shift in education from traditional
content­focused instruction toward deeper learning approaches, where students are ex­
pected to engage in critical thinking, collaboration, creativity, and communication, all skills
considered essential for navigating complex societal and technological challenges (Bink­
ley et al., 2012; Hughes et al., 2022).
21st century skills can be categorised in four broad domains: Ways of thinking, ways of
working, tools for working, and living in the world (Binkley et al., 2012). Makerspaces
provide authentic contexts in which students can develop and enact these skills through
hands­on, project­based learning, enabling them to iterate on ideas and solve open­ended
problems (Demirata & Sadik, 2023). STEM education similarly encourages interdisci­
plinary learning by integrating traditional subjects into real­world problems and inquiry­
based approaches. Makerspaces naturally offer a foundation for enacting STEM prin­
ciples in practice, as they invite students to explore engineering challenges, experiment
with digital technologies, and apply mathematical reasoning in hands­on projects (Breiner
et al., 2012). In this process, learning becomes both contextualised and student­driven,
enabling students to construct meaningful connections across subject domains (Harron
et al., 2022).
Rather than treating science, technology, engineering, and mathematics as separate sub­
jects, STEM in makerspaces encourage students to see the connections between con­
cepts, as they build, test, and revise their creations. This integration not only support
technical skill development, but also nurtures the flexible system­level thinking necessary
in contemporary education and employment landscapes (Breiner et al., 2012). These
competencies are not only instrumental for students’ future careers but are also increas­
ingly reflected in national and international educational goals. As schools seek to prepare
students for uncertain futures shaped by automation and rapid innovation, the integration
of 21st century skills and STEM in education becomes a pedagogical necessity.
10
==PAGE:24==
2.3.2
Technology Comprehension as a New Subject in Danish Elementary
Schools
Society has undergone extensive digitalisation in recent decades. Consequently, all stu­
dents in the Danish Elementary school must learn about how to engage critically and con­
structively with technology. To support this objective, a new subject, Technology Compre­
hension, will be integrated in existing subjects and become mandatory from 2027 (Børne­
og Undervisningsministeiret, 2025). A three year pilot test was conducted across selected
schools from 2017 to 2020 to test the subject both as a stand­alone elective course and as
an integrated competence area in existing subjects such as Danish, Mathematics, Arts,
and Science (EMU, 2022c; Wagner, 2021).
The introduction of Technology Comprehension in Danish schools is driven by the need
to equip students with critical, creative, and practical digital skills. The subject aims to
enable students not only to use technology but also to understand, analyse and shape
it. The subject is built around four core areas: Digital empowerment, Digital design and
design processes, Computational thinking, and Technological knowledge and skills (EMU,
2022a, 2022c).
A midterm evaluation of the pilot test indicated that students across grade levels devel­
oped an improved understanding of digital technologies (EMU, 2022b). However, the pilot
also revealed challenges, particularly a lack of teacher preparation and time to explore the
subject’s goals and methods, making it difficult to translate the academic field into effective
classroom teaching (EMU, 2022b, 2022d; Wagner, 2021). In the final evaluation report
of the pilot test, teachers have emphasised, that having fabrication technologies avail­
able at schools, combined with the possibility to seek inspiration in maker communities
have driven the evolution of their practice (Wagner, 2021). This indicates, that integrating
makerspaces at schools is a valuable addition to teaching Technology Comprehension.
Based on the findings from the pilot study, the next phase of Technology Comprehension
involves strengthening teacher competencies through targeted training programs, col­
laboration across institutes, and the creation of local and national professional networks
(EMU, 2022d; Wagner, 2021). The long­term plan is to implement Technology Compre­
hension as a mandatory subject in the national curriculum, ensuring that all students are
prepared to understand, design, and critically engage with digital technologies that shape
modern society (EMU, 2022a).
2.3.3
National Common Objectives (Fælles Mål)
In the Danish public school system, National Common Objectives (Fælles Mål) serve as a
central tool for defining what students are expected to learn across subjects. The goal is
to support a more learning­oriented approach, where both students and teachers benefit
from clear learning targets. Research shows that students who understand what they
are expected to learn are better positioned to do so, and that teachers who set explicit
learning goals are more effective in assessing and supporting student learning (Højgaard
et al., 2012). Since 2003, these objectives have included binding goals and progression
targets for all school subjects.
In 2014, goal and result oriented measures became a central element in elementary
schools, and a new set of common objectives were made (Mikkelsen, 2020). However,
this can have unintentional consequences, as it can shift the focus to easily measurable
results rather than sustaining a holistic view on learning outcomes.
Although the common objectives are mandatory, teachers tend not to use them when
11
==PAGE:25==
planning lessons or curriculum (Højgaard et al., 2012). In the planning, teachers focus
more on what the students should do rather that what they should learn. This was also
the case in the pilot study with Technology Comprehension. As a part of the pilot test, a
set of common objectives were developed as an addition to the existing subjects (EMU,
2025). However, these were not defining for the teachers practice, and many in the pilot
test reported not having the capacity to engage concretely with the objectives (Wagner,
2021).
12
==PAGE:26==
3
Research Methodology
This chapter presents the methodological foundation of the thesis, detailing the research
philosophy, structure, and design methods applied to explore and develop solutions for
the Gnist Education concept.
3.1
Research Structure
To make sure that our research will contribute to the wider design research field, our ap­
proach will be to use the Design Research Methodology (DRM) by Blessing and Chakrabarti
(2009) which provides an iterative, structured framework that remains open to context
and responsive to findings as they emerge. Our work and report will follow the four main
phases of the DRM which is: Research Clarification (RC), Descriptive Study I (DS­I),
Prescriptive Study (PS) and Descriptive Study II (DS­II).
• Research Clarification: The Prescriptive Study aims to describe the status quo
through a situation analysis and explores the discourses and contextual factors of
the situation. It serves as the preliminary requisite for the situational sense­making
and common ground, before caring out focused and prioritised research in Descrip­
tive Study I. It will end by formulating certain research question that will guide the
rest of the project.
• Descriptive Study I: The Descriptive Study I aims to further explore the assump­
tions revealed in Research Clarification. This is done through an analysis of litera­
ture and empirical studies, to understand the underlying problem and how to combat
them. This will provide a basis for Prescriptive Study with a design specification to
ensure an effective development phase.
• Prescriptive Study: The Prescriptive Study aims to use the gathered knowledge
to develop solutions to enhance the user experience and usability and implement
them for the first edition of the makerspace. The result should be a MVP based on
the design specification.
• Descriptive Study II: The Descriptive Study II aims to test, evaluate and validate
the solutions to gain an understanding of the impact it can deliver and to see if it
contributes as a success. Lastly some suggestions for further improvement can be
made to fuel future work.
3.1.1
Research Type
The overall research type in this project is Type 5 (Blessing & Chakrabarti, 2009). Com­
mon for all research types is the review­based research clarification, where the goal and
assumptions are clarified. In type 5 research projects, a comprehensive Descriptive Study
I is made to develop an understanding of the situation. A comprehensive Prescriptive
Study is made to develop a support tool based on the understanding. This is followed
by an initial Descriptive Study II to analyse the impact on the situation. Due to time con­
straints of the thesis, the research can not move into a type 7, where improvements of
the support tool is implemented and tested again. This will only be described in the future
work section. There will be iterative work between DS­I and PS as new understandings
and findings will come to light during the development of the support tools. An illustration
of the research process can be seen in Figure 3.1.
13
==PAGE:27==
Figure 3.1: Illustration of research process.
3.1.2
Time Allocation
This thesis project will span over a 25­week period, as seen in Figure 3.2. These 25 weeks
will start with a Mini­Project, but mainly focus on the four phases of DRM. Additional time
has also ben allocated for the documentation throughout the project.
Figure 3.2: Gantt chart showing how the time structure of the thesis
3.2
Design Methods
To address the complexity of designing, implementing, and evaluating an educational
makerspace concept, this thesis applies a range of design methods tailored to the differ­
ent phases of the research. These methods support both analytical understanding and
creative development across physical, curricular, and organisational dimensions. The fol­
lowing sections outline each of these methods, their rationale, and how they were applied
throughout the research process.
3.2.1
Situational Mapping - Development Arena and Actor Worlds
U. Jørgensen and Sørensen (1999) transcends the academic scholarly boundaries when
they presented the notion of the ’development arena’ in 1999 ­ bridging the gaps between
sociology, engineering, economics and management. They explain, how the develop­
ment arena should be seen as a metaphor, indicating that it does not depict any real
world phenomena to full extend, but instead is able render a very meaningful represen­
tation of a situated technology development process and its translational potential, when
introduced to the market. U. Jørgensen and Sørensen (1999) elaborate on the metaphor­
14
==PAGE:28==
ical notion by juxtaposition the development arena to a ”kind of ’laboratory’ for developing
technologies”, and further denotes, ”the laboratory is seen as a space for reducing and
ordering processes that are too complex to be understood in ’real life’” (U. Jørgensen &
Sørensen, 1999).
When configuring the development arena for a project, it has formerly been instructed to
fist configure and establish ’actor worlds’ (U. Jørgensen & Sørensen, 1999). Actor Worlds
are the main populating entitities of any development arena. They are not a 1:1 depiction
of the real world situation but merely an analytical tool framework, that aids simplifying
the vast complexity of any given social relationship between one or more actors, group
of actors, smaller communities or larger organisations. Furthermore, it is not only the
human­actors that makes the transitions from the situational mapping, so do many of the
non­human actors as well. Constituting an actor world follows a simple set of principles.
When Clarke (2005) codifies her situational maps from the situational mapping exercise
into, what she refers to as ’social worlds’, she defines some practical guidelines ensuring,
that each Social World caries itself without ambiguity to another social world, and would
in fact be the same social world ­ or more accurately framed; the human­actors in two
or more social worlds cannot share the same ’common concern’, ’commitment to action’
and ’shared objects’. These three conceptual concepts demarcates one social world from
another social world, and while social worlds can inherit one or two of the concepts from
another social world, it cannot inherit all three, as a result would not exist any argument
for why the actors are not part of the same social world(Clarke, 2005).
The social worlds framework of Clarke (2005) is used to describe and define the actor
worlds on the development arena, and are formed on the basis of the Situational Mapping
method in the same framework of the socioal worlds (Clarke, 2005). The method consists
of three maps, namely the messy­ , ordered ­ and relational map, that are used to analyse
the situation.
3.2.2
Combined Literature and Desktop Research Approach
In this project both a systematic literature review (SLR) and desktop research have been
used to strengthen the overall understanding based on academic and real­world context.
The SLR will be conducted in accordance with the Preferred Reporting Items for Sys­
tematic Review and Meta­Analysis (PRISMA) guideline (Page et al., 2021). This ensures
that relevant academic literature will be captured, through a structured and transparent
approach. To complement the SLR, desktop research were conducted, to fill in gaps and
provide a broader background of knowledge. This ensures that a comprehensive review
can be done on the chosen topic.
3.2.3
Interviews and Observations
Both interviews and observations are commonly used methods for collecting empirical
data. Even though the each method have different approaches, the purpose of both is to
obtain a deep understanding of the case being studied, mainly in terms of how individuals
act and think (Cacciattolo, 2015). In this thesis, interviews and observations have been
used to gain insights into teaching practice, students engagement with activities.
One of the main advantages of interviews is the two­way communication. The interviewee
can ask questions to the interviewer if further explanation is needed (Cacciattolo, 2015).
Further, it is possible to provide information on the reasoning behind the interviewees’
responses, which can benefit the quantitative side of a research study. However, this two­
way communication can introduce bias and thereby affect the outcome of the interview.
15
==PAGE:29==
To guide the interview several structures can be used. In this thesis, the semi­structured
interviews have been chosen as it provides a clear guide on which topics to cover while
still allowing freedom to follow the interviewees responses (George, 2023).
In contrast, the main advantage of observations is its usefulness when a study involves
behaviours of individuals, especially how and why certain actions are made (Cacciattolo,
2015). The observation method can either be participatory or non­participatory depending
on the role of the observer in the situation, either as passive “fly on the wall” or an active
part of facilitating activities. However, in both cases, expectations for the observations can
influence what is noted, which can lead to biased interpretations. Further, simply having
the observer present can affect natural behaviour. To make more objective observations,
a specific focus can be made, e.g. noting how many times students ask questions during
instructions.
Notably, both observations and interviews are essential informative qualitative methods.
However, the specific situation and the individuality of participants obstruct generalisability
of the data.
3.2.4
Rich Pictures - A Soft Systems Methodology visualisation method
The Soft Systems Methodology (SSM) by Checkland and Poulter (2010) is an action
based research methodology springing from more classical systems design methodolo­
gies. SSM takes note of ’the flux everyday life’ consisting of events and ideas, that over
time yields ’A perceived problematical Situation’. Checkland and Poulter (2010) makes a
clear point, that it is not the situation itself, that is a problem, referencing the term ’prob­
lem situation’, as the situation does not deal a well defined problem to be solved by itself
(Checkland & Poulter, 2010). Instead the situation can be percieved from many angles
by a various set of actors, and some will find it problematic, and some will not. SSM
bases itself on a list of itereatives cycles, that over time intervenes in system, when a new
problem arises. It does so, by following a relatively strict, however flexible enough to be
interpreted, and used to see fit.
The ‘Rich Picture’ is an embedded method within the entire methodology, however many
have used the Rich Picture as an individual element to outline a ‘perceived problematical
situation’, without moving forward and completing the entire method, as it is described
(Barbrook­Johnson & Penn, n.d.). Barbrook­Johnson and Penn (n.d.) describes the Rich
Pictures as a stand­alone systems methodology leaning towards the qualitative side of the
system methods, rather than the quantiative. A Rich Picture is described as a drawing,
or a picture of a system or a situation. They can look very different from one another, but
are usually drawn up in groups, however this is modified slightly to fit into the context of
the research based on interviews and observations.
3.2.5
Build-Measure-Learn
The Build­Measure­Learn cycle was originally created by Ries (2011), to assist startups
through a lean product development cycle, that help to test ideas, gather data, and create
products based on informed decisions. It generally follows three overall phases:
• Build: This focuses on the creation of a Minimum Viable Product (MVP) that allows
the product to quickly be tested and evaluated based on user feedback.
• Measure: This are used to gather information and insights from multiple experi­
ments and user feedback to describe how well the MVP is performing.
• Learn: The test result and insights gained are analysed to determine which actions
16
==PAGE:30==
should be taken for the development.
The Build­Measure­Learn cycle is well suited as an overall as an overall structural frame­
work for designing the Gnist Education concept based on real user feedback. It will mainly
be used when the solutions are being developed in the prescriptive phase of this project.
3.2.6
Iterative Prototyping - UX Design process
In order to create a successful MVP, multiple prototype iteration are typically needed.
The prototype development in this project is overall based on a user­centered design
framework, by Hartson and Pyla (2019), called The UX lifecycle template (The Wheel),
as seen in Figure 3.3. This framework allowes for multiple iterations, each of different
levels of fidelity, to be developed and evaluated in the Prescriptive Study phase.
Figure 3.3: The UX lifecycle template (The wheel), visualising the UX design process (Hartson &
Pyla, 2019).
Each iteration, will be developed on the basis of the four lifecycle activities, understand
needs, design solutions, prototype candidates, and evaluation of the prototype. Further­
more, as the overall MVP will be measured in a real use context, which means that each
iteration will be evaluated with UX evaluation data methods, such as observations and
user tests. This ensures that each iteration will reflect the end goal of producing a suc­
cessful MVP, that is designed based on user needs.
3.2.7
Co-Creation
Co­creation is a method for collective creativity, to gather insights from the end users as
an integrated part of the design and development process. Co­creation practised at the
early stages of a design development process can have a positive impact on the solution
(Sanders & Stappers, 2008). Both at the point of idea generation and decision making, co­
creation can provide valuable insights into the users needs and thereby increase the users
acceptance of the final solution. Through this method, users are not passive recipients
but active contributors to innovation.
17
==PAGE:31==
Compared to classical design approaches, co­creation changes what is designed, how
it is developed, and who is involved in the process. The focus is shifted from a prod­
uct perspective to a purpose perspective. Here, both the researcher, designer, and user
is a part of the development process, each contributing with their expertise and experi­
ences (Sanders & Stappers, 2008). Co­creation is especially valuable for solving com­
plex societal or institutional challenges, where the outcomes cannot be predefined, and
where success depends on stakeholders feeling of ownership and contextual understand­
ing (Lystbæk & Harbo, 2018).
To support the user in this process, the researcher must take on a facilitating role by
leading and guiding the process in ways that are suitable for their ability to participate
(Sanders & Stappers, 2008). The process must be designed with careful attention to
participants capabilities. This includes using tools and formats that are appropriate to
the context and ensuring that all voices are heard. When done well, co­creation not only
enriches the design process but also builds mutual learning, trust, and commitment among
participants (Lystbæk & Harbo, 2018).
18
==PAGE:32==
4
Research Clarification
The Research Clarification phase constitutes the first step in Design Research Method­
ology and serves to establish a clear and well­founded basis for the remainder of the
study. This phase focuses on identifying and understanding the broader problem context
by mapping assumptions related to the scope. This will be done through a thorough situ­
ational analysis to find areas of relevance, that need further exploration in the Descriptive
Study I.
One of the key outcomes of RC is the formulation of Research Questions that precisely
articulate what is to be investigated and why it is relevant. These questions go on to
guide the subsequent stages of the design process. In this way, RC functions as a bridge
between a complex real­world context and a structured systematic research process.
All the assumptions will be condensed in initial development arenas for the current situa­
tion (As­Is) and the intended impact (To­Be). These models will throughout the report be
updated as more insights is gained on the problem and solutions.
Overview of Methods and Activities
In this phase, the following methods will be used to map the assumptions for the scope:
• Situational mapping
• Systems Engineering levels
• Actor worlds
• Development arena
19
==PAGE:33==
4.1
Approach of methods
To clarify the research direction and identify key assumptions, this project adopts a struc­
tured methodological approach based on Situational Analysis (Clarke, 2005) and inspired
by the Development Arena framework (U. Jørgensen & Sørensen, 1999). The chosen
approach consists of three interrelated methods: situational mapping, actor worlds, and
development arenas. Together, these methods enable a systematic unpacking of the
complex and heterogeneous makerspace landscape in Danish schools by revealing con­
troversies, dependencies, and strategic opportunities.
All three methods are used across the artefact, complex, and engineering system levels
to capture the multifaceted nature of makerspaces in educational contexts. This system­
based structure ensures that any proposed interventions will be context­sensitive and
applicable in real­world implementations.
4.1.1
Situational Mapping
Situational mapping have been used to identify all relevant actors, both human and non­
human, in the current makerspace situation. This method visualise the full complexity of
the system that shape the context. An initial workshop was conducted based on assump­
tions. During this workshop, a messy map was created that with all actors, see appendix
RC1.2, which then was ordered into categories such as objects, organisation, discourse,
implicated actors, infrastructure, etc (appendix RC1.3). After the workshop, additional re­
lation maps were created to identify how each actor is related to each other, see appendix
DS1.4 and RC2­4. These maps enables a grounded understanding of what constitutes
the situation and serves as a foundation for further analysis of the controversies and ne­
gotiations, to identify areas for improvement.
4.1.2
Actor Worlds
Building on the situational map, the identified human actors were grouped into actor worlds
based on common concerns, commitment to action, and shared objects, see appendix
RC5­7. This step helps to understand the plurality of perspectives present in the situation
and how different actors may be motivated by diverging interests. Actor worlds support
a more nuanced interpretation of stakeholder positions by highlighting not just who is
involved, but how and why. This is particularly useful when aiming to navigate tensions
or align goals across stakeholders in later phases of the design process.
4.1.3
Development Arenas
The insights from the actor worlds were synthesised into a development arena that vi­
sualises how these worlds interact and negotiate within the makerspace context. A key
advantage of this step is its ability to render complex, multi­actor, multi­level developments
visible, across both the current (As­Is, see appendix J­DA1) and desired (To­Be, see ap­
pendix J­DA2) situations. Throughout the thesis, these arenas will be updated based on
the gained knowledge and narrowing of the scope. These will be presented at the end of
each chapter. All arenas can be found in appendix J in full size.
By analysing the arena through the lens of system levels, the arena becomes a space for
exploring how national policies, funding structures, pedagogical ambitions, and physical
conditions co­evolve. It also serves as a reference model for identifying design spaces for
Gnist’s future interventions, such as business model opportunities or policy recommen­
dations.
20
==PAGE:34==
This methodical progression, from situational mapping to actor worlds to development
arenas, enables a grounded, multilevel understanding of the educational makerspace
landscape. It ensures that research efforts are not only theoretically informed but also
practically relevant, helping bridge gaps between policy intentions, educational practices,
and technical implementation.
4.2
Status Quo
The current situation across Danish schools regarding makerspaces is highly heteroge­
neous. Schools differ widely in terms if access to physical spaces, equipment, technical
competencies, and institutional support, as described in subsection 2.2.2 (Buus, 2021).
This is particularly evident when comparing public and independent schools, as the mu­
nicipal funded centralised makerspaces only serves public schools.
A central issue is that accessibility, infrastructure, and pedagogical integration vary signif­
icantly, depending on local funding structures, teacher readiness, and school leadership.
While some schools have well­equipped makerspaces with dedicated staff, others rely on
ad hoc solutions, and many have no makerspace access at all. These conditions create
inconsistency in the opportunity to implement Technology Comprehension as a part if the
curriculum, which by 2027 will become a mandatory subject in Danish schools (Børne­ og
Undervisningsministeiret, 2025).
4.3
Outcome of the Situational Mapping
The situational mapping workshop was initially made for the full situation, to identify all
actors in the situation based on assumptions and prior knowledge, see appendix RC1.
However, when mapping the relation between actors, the full complexity of the situation
became evident. Figure 4.1 illustrates the relations based on 5 selected actors. If this had
been continued for all key actors, the relation map would not be possible to comprehend.
Figure 4.1: The relation based on 5 selected key stakeholders: Students, Teacher, Central mak­
erspace, municipality, Public schools (appendix RC1.4)
It is assumed, that independent schools rarely has access to centralised makerspaces that
21
==PAGE:35==
is directly oriented towards schools. Other public makerspaces situated in e.g. libraries
will arguably grant access to independent schools. However, as these are not specifically
school­oriented, they will be left out of the situational context. To generalise the situation,
there are two main contextual factors that can frame the full situation:
• Does the school have access to a makerspace?
• Is the school public or independent?
The public schools are funded through the municipalities. This means, that the schools
can not spend the resources as they please, but have to comply with the requirements
from the municipalities, including which online curriculum they can use etc. In the inde­
pendent schools, the money comes primarily from the parents, meaning a school board
consisting mostly of parents have significant influence on what the money can be spend
on. In regards to the school based makerspaces, this means that either the municipality
or the parent board needs to approve spending money on the equipment and the inte­
gration at the schools. The centralised makerspaces are funded through the municipality,
meaning it is only available for the public schools. Because of these major differences
between the situations, the relation maps in the situational analysis have been split into 5
scenarios:
1. Public schools with access to school­based makerspace.
2. Public schools with access to centralised makerspace.
3. Public schools without access to makerspaces.
4. Independent schools with access to school­based makerspace.
5. Independent schools without access to makerspaces.
Even with this distinction the situations are still very complex. The goal of the situational
analysis and relation mapping is to get an overview of the assumptions we have that are
critical to investigate in order to develop a solution that are suited to be implemented at the
schools. Depending on the focus of the analysis, e.g. viewing the situation from different
stakeholders perspective, different factors seems to be the most important. During the
initial relation map, especially the physical aspect of the makerspace, the teachers who
has to integrate it in their practice, and the schools who have to invest in the access seems
to be the three key focus areas. These three focus areas are highly depended on each
other, meaning, in order to make a desirable makerspace solution for Gnist Education, all
three areas needs to be considered. In this thesis, all three areas will continuously be of
importance and distributed between the team members, during research, development,
and testing to ensure a full package solution for the mobile makerspace that are suitable
for the reality of teaching practice. By dividing into focus areas, it is possible to ensure a
narrow scope for each, while still developing a holistic solution ready for implementation.
In the process of making the relation maps, all five scenarios have been mapped based
on the three focus areas. Further, to gain the holistic overview, each scenario will also be
considered on the artefact, complex, and engineering level. Having only one focus area in
mind while making the analysis will help identify the key factors in terms of controversies,
negotiations, and obligatory passage points in each situation, map the differences and
similarities between the situations, and identify assumptions to explored further in the
Descriptive Study I. Figure 4.2 illustrate how the situational analysis have been divided,
where each scenario and aspect also includes all three system levels. This will ensure a
22
==PAGE:36==
systematic approach to ensure the a more seamless integration of the proposed solution
into the already existing systems at the schools.
Figure 4.2: Overview of the 5 scenarios and 3 focus areas, each with covering the 3 system
levels.
4.3.1
Situaational mapping related to the Physical Makerspace
This aspect, focuses on what is required to align the makerspace with teacher and student
needs. 20 relation maps were created to cover the physical aspect of the makerspace
situation on a artefact, complex, and engineering system level (see appendix RC3.1).
Based on these maps, multiple focus areas were identified based on assumptions, to state
controversies, negotiations, and obligatory passage points related to the makerspace (see
appendix RC3.2).
The assumptions surrounding school­based and centralised makerspaces on an artefact
level, are based on how the physical tools and equipment are managed, maintained, and
matched with user needs. It can be assumed that the schools is responsible for its own
infrastructure regarding school­based makerspaces. Equipment procurement, mainte­
nance, and classroom integration are therefore handled locally. This means, that teach­
ers should possess both technical and pedagogical competencies in order to manage
and facilitate a broad and diverse range of makerspace tools effectively. Furthermore, it
is up to the individual school or teacher to align the equipment use with varying teach­
ing preferences and student interest. This can lead to inconsistency in the available and
functional equipment, as space and budget constrains can have limits on procuring new
tools, and lack of maintenance limits the quality and functionality of available equipment.
The school­based makerspaces can overall, be assumed to offer immediate accessibil­
ity to students, but might not be able to deliver sufficient opportunities, due to resource
limitations that impact functionality and user experience.
In contrast, it can be assumed that centralised makerspaces combines learning oppor­
tunities to a dedicated space that manages these responsibilities on behalf of multiple
schools. This can allow for greater efficiency and quality for a broader selection of mak­
erspace tools, as equipment maintenance and procurement is carried out by trained staff.
A larger budget and dedicated space could likewise offer more diverse learning opportu­
23
==PAGE:37==
nities to better fit teacher and student interests. However, centralised makerspaces could
also experience certain trade­offs regarding accessibility, particularly for students dealing
with physical, cognitive or mental impairments, who may be challenged in travelling to
these makerspaces.
General for both the school­based and centralised makerspace, is that the effectiveness
and usability is tied to the overall flow. This flow can be influenced by slow or faulty mak­
erspace equipment which can create bottlenecks in the learning activities. Particularly 3D
printing might be a critical point for bottlenecks to happen, as the technology is notoriously
slow and can experience a lot of technical issues. 3D printing remains a popular piece of
equipment which might result in scheduling and demand challenges, that can lead to a
reduced user experience.
Overall, the artefact system reveal a tension between local accessibility and centralised
efficiency. Centralised makerspace can be assumed to offer more reliable and functional
tools that support varied teaching opportunities. However, they risk becoming discon­
nected form the day­to­day educational needs that individual schools might have. School­
based makerspaces on the other hand are more accessible and offer immediate learning
opportunities, but is limited with space, budget and technical upkeep.
Controversies from the artefact level:
• Teachers in school­based makerspaces are expected to manage both tech­
nical equipment and classroom integration, often without sufficient support or
training.
• Challenges regarding equipment availability, maintenance, and functionality
can hinder learning.
• Lack of technical and pedagogical competencies might result in ineffective use
of makerspace tools.
• High demand on equipment like 3D printers can create bottlenecks, that neg­
atively impact flow and user experience.
• Students with physical, cognitive, or mental impairments can experience ac­
cessibility challenges in centralised makerspaces.
Negotiations from the artefact level:
• In centralised makerspaces, negotiation between school staff/teachers and
centralised makerspace personnel is necessary to ensure that the equipment
meet the needs of each school.
• Shared equipment like 3D printers require ongoing negotiations, as to not cre­
ate flow and scheduling issues.
Obligatory Passage Points from the artefact level:
• Frameworks for maintenance, access, and procurement responsibility are crit­
ical in ensuring consistent makerspace quality.
• Makerspace equipment must be aligned with curriculum integration to ensure
successful learning.
At the complex system level, the integration of makerspace equipment and spatial layouts
that are designed for different end­users, reflect implication on the overall usability and
24
==PAGE:38==
accessibility in the makerspace.
First of all, it can be assumed that bottlenecks in makerspace activities, can be the result
of equipment that is designed for a different use case altogether. As makerspaces follow
industry standards and try to reflect similar manufacturing processes, means that some
equipment such as 3D printing and laser cutting, is mirrored as learning opportunities for
the students. Although this can provide hands­on learning with fabrication tools relevant
to the industry, the tools might not be designed for classroom use. One example where
this might be the case is with 3D printers. The 3D printing technology has many different
use cases and are particular useful for creating prototypes with medium to high fidelity.
However, most 3D printers is typically designed to fit the needs on a individual level,
which means the use case for groups is overlooked in the design of the equipment. This
could explain why many makerspaces is assumed to experience bottlenecks with the
equipment, as it was never meant to used in a large group or school setting to begin with.
A different aspect is the physical environment of the makerspace itself. In most cases,
an old classroom might be converted into a makerspace, if budget or spatial restrictions
do not allow for a new extension to the school. This can result in spatial layout chal­
lenges as some makerspace equipment require a certain amount of room and might have
safety requirements not suited for the interior design. As the room itself was originally
not designed for this specific use case, means that these limitations might not allow for a
functional makerspace.
Controversies from the complex level:
• Makerspace equipment that reflects the industry may not be designed for
classroom use, leading to bottlenecks and usability issues.
• Makerspaces converted from regular classrooms may not allow for spatial or
safety requirements of the equipment, reducing functionality and user safety.
Negotiations from the complex level:
• Schools must negotiate between existing spatial limitations and operational
requirements for makerspace equipment to ensure safety and usability.
• Equipment not build for classroom use require ongoing negotiations, to man­
age shared access.
Obligatory Passage Points from the complex level:
• Guidelines for educational makerspaces that reflect the needs of school and
group based learning.
• Planning for safety requirement when repurposing classrooms into functional
makerspaces.
At the engineering system level, new initiatives, rules, regulations, and standards might
impact how the makerspace is designed and used. One assumption is that the both
school­based and centralised makerspaces must adhere to certain rules and regulations
to ensure it operates as a safe environment for students. This might limit certain possibil­
ities and tools that are considered too dangerous or not up to code. Furthermore, each
individual municipality might have its own list of rules and regulation that the makerspace
in addition must adhere to, which further limits the possibilities. The definition of what a
makerspace is and how it is used might therefore be different depending on where it is
situated or change over time if rules and regulations are changed.
25
==PAGE:39==
New initiatives might also impact how a makerspace is designed. These initiatives could
try to force certain ideas into the makerspace thereby changing or impacting the design
and activities. In addition, new industry standards could have implications on what equip­
ment used in the makerspace, as they try to stay relevant and up to date. Overall, this
reflect the assumptions that the makerspace could become irrelevant over time if not
properly maintained and updated.
Controversies from the engineering level:
• Safety regulations and standards may limit the variety of equipment that can
be implemented in the makerspace.
• Municipality specific rules can lead to variability in makerspace design and
operation.
• Initiatives may conflict with local educational preferences and needs.
• Makerspaces risk becoming outdated or irrelevant if not continuously adapted
to new industry standards.
Negotiations from the engineering level:
• Makerspaces must adapt to shifting policies, safety standards, and novel tech­
nologies to ensure long­term viability.
Obligatory Passage Points from the engineering level:
• National and municipal safety standards and regulations that define what
equipment can be used in the makerspace.
• Educational initiatives that influence makerspace goals and usage.
• Standards, ensuring makerspace relevancy with current and future educa­
tional and industry practices.
4.3.2
Relation Mapping related to Curriculum and Teaching
In this aspect, the focus is on empowering teachers to be able to teach Technology Com­
prehension at the schools. Four main focus areas have been identified based on assump­
tions. On the artefact level, the main focus is the technical requirements for the teachers
in order to be able to use the equipment and teach the students how to use it. On the
complex level two areas have been identified. The first is fitting the curriculum to own pref­
erences in teaching methods and the students different interests. The second is making
the curriculum inclusive for everyone by differentiating the difficulty level and have options
for the students with mental and cognitive impairments. Finally on the engineering level,
the main focus area is the rules and requirements for using the makerspace equipment
as well as the requirement for integrating Technology Comprehension in the ordinary sub­
jects. These specific focus areas within the teaching aspect have been validated as the
most significant influencing factors through conversations with teachers, although the re­
lations between actors are based on assumptions. To make the full picture of each area
within the above defined scenarios, a total of 25 relation maps have been made, cover­
ing the specific areas as well as the general situation (see appendix RC2.1). Selected
maps have been highlighted in appendix PC2.2 to identify controversies, negotiations,
and obligatory passage points.
On the artefact level, the main finding in the analysis of assumptions is the problem related
to technical skills required for using the equipment. The teachers might not have the
26
==PAGE:40==
necessary competencies to operate the machines or teach students how to use them.
The problem is less significant in the situation with centralised makerspaces, as in this
case an employee will be in charge of teaching, and the regular teacher will be in charge of
classroom management. Contrary, in the case of school based makerspaces, the teacher
not only have to be able to operate the equipment alone, but also have to find time to help
all students. Here, the students technical skills play a crucial role, as the more difficult they
find a task, the more help is required from the teacher. With approximately 25 students
in a class and 45 minutes per lesson, the available time becomes a limiting factor for the
learning opportunities in the makerspace.
If the school do not have access to a makerspace, this will limit the teaching opportu­
nities for Technology Comprehension, and the teachers will have to find other ways to
implement it in their practice. The only difference identified between the public and in­
dependent schools is, that the independent schools do not have the option of using a
centralised makerspace, which means the problem with acquiring technical knowledge
becomes more significant.
Controversies from the artefact level:
• Teachers does not know how to integrate makerspaces in their practise.
• Teachers with low technical competencies can feel insecure or resistant in
integrating technologies in their practise.
• Teachers might feel the technologies as more of a hurdle than an opportunity
for their practice.
Negotiations from the artefact level:
• In centralised makerspaces, a negotiation between teacher and employee is
necessary to ensure alignment in areas of responsibility.
• Teachers and students needs to align on how and when help can be given to
ensure equal opportunities for all.
Obligatory Passage Points from the artefact level:
• Guidelines for how to teach Technology Comprehension.
• Legal framework for learning objectives and methods related to Technology
Comprehension.
At a complex system level, the assumptions and findings are related to modifying curricu­
lum based on students needs and preferred teaching methods. Every class is different
in terms of skills, interests, mindset, etc., and fitting the curriculum to the specific class
context takes a lot of time. In public schools, there is a legal requirement of inclusion of
all children, including those with mental, cognitive, and physical impairments. While this
is a good thing, it places pressure on the teacher who has to adjust the curriculum to ac­
commodate all students’ needs. In the independent sector, this is still a requirement, but
the school have different opportunities for allocating students with special needs to other
institutions. While this is also possible in the public sector, the process is quite different
an more comprehensive.
Adapting the curriculum to fit all students needs is a time consuming task. The curriculum
available for teachers is often very generic, meaning that while suitable in most cases,
adjustments are almost always needed to fit the specific class context. Curricula often
27
==PAGE:41==
comes with suggestions for how to adapt the difficulty of activities but rarely advise on how
to include children with mental and cognitive impairments. Furthermore, the curriculum
also have to be fitted to the teacher’s preferences for pedagogical approaches. Diversity
in teacher profiles can lead to complications in collaboration in planning lessons, as each
teacher have a specific agenda for activities. Adapting curricula and activities to both
students’ and teachers’ needs will increase motivation for both parts and create better
learning experiences.
However, this is only possible if the teacher are familiar with the class, and therefore not
possible in centralised makerspaces. The curriculum is therefore often generic and might
be demotivating for some of the students. Furthermore, the makerspace professionals are
not necessarily teachers themselves, and might not be familiar with the subject’s learning
objectives they have to comply with. This might be a reason for teachers not to choose
to utilise a centralised makerspace even though they have access.
Controversies from the complex level:
• Teachers are expected to adapt standardised curricula to meet widely diverse
needs but they might not have the necessary preparation time.
• Adapting teaching material requires extensive knowledge about the class con­
text.
• Inclusion is a legal and ethical obligation, but places significant extra demands
on teachers. The political ideals are not reflecting the practical constraints of
reality.
• Teachers have different values and preferences, leading to friction in collabo­
rative lesson planning.
• Makerspace employees might lack pedagogical and subject specific compe­
tence, leading to uncertainty about the quality of learning in centralised mak­
erspaces.
Negotiations from the complex level:
• Teachers must actively negotiate how to make learning accessible for stu­
dents with impairments, often without sufficient guidance or resources from
curriculum materials.
• Collaborative planning requires alignment in methods and approaches to cre­
ate coherent and inclusive lessons.
• In centralised makerspaces, there is a trade­off between access to equipment
and loss of pedagogical coherence.
Obligatory Passage Points from the complex level:
• Adapting generic curriculum based on specific classroom context or develop
new curricula.
• Definition of inclusive practice.
• For makerspace integration to succeed there must be a common understand­
ing about goals, methods, and roles.
The main finding of the assumptions at the engineering system level is that there is a
misalignment with the requirements from the ministry and the resources available for the
28
==PAGE:42==
schools to fulfil the requirements. It is a requirement from the Ministry of Children and
Education that all public schools have to teach Technology Comprehension in 2027, but
the education of the teachers to take on this extra task have been neglected. A lot of
money have been spent building makerspaces, however, not every school have access.
Furthermore, the teachers needs education and courses in how to teach this new subject
area. It is possible to teach Technology Comprehension without a makerspace, but the
makerspace is providing more opportunities and can help enhance STEM education and
21st century skills.
The main differences between the public and independent schools is where the resources
come from and what it can be spend on. This difference needs further investigation to
analyse the impact it has on schools and teaching practice.
Controversies from an engineering level:
• There is a misalignment between the rules and regulations for implementing
Technology Comprehension and the resources available in practice.
• Independent schools have more freedom in spending resources whereas pub­
lic schools have to adhere to political requirements and priorities. This creates
a systemic tension between the different school types.
• Uneven access to technologies can raise the question if makerspaces are
even necessary for teaching Technology Comprehension.
Negotiations from an engineering level:
• Teachers and school management needs to negotiate how the requirements
for Technology Comprehension will be implemented in practice.
• Public schools can try to push for more resources from the municipality.
• A compromise needs to be made between educational ambitions and practical
limitations.
Obligatory Passage Points from an engineering level:
• Requirements of implementing Technology Comprehension in 2027 by the
Ministry of Children and Education.
• Local strategies for how to implement the subject in practice.
4.3.3
Relation Mapping related to Business Model and Implementation
Strategy
The business model and implementation strategy are an important aspect for Gnist to
further explore and make concrete in order for it to become a sustainable business initia­
tive. The focus in this aspect of the thesis is therefore to make a proposal for a sound
business model and recommendations to an equally plausible implementation strategy
for Gnist Education ApS. The situational mapping workshop, see appendix RC4.1, were
used to identify controversies, nogotiations, and obligatory passage point from a business
standpoint.
The main findings of the situational analysis at the artefact level, were the the financial
cost tied to the establishment and running cost of a school oriented makerspace.
Key cost factors include the selection of equipment, how it is maintained, and the ongoing
29
==PAGE:43==
material usage. The usual makerspace equipment typically require a high cost such as
3D printers, laser cutters, vinyl cutters and other various hand tools. These tools might be
relatively expensive compared to a school’s usual expenses on materials and equipment
in similar categories such as paper printers, and crafting or arts supply.
Additional challenges are linked to the infrastructure requirements within the schools, such
as space and electricity. These infrastructural needs and associated costs are expected
to vary significantly between different school contexts.
Controversies on an artefact level:
• High financial cost of establishing and maintaining a school­oriented mak­
erspace.
• More expensive equipment compared to typical school equipment.
• Varying infrastructural capabilities across schools causing unequal implemen­
tation potential.
Negotiations on an artefact level:
• Selection of cost appropriate equipment, that balances cost and educational
value.
• Agreement on material consumptions and ongoing maintenance costs
• Discussing existing infrastructure with schools, to support makerspace needs
for implementation.
Obligatory Passage Points on an artefact level
• Guideline for aligning school budget with operational realities.
• Strategies for aligning school infrastructure with makerspace needs.
The main findings of the situational analysis at the complex level concern the internal
organisational structures of the schools. This include, their decision­making processes,
and financial capacity. These are especially important to consider, as they significantly
influence how the business model and implementation plan may be created and executed.
Furthermore, this analysis shows that public and independent schools are very different
from each other in this regard. These differences should be further investigated, espe­
cially in relation to financial capacity and decision making process. However, their overall
willingness and available resources to support teachers in implementing makerspace­
oriented teaching into their curriculum, should also be explored.
Understanding these distinctions are essential for the development of a business model
and implementation plan, that adaptable to meet the diverse realities of school environ­
ments.
30
==PAGE:44==
Controversies on an complex level:
• The difference between public and independent schools may have lead to
unequal implementation outcomes.
• The difference in priorities regarding openness to pedagogical implementa­
tion.
Negotiations on an complex level:
• Understanding of how to accommodate the difference in public and indepen­
dent schools.
• Aligning teacher training with each schools existing resources and structure.
Obligatory Passage Points on an complex level
• Identification of the schools decision­makers, and how they might influence
the implementation of the makerspace initiative.
• Financial and administrative differences of public and independent schools.
The main findings of the situational analysis on the Engineering Level relates to the
broader aspects of a makerspace implementation, and what the opportunities and bar­
riers coincidental in the current school­oriented makerspace situation.
It has previously been investigated, that in the period from 2019 to 2023 the non­profit and
philanthropic foundation Villum Foundation has supported the establishment of school­
oriented makerspaces in approx. one third of all Danish Municipalities and did so with
over 350 million Danish crowns in funding grants (Buus, 2021). Depending on the given
situation of the municipality and what made most sense to them, these makerspace were
either based at the school or centrally in the municipality as stated earlier. The munic­
ipalities who have succeeded with getting the grant have had a well defined scope and
strategy to how they were to implement their school­oriented makerspaces. They were
also able to cover half of the total expenses on equal terms with the foundation.
In practice, this type of funding structure, which is very common at this economical scale,
has in consequence marginalised other less fortuned municipalities.
This limits their
chances to make a strategic effort to implement makerspaces at their schools, as it is
too costly. From a hypothetical standpoint, it is believe that a large portion of independent
schools have not made a strategic makerspace implementation to their school. However,
the reason for this, is still unknown, and should be investigated further.
31
==PAGE:45==
Controversies on an engineering level:
• Funding structures and strategies, tend to favor municipalities with existing
financial and strategic capacity.
• There remain a uncertainty regarding why independent schools have not
adopted a strategic makerspace implementation.
Negotiations on an engineering level:
• Barriers and motivations for the makerspace implementation within the inde­
pendent schools should be explored.
• Accommodate a broader range of municipalities and school types, with fair
and aligned strategies.
Obligatory Passage Points on an engineering level:
• Strategies regarding funding models.
• Identification of decision­makers at independent schools, and the reason be­
hind them not to have a strategy for makerspace implementation.
4.4
Identified Actor Worlds and their Characteristics
Actor worlds were constructed by grouping identified human actors based on their com­
mon concerns, commitment to action, and shared objects. These actor worlds interact
differently depending on the system level and scenario, contributing to both tensions and
potential collaboration. Table 4.1 includes a few examples of the defined actor worlds. All
defined actor worlds on an artefact level can be found in appendix RC5, complex level
can be found in appendix RC6, and actor worlds on an engineering level can be found in
appendix RC7.
