These actor worlds were developed as a part of the transition from situational mapping
to the configuration of the development arena. By grouping actors through common con­
cerns, commitment to action, and shared objects, it became possible to abstract from in­
dividual stakeholder level and instead work with collective orientations and conflicts. This
abstraction enables the identification of strategic alignments, tensions, and gaps across
different system levels. The actor worlds makes is possible to model change not just in
terms of physical infrastructure or curriculum content, but as a transformation of actor
roles and relationships.
4.5
Initial As-Is Development Arena Based on Assumptions
To conclude the situational analysis, a development arena has been configured on the
findings from the situational mapping and actor worlds. The development arena is con­
structed on each systematic level to transgress a more extensive level of complexity and
heterogeneity, than what is usually possible to show in a development arena, while still
maintaining coding of information that extends across all three system levels. Figure 4.3
illustrates the final iteration of the As­Is development arena, mapping the current mak­
erspace situation in elementary schools on all three engineering levels. The full size
model can be found in appendix J­DA1.
The purpose of the arena is to provide a comprehensive overview of the dynamics shaping
the current makerspace situation in Dansih elementary schools. It serves as a diagnostic
tool for identifying systemic gaps, conflicting interests, and opportunities for invention.
Ultimately, it is forming the analytical foundation for the To­Be development arena mapping
the desired change, and the design of specific solutions to reach the target.
33
==PAGE:47==
Figure 4.3: Initial development arena for the current situation (As­Is). See model in full scale in
appendix J­DA1.
4.6
Problem Statement
Based on the findings from the situational mapping and the configuration of the As­Is
development arena, it has become evident that the current school­oriented makerspace
situation is characterised by fragmentation, unequal access, and a lack of cohesive sup­
port for implementation and use. These challenges span across physical infrastructure,
pedagogical practices, and strategic planning. In response to these insights, the following
problem statement has been formulated to guide the development of targetes solutions
across all three system levels.
Initial Problem Statement:
How can we make a detailed design of Gnist Education’s physical makerspace,
curriculum and teaching support, and a business model and implementation strat­
egy, that fits the needs and requirements in both public and independent elementary
schools, that will ultimately enhance the overall makerspace accessibility and oppor­
tunities for integrate Technology Comprehension as a part of the everyday teaching
practice?
4.7
Initial To-Be Development Arena of the Desired
Displacement
While the As­Is development arena highlights the fragmented nature of the current mak­
erspace situation for Danish elementary schools, the To­Be arena presents a vision for
how the situation could be transformed through targeted interventions. Figure 4.4 illus­
trated the desired displacement across all three system levels by repositioning actor roles,
relationships, and responsibilities. The To­Be development arena serves as a strategic
design framework that informs the development of Gnist’s mobile makerspace concept. It
reflects not only a response to identifies challenges but also proactive attempt to enable
more inclusive, scalable, and sustainable makerspace integration in Danish elementary
34
==PAGE:48==
schools. The full scale development arena can be found in appendix J­DA2.
Ultimately, the goals is to enhance makerspace accessibility for all school students in
Denmark both in public schools and in independent schools. Therefore, the initial solution
should focus on giving access to schools currently without access, while in the long term,
all schools should have access to the Gnist Mobile Makerspace.
Figure 4.4: Initial development arena for the desired situation (To­Be). See model in full scale in
appendix J­DA2.
To realise this vision, it is essential to define a clear scope and set of priorities for the
project. The following section outlines the practical limitations, focus areas, and strategic
choices that frame the research in DS­I and development of proposed solutions in PS.
These boundaries help ensure that the project remains feasible, targeted, and grounded
in the real­world conditions of Danish elementary schools.
4.8
Limitations and Scoping of the Thesis
As most of the analysis in the research clarification phase have been created based on
assumptions, it is necessary to get a better understanding and scope the research so that
the solutions can be created on a foundation of academic literature and key stakeholder
exploration.
The research will be divided into the three key areas related to the physical makerspace,
teaching, and business aspect which later will form the basis for the development of so­
lutions for the makerspace platform. Figure 4.5 illustrates the aspects within each focus
areas relevant for further investigation. Each of these areas represents the essential
(blue), and useful (light blue) research elements that has significant relevance to the cor­
responding focus area. Additional elements that might be useful to look into (dashed line),
is also visualised in the diagram as they should not be completely overlooked as a fac­
tor that can strengthen the final solutions, even though they are not prioritised during the
literature search.
35
==PAGE:49==
Figure 4.5: Overview of potential areas of relevance for further exploration in DS­I.
Each of the three focus areas are essential for making a viable solution. The physical as­
pect will ensure the makerspace is usable by students and teachers, the teaching aspect
will ensure that the makerspace can be integrated in everyday practice, and the business
aspect will ensure the makerspace can be booked and placed at the schools. All three
aspects are deeply interrelated and all essential for the full Gnist Mobile Makerspace con­
cept.
4.8.1
Goal for the Physical Makerspace
The goal of the physical space is to ultimately enhance the user experience and usability
of a makerspace and its equipment, thereby contributing to a higher quality of a learning
experience for both teachers and students in both the public and independent school
sectors.
To achieve this goal, the technical knowledge required to maintain the equipment in the
makerspace must be considered in order to enhance the usability, as it is crucial that the
equipment can be operated when needed and without being troublesome for the user.
Another key consideration is the equipment availability, as more equipment can strengthen
the overall learning experience and give more opportunities for the teachers and students.
However, a lot of different equipment require a considerable large amount of space, which
can be troublesome for certain schools. The mobile makerspace should therefore con­
sider the modularity of the space which would allow a tailored experience to be created
for different user needs, and allow a high utilisation rate, for multiple sets of equipment.
36
==PAGE:50==
4.8.2
Goal for the Possibility of Teaching in Makerspaces
The goal of this focus area is to support teachers in integrating makerspaces in their
practice and thereby enhance their ability to teach Technology Comprehension in both the
independent and public schools. This will enhance the learning experience for students,
and give all equal opportunities for accessing and utilising makerspaces.
To do this, the teachers needs to feel confident in facilitating makerspace activities in­
cluding the technical knowledge required to operate the machines themselves or have
someone else available to help with the equipment. In order to make good learning expe­
riences for the students, the curriculum should be fitted to the students interests and skills.
This means that a good solution will also help the teachers modify or develop curriculum
and thereby also minimise the time the teacher has to spend on this on their own. Finally,
a strong community among maker teachers can further support their practice, as this will
enable sharing of ideas and support collaboration among colleagues when planning ac­
tivities and curriculum.
4.8.3
Goal for the Strategic Implementation Plan
The goal of the strategic implementation aspect is to enhance the chances of a successful
solution roll­out. The solution is therefore aimed towards the management of Gnist Ed­
ucation ApS, rather than towards the target user­group. The development of a strategic
business plan can be considered to be a cornerstone for the distribution of the mobile
makerspace to schools.
In order for the strategic implementation plan to be successful it needs to take a holistic
perspective of the intricate systems that surrounds the current situation. For that reason
the strategic implementation plan will cover key aspects of the business model and take
note of logistical and infrastructural problem areas.
4.9
Research Plan
With the three focus areas defined and their respective goals clarified, the next step is
to investigate the assumptions that have formed the foundation of RC. This requires a
structures research plan to ensure that the final solutions are grounded in stakeholder
needs, supported by academic knowledge, and feasible to implement. The following sec­
tions presents the central research questions that will guide this investigation in DS­I and
inform the design and development of solutions in PS.
4.9.1
Research Questions
Through the situational analysis and creation of the initial development arenas, a lot of
assumptions have been made regarding the situation. In order to be able to develop
solutions that are relevant for the user, these assumptions needs to be investigated further.
This have led to the formulation of three research questions which this thesis aims to
answer.
In order to develop a solution that can motivate schools to choose to implement the Gnist
Makerspace, the first aspect that needs further investigation is the factors that can in­
fluence this decision. As seen in the situational mapping, the decision is not only made
by the teachers, but in collaboration with the school board or municipality. The first re­
search question aims to get a broader understanding of the problem space and define the
areas of improvement in the current situation by investigating the factors at play for the
implementation of a mobile makerspace in the school system.
37
==PAGE:51==
RQ 1: What factors are influencing a schools decision on integrating a makerspace
in the Danish elementary school and how is this affecting Gnist Education?
To answer this question, we need to talk to stakeholders and gather information through a
systematic literature review. This will be covered in Descriptive Study I. The found factors
will then be summarised in a design specification with requirements and criteria for the
solution. This will ensure the development in the Prescriptive Study will fit the user’s
needs.
It is expected that different stakeholders have different needs, which will make the final
solution threefold, with one aspect focused on the teachers who has to facilitate teaching
in makerspaces, one on the schools financial situation, and one on the actual physical
space. Different factors can be in play for different stakeholders. For the teachers this
might include how the makerspace can be integrated in the teaching, and for the schools
it might be the price that are the determining factor.
When most influencing factors are covered, the development of the solutions can begin.
Here, the overall goal is to enhance the quality of the learning experience, and the usability
of the makerspace and fulfil the user’s requirements defined by RQ1.
RQ2: How might a detailed design of the Gnist Education concept be developed to
integrate the makerspace in a Danish school setting?
In order to answer this question, solutions need to be developed and tested with the users
through the Prescriptive Study. Methods like co­creation will be used, as having the end
user involved in the development can give valuable and in­depth insights and help ensure
the solution is grounded in real­world experiences and needs. Prototyping will be used to
perform smaller tests on parts of the system. RQ2 have many different aspects, and to
cover all of it, three different solutions within each focus area will be developed.
Finally, when the support tools are developed, the impact needs to be validated. It is
important, that the solutions are feasible and possible to do. This includes the technical,
financial and operational aspects. But it is also important, to keep the viability in mind,
and determine long­term success. Finally, the solutions should be assessed to evaluate
if they are actually solving the problem and are desirable for the user.
RQ3: How can the developed solution(s) be assessed and validated to strengthen
the integration of the Gnist Education concept and what significance will it have for
the startup?
To answer this question, a final MVP test will be made in the Descriptive Study II. Here,
the solutions will be evaluated based in the criteria and requirements defined in the design
specification. Ultimately, an assessment of whether the solutions leads to the expected
outcome will be used to evaluate the success of the proposed concepts. The findings
might reveal areas of improvement for enhancing the overall goal of optimising the us­
ability of the makerspace and give the students a good quality of learning experience.
Unfortunately due to time limitations of the thesis, these areas of improvement will not
be implemented in the solutions, but an implementation plan will be presented and fu­
ture work described. This is especially important to ensure the long­term viability of Gnist
Education as a start­up and further development of the solution.
38
==PAGE:52==
4.9.2
Research Process
Each research questions is tied to the different stages in the DRM framework, as illustrated
in Figure 4.6. Each phase is building on top of the next, which means they are overlapping
in research questions. The research questions are covering the user’s needs which will be
uncovered in DS­I, the development of solutions in PS, and evaluation of the final concept
through an MVP test in DS­II.
Figure 4.6: Overview of the how the research questions are related to the different phases of the
thesis.
39
==PAGE:53==
5
Descriptive Study I
The purpose of Descriptive Study I is to further explore the current key situational aspects
of controversies, negotiations and obligatory passage points related to the school­oriented
makerspace situation. Through literature review, desktop research, and empirical data
collection, this chapter makes an effort to inform the development, and to expand the
inventory of Gnist Education’s accumulated knowledge to guide Gnist in the right direc­
tion forward. Throughout the study, the findings will be summarised in ‘key takeaways’,
‘design implications’ and ‘criteria for success’. These will be used to inform the design
brief, that will serve as our leaping point in the development phase, which the prescriptive
study explores. To summarise, the findings will be contextualised to inform the the new
situational starting point, and to then re­iterate the As­Is and To­Be development arena,
with updates to the key situational aspects.
Overview of Methods and Activities
The following methods were used in DS­I to investigate the factors influencing the imple­
mentation of makerspaces in schools.
• Systematic literature review
• Desktop research
• Empirical data collection
• Analysis of findings
• Design specification
Research Questions Covered in this Phase
RQ1: What factors are influencing a schools decision on integrating a makerspace
in the Danish elementary school and how is this affecting Gnist Education.
40
==PAGE:54==
5.1
Approach of Methods
To gain new insights and explore the factors at play for the implementation of a mak­
erspace in the school system (RQ1), a systematic literature review (SLR), desktop re­
search and empirical data will be used to clarify the assumptions in RC.
A combined analysis of the findings from the SLR, desktop research and empirical data
collection will be used to assess potential controversies, negotiations and obligatory pas­
sage points for implementing the Gnist Makerspace. The findings will also provide the
information needed to form an understanding of the design requirements and the suc­
cess criteria summarised in a design specification. The three approaches and methods
used will be clarified in the following sections.
5.1.1
Systematic Literature Review
The SLR was conducted with the purpose of gathering different perspectives based on
the three focus areas, the physical makerspace development, teaching and curriculum
development, and the possibilities and limitations of business implementation. The goal
was to expand the understanding and close the knowledge gaps of the assumptions pre­
viously stated in the Research Clarification. To structure and keep a level of transparency,
the SLR was conducted in accordance with the 2020 updated Preferred Reporting Items
for Systematic Reviews and Meta­Analysis (PRISMA) created by Page et al. (2021). The
PRISMA are used as a guideline for ensuring a specific literature search strategy which
were be mapped out in a flow map, seen in Figure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: The PRISMA flow map, showing the process of the SLR (see appendix B for more
detail).
41
==PAGE:55==
The initial problem statement, created in RC, was used to create multiple search strings
that reflect the three focus areas. These search strings were used to find relevant literature
through databases such as DTU FindIt, Scopus and Google Scholar. These records were
then narrowed through a screening process, where each article were evaluated based on
inclusion criteria. This screening process were performed on three different levels, title
and abstract, introduction and conclusion, and full text read, see appendix B. This allowed
only relevant articles to be included in the final analysis.
5.1.2
Desktop Research
Although the SLR proved to be useful, particular in identifying new pieces of literature that
otherwise would have been overlooked, it also had its limitations. To fill certain knowledge
gaps, especially regarding the possibilities and limitations of business implementation, a
complementary desktop research approach was used. This allowed for a broader back­
ground search to fill contextual gaps which was not included in the SLR databases. This
ensured that relevant topics, not yet covered through academic literature, could be used
to enhance the analysis.
5.1.3
Empirical Data Collection
Finally, to compare the insights from the literature with the current situation at schools,
empirical data was gathered through semi­structured interviews, observations, and atten­
dance at presentations during the Big Bang Conference. These insights were analysed
thematically and organised into categories that reflect key areas of concern identified in
RC. All insights from the empirical study can be found in appendix DSI.
At the Big Bang Conference several observations were made at the expo by visiting booths
and engaging in informal conversations (appendix DSI3). In addition, several presenta­
tions were held by teachers and researchers an various topics related to STEM education
(appendix DSI4). The conference also served as a networking opportunity to establish
contact with curriculum developers for follow­up interviews.
Semi­structured interviews were conducted with both teachers (appendix DSI5) and cur­
riculum developers (appendix DS6). Notes were taken during the interviews to capture
quotes and key points and were later transcribed and translated. Each statement was
colour­coded: green for positive feedback or suggestions for a solution, and red for com­
ments indicating aspects to avoid or improve. Table 5.1 contains an overview of the all
the interviews conducted as a part of DS­I.
Table 5.1: Overview of interviews
Name
Position
Contact

5.2
Findings Related to Designing a Makerspace and
Equipment Considerations
In this section, the literature and empirical findings related to the physical makerspace and
the equipment will be presented and analysed to find specific design implications. Certain
controversies and challenges will furthermore be discussed as designing a makerspace
goes beyond simple tool selection and allocation of space. Moreover, physical design
considerations also play important roles in creating an effective and future­ready learning
environment that adapts over time and feel welcoming. These elements overall ensures
that the makerspace can continue to serve as a dynamic environment, built for learning
and creativity.
5.2.1
Equipment Choice
The choice of equipment in a makerspace setting is a critical design decision that can af­
fect not only the learning outcome but also the accessibility of different activities. Both the
literature and empirical study revealed several dimensions that are important in evaluating
the optimal equipment choice such as, manufacturing, efficiency and cost consideration,
teacher perspectives and pedagogical flexibility, practical limitations, and safety consid­
erations.
Manufacturing Efficiency and Cost Considerations
A recurring theme in the literature regarding makerspace equipment is the challenge of
balancing the high upfront cost of the equipment against the long­term educational ben­
efits. Equipment like 3D printers and laser cutters require a significant initial investment,
whereas certain design software like TinkerCAD which is freely available can act as a pow­
erful enabler to support design thinking (Tilak et al., 2024). Tilak et al. (2024) additionally
finds that every technology in the makerspace can be used to greatly develop positive
perceptions related to design thinking, collaboration, and social skill development.
When it comes to the manufacturing process of the equipment, Rayna and Striukova
(2021) finds that 3D printing has critical advantages in a low volume setting due to its ad­
ditive manufacturing process which produce less waste compared to subtractive methods.
43
==PAGE:57==
Consumer grade 3D printers have additionally become more affordable, reliable and safe
in the recent years, which makes them more accessible and desirable to schools (Har­
ron et al., 2022). That said, some controversies remain with 3D printing technology as
noted with empirical observations from CFU in their makerspace Future Classroom Lab
in Denmark. They reveal that their 3D print workshop as versatile and popular as it is,
remain slow and can often fail, making them less suited for fast classroom production and
manufacturing (see appendix DSI6.2).
Laser cutting on the other hand, uses a subtractive manufacturing process by focusing
a beam of light that cuts and engraves materials such as wood, cardboard and acrylic
sheets. While laser cutting is considerably faster compared to 3D printing, it requires
more safety precautions due to the fumes created by cutting certain materials (Harron
et al., 2022). Still, Harron et al. (2022) finds that laser cutting as a technology can help
expand the learning opportunities with various projects.
Overall, while 3D printing and laser cutting each have their own benefits and advantages,
neither technology make up a makerspace on its own. As stated by Welbourn (2019)
“makerspaces are not a one­size fits all”, instead a varied and balanced selection of tools
best support a meaningful learning opportunity for the students. This furthermore max­
imises the manufacturing efficiency while managing the costs of both equipment and ma­
terials effectively.
Key takeaway: A balanced mix of accessible tools and technologies is essential to
maximise both educational value and cost effectiveness in makerspaces.
Design implication: The final makerspace must include a diverse set of equipment
and accessible design software that can create an efficient and economically viable
environment.
Criteria for success: Equipment cover both additive and subtractive manufactur­
ing processes to foster design thinking, collaboration, and skill development while
considering material cost.
Teacher Perspectives and Pedagogical Flexibility
The teacher’s perspective can greatly help influence the final choice of equipment as they
play a crucial part in creating learning opportunities for students. In a study done by Hira et
al. (2018) multiple teachers were asked to provide insight related to different makerspace
equipment such as laser cutters, 3D printers, and vinyl cutters. The study finds that the
teachers strongly emphasise the need for flexibility and variety in the equipment. By
providing a wide range of tools allows students to personalise their artefacts and connect
their learning to meaningful experiences using novel technologies like 3D printing. This
aligns closely with empirical findings done in a survey, where teachers from eight different
primary schools in Denmark showed interest in trying many different makerspace tools
(see appendix DSI1.1). However, they particularly valued laser cutters, 3D printers, and
green screens highest based on what they felt comfortable working with and what showed
most potential.
These findings are also mirrored in well­established makerspaces such as Udskoling­
shuset and Future Classroom Lab. Udskolingshuset, which is a centralised makerspace
for 6th to 9th grade students in Gentofte municipality, offer a broad and diverse selection
of tools and equipment, from 3D printers and laser cutters to green screen rooms and
podcasts studios as seen in Figure 5.2 (also see appendix DSI2.3). CFU have likewise
44
==PAGE:58==
created a makerspace called Future Classroom Lab with a broad selection of tools and
equipment to showcase and help give agency to teachers (see appendix DSI6.2).
Figure 5.2: Podcast studio observed at Udskolingshuset (left), and vinyl cutter station with multiple
types of equipment observed at CFUs makerspace called Future Classroom Lab (also seen in
appendix DSI2.3 and DSI6.2).
Common results from both these makerspaces show that a broad selection of tools and
equipment have proven valuable for fostering student creativity and motivation. Observing
both makerspaces have furthermore shown the importance of continuously updating the
equipment and curriculum to stay engaging and relevant for the student and teachers
(see appendix DSI2.3 and DSI6.2). In addition, an interview with LIFE have shown the
importance of avoiding equipment bottlenecks as a key priority which can be caused by
over reliance on one type of equipment (see appendix DSI6.3).
New learning opportunities was also presented at the Big Bang Conference, where new
technologies and equipment were observed as seen in Figure 5.3. These included modu­
lar building components called Sphero blueprint that had the function of teaching founda­
tional engineering concepts through prototyping. Sphero Indi was another inspiring piece
of equipment where kids could learn to code and develop computational thinking with
a guiding a small robot through mazes built using modular pieces. The last technology
to make an impression was MiniOne Systems which used Fluorescence Reader along
with Electrophoresis and PCR systems, these could be a great addition to create a small
chemistry lab, as all the equipment was small and portable (see appendix DSI3.1).
45
==PAGE:59==
Figure 5.3: Equipment observed at Big Bang Conference, Sphero blueprint (left), Sphero Indi
(middle), and MiniOne Systems (right), also seen in appendix DS3.1.
With this, both the literature and the empirical findings from a teacher perspective sup­
port the idea that a makerspace should be designed as a dynamic learning environment.
Equipment that focusses on flexibility and adaptability therefore needs to be prioritised
over a one­size­fits­all toolkit.
Key takeaway: A flexible and varied selection of makerspace equipment is essen­
tial to support student creativity, engagement, and meaningful learning.
Design implication: The makerspace must implement user­friendly equipment that
supports adaptability and an evolving curriculum to stay relevant.
Criteria for success: The makerspace are able to empower and give students and
teachers a spark to develop new skills, including design thinking through relevant
equipment.
Safety Considerations
Safety plays a crucial part when it comes to equipment and material choice, particular
in relation to air quality and emissions that can be affected by manufacturing processes.
One study done by Li and Glassford (2025), shows that 3D printers can release ultrafine
particles (UFPs) especially during the startup of the printer, or when a print failure oc­
cur. The study moves on to list a couple of best­practice step that can prevent potential
exposures, control odours and reduce emissions. These include placing 3D printers in
ventilated enclosures, connecting them to local exhaust ventilation, and selecting filament
materials that emits fewer UFPs such as PLA rather than ABS.
Similar findings are shown by Branche Fællesskab Arbejdsmiljø (BFA), that likewise rec­
ommend using safer material when operating 3D printers. Even though they find that
process ventilation is not required for PLA­based filament, they still recommend having
some kind of ventilation as the material can have unknown chemicals added in the dyeing
process (BFA Velfærd og Offentlig administration, 2024). They continue to add that other
type of tools such as laser cutters should, unlike 3D printers, always be using process
ventilation regardless of the type of material. Furthermore, they add that the laser cutter
should use invisible infrared light such as a CO2 laser, as laser cutters using visible light,
such as diode laser cutters, can damage the eyes and are therefore banned in primary
46
==PAGE:60==
schools in Denmark (BFA Velfærd og Offentlig administration, 2024).
Empirical observations from Udskolingshuset and CFU reinforce the importance of effec­
tive safety measures as they help ensure student and teachers well­being while maintain­
ing an engaging learning environment. These measures include workshop ventilation,
clear operational policies, and keeping equipment up to date with maintenance (see ap­
pendix DSI2.3 and DSI6.2).
Key takeaway: Certain safety measures, especially around air quality and emis­
sions, are important to consider when designing a makerspace to ensure a healthy
learning environment.
Design implication: The makerspace must comply with safety standards for equip­
ment which include a proper ventilation system that can support process ventilation,
and furthermore must use materials that limits the exposure to UFP’s.
Criteria for success: Students and teachers feel safe in the makerspace envi­
ronment, as their well­being is prioritised through throughtful use of material and
equipment.
5.2.2
Technical Challenges and Opportunities
The equipment used in a makerspace setting can offer high educational benefits, however
it is important to address a range of technical challenges that can influence the effective­
ness of different maker activities. It is furthermore interesting to see if opportunities exist
to optimise certain workflows and processes to enhance usability.
Equipment Reliability and Functionality
Multiple studies from the literature highlight several recurring controversies and chal­
lenges related to the implementation of specific equipment in a makerspace setting. Lee
et al. (2020) find that teachers are frequently faced with unreliable equipment which in­
clude 3D printers that break down, have lengthy boot­up times, and internet connectivity
problems. These challenges can interrupt and waste a lot of class time. Especially 3D
printers can be troublesome with slow printing speeds that further limit the feasibility of
working with them in short lesson periods. Some 3D printers also need additional bed­
levelling before the printing process can begin, which further slows the entire process
(Knochel, 2017). Most of these findings can be tied to the fact that most consumer­grade
3D printers are designed for individual and not group use, which is making them poorly
suited for large classes and group projects (Fontichiaro, 2020). Welbourn (2019) continue
the argument that even though 3D printing remains a powerful tool for supporting iterative
design thinking, it keeps struggling with controversies and challenges that can dampen
the excitement from student and teachers.
Additional field observations support these findings. For example, participants explained
during a trip to the Big Bang Conference that they rarely used their 3D printer because
it was somehow almost always broken down (see appendix DSI3.1). Another participant
who worked at Odense municipality highlighted specific administrative and policy restric­
tion such as GDPR rules. These rules prevent students from downloading the necessary
programs and software used to operate certain equipment found in makerspace settings,
such as 3D printers and laser cutters (see appendix DSI3.1).
In response to the controversies and challenges found with 3D printing, CFU confirmed
that a single print could occupy the 3D printer an entire day which would make it unsuitable
47
==PAGE:61==
for rapid classroom iterations (see appendix DSI6.2). One possible solution that was
presented by LIFE, was to install cameras on the equipment and 3D printers (see appendix
DSI6.3). This would allow students to monitor the progress of their prints remotely and
help to address their frustrations with the speed and reliability issues.
Key takeaway: Certain issues like unreliable equipment, slow production speeds
and administrative restrictions present major obstacles to the implementation of an
effective makerspace.
Design implication: The makerspace must adhere to GDPR rules with streamlined
administrative access, and use equipment that is designed for group use rather than
individual operation.
Criteria for success: Downtime of the equipment is minimised, support rapid iter­
ations, and use smart solutions to improve equipment reliability and general user
experience.
Workflow and Process Optimisation
To combat some of these equipment challenges, certain workflow and process optimisa­
tions can be made. First of all, it is important to note that a makerspace cannot always
run on autopilot as it must adapt and be supported with professional learning over time.
Shively et al. (2021) recommend an on­site expert to be present at the makerspace, which
will allow and provide students with ongoing professional learning and equipment train­
ing. Some workflows in the makerspace can however be optimised to better manage the
student projects. One example by Michael et al. (2020) shows an automated ordering
system used at Penn’s Bio MakerSpace, where students can order their 3D print by filling
out a form on a spreadsheet. This allow the employees at the makerspace to check and
run these orders in a structured way and provide feedback to the students when prints
have been completed and ready to be collected. Another similar solution was presented
by Keune and Peppler (2019), where a queuing software that distribute print jobs across
multiple 3D printers overall helped the production at a makerspace and overall reduced
bottlenecks.
The empirical observation further stresses the importance of implementing new ways to
optimise the workflow and manufacturing process. At Udskolingshuset, their 3D printers
were running continuously throughout the day as demand was usually high from the stu­
dents. A lot of time is therefore spent in starting and swapping out prints (see appendix
DSI2.3). At CFU’s makerspace it was likewise noted that 3D print was in high demand.
Their 3D print workshop is however considerably larger, as seen in Figure 5.4, meaning
that even more time is spent on starting and swapping prints, as they currently have no
automation to optimise this workflow (see appendix DSI6.2).
48
==PAGE:62==
Figure 5.4: 3D printing workshop at Udskolingshuset (left), 3D printing workshop at CFUs mak­
erspace (right), also seen in appendix DSI2.3 and DSI6.2.
Key takeaway: Makerspaces generally requires ongoing professional support and
maintenance to optimise workflows and manage high equipment demand to ensure
smooth operations, which require a lot of man­hours.
Design implication: The makerspace must streamline processes to support user
experience and high demands on popular equipment to reduce bottlenecks.
Criteria for success: Workflow is optimised and manufacturing processes with
with automated solutions are used to achieve higher efficiency.
5.2.3
Knowledge, Skills, and Pedagogical Scaffolding
Effective learning in a makerspace requires more than just access to tools and equipment.
It is crucial to understand how proper knowledge and skill building are fundamental to
realising the educational potential of a makerspace, and how confidence is created in
students to use these tools effectively. Multiple literature and empirical findings highlight
which knowledge points are required, and how to use the makerspace as a scaffolding
and skill building environment.
Knowledge Requirements for Digital Fabrication
One study by Leskinen et al. (2024) finds that working effectively with digital fabrication
technologies such as 3D printers and laser cutters requires more than just basic tool
familiarity. By observing students in a makerspace called FUSE Studio, the study high­
lights that debugging along with testing and refining technical solutions enables students
to build knowledge through hands­on experimentation. Furthermore, it can help the stu­
dents to identify themselves as experts in specific areas. This can even foster collabora­
tive knowledge building, where students and teachers help each other to enhance their
understanding of the technologies.
To add to these knowledge requirements, students also need to have strong spatial think­
ing skills if they are planning to work with equipment like 3D printers. Bhaduri et al. (2021)
find that the spatial thinking skill is crucial to identify potential design flaws, that could
cause 3D print failures, which can be discouraging for the students. However, these
49
==PAGE:63==
challenges can also be reframed into learning opportunities that build resilience, criti­
cal thinking, and problem­solving skills. Welbourn (2019) finds that this can be done by
shifting the focus from producing finished objects with the equipment, to create room for
understanding the design cycle.
The frequent use of computers for digital fabrication is another important aspect, which
likewise requires specific knowledge and skills to operate effectively. Students are how­
ever shown in a study by Iwata et al. (2020) to effectively use computers to develop ideas,
design artefacts, and find relevant project information, and generally use the computer as
a tool to achieve their creative goals. This only highlights the possibilities that students
can begin their digital fabrication journey even from an early age.
Empirical findings add to this, by debating the overall purpose of a makerspace. CFU
for instance debated whether the focus should be on understanding the technology and
equipment used in the makerspace setting, or focus on the design process with the equip­
ment as a means to enhancing the learning opportunities (see appendix DSI6.2). With
the introduction of technology comprehension as a subject in Danish primary schools from
2027, makerspaces are expected to support advanced digital fabrication. This will give the
students the opportunity to experience such design processes through hands­on learning
with equipment and tools that mirror real­world cases. This is reflected in an interview with
LIFE where they state that Technology Comprehension is not about how the technology
works, but a reflection on the possibilities with the technology (see appendix DSI6.3).
Key takeaway: Students need to meet certain knowledge requirements to effec­
tively work with digital fabrication tools and equipment, which can be built with
hands­on experience.
Design implication: The makerspace must support the development of spatial
thinking, debugging and multiple digital fabrication skills to foster exploration and
iterative learning.
Criteria for success: Students are empowered to utilise multiple equipment to build
technological comprehension through reflection, creativity and problem solving.
Makerspaces as Scaffolding and Skill Building Environments
Confidence and progressive skill building has shown in literature findings to greatly factor
student motivation. In a study by Milara et al. (2017) findings show that students reported
a rise in their confidence levels after learning how to use 2D/3D design software and
fabrication equipment. They however also found that this would sometimes reveal certain
new gaps in their knowledge, and particular around electronics. Some tools can serve as
an accessible entry­level scaffold, which would allow students to gradually develop the
basic skills before moving to more advanced equipment. 3D pens are a great example as
they can give students a basic understanding on 3D technology without needing to learn
complex software beforehand (Imeri et al., 2017).
Providing multiple entry points is also reinforced in the empirical findings. Multiple pre­
sentations at Big Bang Conference, emphasised that starting early and embedding design
challenges into stories (see appendix DSI4.7 and DSI4.8), or collaborating with companies
(see appendix 4.6) can make the experience more relatable for young students, helping
them understand the purpose before diving into technical tasks.
In addition, teachers also pointed out that inclusion and skill building is not just about
access to tools, but about emotional safety and the ability to see oneself as capable. In
50
==PAGE:64==
a test with Lundtofte Skole, the students were not scared to work with the activities, even
though they perceived them as difficult. For example, most students chose programming
activity as their favourite and the one they would like to do in class, even though this was
also selected as the most difficult to learn (see appendix PS1.2). This shows that when
properly scaffolded, with multiple entry points and in an emotional safe environment that
student can embrace even complex challenges rather than be intimidated by them.
Key takeaway: Confidence and motivation grow when students are given emo­
tional safety and opportunities to succeed with progressively challenging tasks.
Design implication: The makerspace must help bridge understanding and skill
building by offering accessible tools and embed design challenges in relatable con­
texts that creates a supportive environment.
Criteria for success: Students feel empowered to work with difficult or advanced
tasks, and see themselves as capable, which can be achieved through progressive
skill building and continuously engaging technologies.
5.2.4
Mobility, Modularity, and Flexibility
The ability to adapt and respond to shifting needs is critical for a makerspace to ensure a
continuously engaging learning environment. It is therefore interesting to explore existing
solutions to understand both opportunities and limitation that might play a role, in a new
wave of mobile and modular makerspaces. Furthermore, spatial layouts and flexible room
designs will be examined to understand how to promote creativity, collaboration, and a
sense of belonging.
Mobile Solutions and Makerspace Opportunities
Over the last few years there have been a rising trend in creating mobile makerspace.
These makerspaces have even been compared to food trucks in their ability to go directly
to the customer or user instead of waiting for them to arrive (Fontichiaro, 2020). The liter­
ature present mobile makerspace in many different shapes and sizes ranging from vans
(Pupo, 2020) to portable kits that can be wheeled around in suitcases (Knochel, 2017).
The mobility of these spaces can provide new possibilities in the form of exploration, in­
novation and collaboration opportunities to schools, libraries and community centres by
extending their reach.
However, mobile makerspaces also face some multiple challenges which include, demand
fluctuations, transportation costs, and planning for routes and destinations (Moorefield­
Lang, 2015). Additionally, the small sizes of portable mini makerspaces can have certain
trade­offs between portability and performance (Knochel, 2017). Similar trade­offs are
shown in a study by Pupo (2020), where a mobile makerspace van is limited in perfor­
mance, and is mostly used to present and introduce new technologies and equipment and
not used as a fully fleshed out makerspace that can benefit larger groups of people.
The empirical findings did not show as many direct examples of fully mobile makerspaces,
with only one key example being showcased at the Big Bang Conference by LIFE Fonden.
They have created the LIFE Mobile Lab which is a science lab build into a truck that can
be rolled out to different schools across Denmark. The size of the LIFE Mobile Lab allows
students to have entire classes inside the truck and use the lab to its full potential. They
are however not focused on creating a maker environment but rather enhance ordinary
classes such as physics and chemistry (LIFE Fonden, 2018a). In addition to observing the
51
==PAGE:65==
mobile lab multiple sources from the Big Bang Conference indicated interest in modular
and transportable equipment that support the maker mindset. This suggests an opportu­
nity for the implementation of a true mobile makerspaces where mobility is combined with
high equipment functionality.
Key takeaway: Mobile makerspaces can offer flexible learning opportunities while
extending access to design and fabrication tools in schools and communities.
Design implication: The mobile makerspace must balance portability with func­
tionality, which include adaptable equipment that is suitable for transportation and
various locations.
Criteria for success: The mobile makerspace deliver meaningful, hands­on ex­
periences with novel technologies and equipment without sacrificing efficiency or
educational impact.
Modular Solutions and Makerspace Opportunities
Modularity is presented in the literature as a critical strategy for future proofing a mak­
erspace.
One study finds that a modular design allows the equipment, furniture and
layouts to evolve over time with changing educational and technological needs in mind
(Mathuews & Harper, 2018). Another study emphasises that a makerspace should be
flexible with reconfigurable spaces, for example, mobile furniture and storage which help
to accommodate new equipment over time (Kellam et al., 2021). Feedback forums are
also being recommended, where teachers and students can provide feedback and sug­
gest changes to the makerspace (Shively et al., 2021). With modular solutions, changes
like these would be more easily adapted, thereby creating a makerspace that respond to
its users.
In empirical observations, various modular workstation designs was observed. One of
these was observed at the Big Bang Conference, where the Lekular Creo system was
presented, which is a collection of various furniture that implements a slatwall for flexible
mounting both storage and equipment (see appendix DSI3.3). At Udskolingshuset and
CFU’s makerspace the spatial layout was being optimised with workbenches on wheels
that allowed easy room reconfiguration, and wall mounted storage systems. These flex­
ible workstations furthermore help the makerspaces to create engaging environments
with various configurations and learning opportunities (see appendix DSI2.3 and DSI6.2).
These findings can confirm that modular designs supports adaptability over time, while
simultaneously create room for a wide range of learning activities.
Key takeaway: Modular designs enable makerspaces to evolve and grow to ever­
changing educational and technological needs which ensures long­term adaptability
and relevance.
Design implication: The makerspace must include modular designs and furniture
that supports flexible storage and reconfigurable layouts that can respond to user
feedback and be future­proof.
Criteria for success: The modular makerspace setup supports diverse learning
activities, is able to accommodate new equipment and can easily adapt based on
user needs.
52
==PAGE:66==
Spatial Layout and Flexible Room Design
Literature findings point towards that spatial layout and room design in a makerspace
can significantly influence their overall success. Kellam et al. (2021) finds that students
preferred open and uncluttered worktables as they are more flexible in their use, which re­
sulted in 3D printers and other equipment to be moved to outer parts of their makerspace.
Moreover, they find that certain questions can be considered to help create a welcom­
ing and more inclusive space. These include, if the physical environment encourages or
limit collaboration and how the makerspace can be arranged to encourage even more
collaboration and be a space that students want to be in (Kellam et al., 2021).
Empirical findings further emphasise the importance of creating a space that feel wel­
coming. Udskolingshuset for example, had plants and soft lighting in almost every room,
as seen in Figure 5.5, along with curtains that functions as both soundproofing and to
hide materials and equipment not in use. This helped to create a calming and welcoming
space for the students, that is not too busy or distracting (see appendix DSI2.3).
Figure 5.5: Plants and soft lighting used throughout Udskolingshuset. (also seen in appendix
DSI2.3).
Both CFU and Udskolingshuset state that students feel more comfortable and engaged in
welcoming and flexible spaces and agreed that the physical design of the space affected
the overall learning outcome (see appendix DSI2.1 and DSI6.2). These aspects (spatial
layout and flexibility) can therefore help the students to develop a sense of belonging in
the makerspace, which can have direct implication on the learning outcome.
53
==PAGE:67==
Key takeaway: The spatial layout and general atmosphere in a makerspace can
significantly influence students learning outcomes by being a welcoming and com­
forting environment that supports collaboration.
Design implication: The makerspace should prioritise open, uncluttered work ar­
eas, and implement calming elements such as plants and soft lighting along with
thoughtful equipment placements.
Criteria for success: Students feel a sense of belonging in the makerspace and
are generally positively affected by the physical environment.
5.3
Findings Related to Teaching in Makerspaces and
Curriculum Development
In this section, the research findings related to teaching in makerspaces will be presented
and analysed to find design implications. It is essential to understand how teachers expe­
rience and navigate the complexities of teaching to ensure the development of an effec­
tive solution that supports the integration of makerspaces in education. This part of DS­I
draws on systematic literature review, desktop research, and empirical data to explore the
teacher’s role in makerspace education and the transition from a passive implementer of
predefined activities to a key agent in shaping meaningful maker­based learning experi­
ences.
The findings highlight both the opportunities and constraints teachers face when inte­
grating makerspace activities into everyday practice. They reveal underlying tensions
between the aspiration of maker pedagogy and the realities of classroom management,
including structural limitations, professional identity, and confidence in integrating new
theory and technologies in subjects. This study view these factors as interdependent
factors that collectively influence whether teachers feel empowered to take ownership of
makerspace teaching. By identifying recurring themes across sources and contexts, this
section aims to clarify where support is needed, what enables successful integration, and
how a solution can be designed to align with teachers’ needs and motivations. These
insights form the foundation for the design specification and proposed solution in the pre­
scriptive phase of the thesis.
5.3.1
The General Teaching Context and Structural Conditions
Before introducing new pedagogical practices like makerspace education, it is crucial to
understand the everyday realities that shape teachers’ decision­making and capacities.
In Denmark, public school teachers face a range of systemic constraints, from time pres­
sure to limited autonomy over curriculum development, that influence their ability to adopt
new approaches. This section outlines the structural, professional, and emotional factors
that shape teachers’ daily experience. These insights serve as a foundation for under­
standing why new initiatives like makerspaces must be aligned with the practical realities
of teaching.
The Core Task of Teaching vs. Everyday Demands
Teaching practice is not only planning curriculum and facilitating teaching. It also includes
dealing with parents, solving conflicts among students, participating in meetings, and so
much more. In reality, the teaching aspect of being a teacher is surprisingly small com­
pared to all the other tasks, forcing teachers to compromise the quality of their teaching.
54
==PAGE:68==
“Det sværeste ved lærerarbejdet er, at hverdagen er så presset, at de altid er
bagud og bliver nødt til at gå på kompromis med deres idéer om, hvad gode
lærere er og gør.” (Böwadt & Vaaben, 2021)
During the last 10 years, multiple studies have been conducted to investigate the well­
being of teachers in public schools. Here, three studies have been included; a qualitative
study among 107 new teachers (Böwadt & Vaaben, 2021), a quantitative study among
1142 new teachers (Frederiksen et al., 2017), and a qualitative study with 1232 teaches
where the majority is more experienced (Böwadt et al., 2019). Common for all three
studies are that they are not statistically representative for all teachers in Denmark, but
are still containing insights into the reality of teaching practice relevant for the scope of
this thesis.
First and foremost, the teachers describe their core task as educating their students and
solve the tasks related to teaching. This includes planning curriculum and coordinating
with colleagues, developing teaching material, correcting homework, building relations
with student, and deal with student’s social and academic development (Böwadt et al.,
2019). This corresponds to the purpose of the Danish public schools cf. Danish Primary
and Lower Secondary Education Act (Folkeskoleloven) §1, which is describing the core
task of a teacher as providing students with knowledge, skills, and personal development
that foster a desire for learning, support their all­round growth, and prepare them to par­
ticipate responsibly in a democratic society (Børne­ og Undervisningsministeriet, 2024).
The problem is that teachers do not have time to focus on their core task, as their prepa­
ration time often is used for other tasks like documentation, meetings, evaluations, mes­
sages, tests, etc., all something outside the actual teaching practice. Some teachers point
to it as crucial to the success of their teaching, while others call it a waste of time, as it
neither supports the students nor the teaching (Böwadt et al., 2019). Most have an idea
of a “good” teacher as someone who has developed their own curriculum that are exciting
for the students, are good communicators and facilitators, see the students, and makes a
difference in the students lives by helping them academically and socially, but having to
compromise their believes leads to frustrations (Böwadt et al., 2019; Böwadt & Vaaben,
2021). To motivate the teachers, integrating makerspaces in education should be related
to their core task and not feel like yet another project initiated by the state.
Key takeaway: Teachers want to focus on student­centred learning but are often
pulled away by administrative and non­instructional tasks.
Design implication: The solution must be directly related to core teaching tasks to
avoid feeling like an external initiative.
Criteria for success: Teachers perceive the solution as supporting and not com­
peting with their core educational responsibilities.
Time Constraints and their Effects
In all three statistical studies, lack of time is mentioned as something that complicates
practice, a stress factor, and a cause for teachers to lose motivation, but most teachers
still express their love for their profession (Böwadt et al., 2019; Böwadt & Vaaben, 2021;
Frederiksen et al., 2017). Time constraints give the feeling of always being behind and
being inadequate, and teachers are often told to lower their ambitions to avoid burning
out (Böwadt & Vaaben, 2021). Further, introducing the new subject Technology Compre­
hension can add to the feeling of inadequacy when teachers have to integrate a new role,
55
==PAGE:69==
theory, and equipment they are unfamiliar with (Wagner, 2021). It requires time to get
familiar with the technologies and be able to integrate them in curriculum, but teachers in
the pilot project reported that they were experiencing pressure and lacking time for infor­
mal tasks such as conversations with the students (Mikkelsen, 2020). A key point in this
context is that neither new nor experienced teachers differ from each other when it comes
to the experiences of time pressure and the frustrations associated with it (Böwadt et al.,
2019). New teachers feel alone in a setting where everything is new, and even though
their colleagues are willing to help, they are not able to due to their own time constraints
(Böwadt & Vaaben, 2021).
Overall, teachers are pointing at the misalignments between requirements and resources
as the main reason for their limited time, which then is spent inefficiently because the
school is governed by people who are too far removed from the everyday reality of the
school and therefore launch initiatives that pull teachers’ time and focus away from their
core tasks (Böwadt et al., 2019). Time pressure is, by many teachers, seen as the biggest
limitation for trying something new, especially in the beginning of integrating new initia­
tives (Wagner, 2021). In the national test integrating Technology Comprehension, time
constraints at schools was mentioned as the deciding factor for teachers’ involvement in
the project. The limited preparation time would cause teachers to choose not to partici­
pate even though they found the project exciting, as they would have to spend their free
time getting familiar with the technologies (Wagner, 2021).
Due to the already restricted time for the teachers, it is important that integrating mak­
erspace education will not have a higher time consumption than teachers are currently
using for planning their curriculum.
Key takeaway: Time pressure affects all teachers, undermining motivation and
limiting pedagogical innovation.
Design implication: The solution must be time­efficient. Integrating maker activi­
ties in teaching should be achievable within existing preparation time.
Criteria for success: Teachers report that they save time or is spending the same
time as their current planning processes.
Professional Identity, Recognition, and Teacher Motivation
A common theme for all three statistical studies is lack of respect for teaching practice.
75% of new teachers report that their management team sees them as a valuable resource
and respect their knowledge, and even more from their colleagues (Frederiksen et al.,
2017). It has a positive effect on teachers when the leaders are meeting them with trust,
respect and acknowledgement (Böwadt et al., 2019). Quite the contrary is said about the
parents. Many new teachers experience parent relations as hard due to lack of respect
for teaching practice (Böwadt & Vaaben, 2021).
Some point to the fact that they are young and new in the field as a reason for the lack of
respect from parents, but experienced teachers are also highlighting this problem (Böwadt
et al., 2019). They are especially highlighting the view of teaching practice in the media as
problematic. Here teachers are presented as undereducated compared to neighbouring
countries (L. B. Jørgensen, 2019), as lazy without ambitions (P. S. Hansen, 2020), and
that too many substitute teachers are in charge (Rosenqvist & Warner, 2024). What is
curious is that these statements are often only related to teachers in public schools, even
though teachers in private schools have the same education (Winther­Jensen, 2010).
56
==PAGE:70==
This indicates that the problems might not be related to the education itself, but to the
teaching conditions in public schools. There is a lack of understanding for what teach­
ing entails, so it is important that the developed solution for integrating makerspaces at
schools is highlighting the teacher’s effort in a way that induces respect and recognition
among parents, the ministry, and other teachers.
Another key motivator for teachers is recognition from colleagues and other teachers,
particularly from strangers. That way, they know that the recognition is genuine as there
is no personal connection or hidden agendas (appendix DSI5.3). Recognition for school
leadership can sometimes feel forced or insincere, like they are only saying it because
it will make them a good leader. In the interviews, teachers expressed a desire for a
professional space where it would feel “safe” to share their work without fear of judgment
or performance anxiety. For instance, one teacher highlighted that sharing achievements
on Linkedin can feel like bragging and puts you in a vulnerable position, see appendix
DSI5.3. This sense of vulnerability plays a significant role in teachers’ willingness to share
or contribute to collaborative curriculum platforms. These insights suggest that motivation
is closely tied not only to the curriculum itself, but also to how teachers see their own work
valued by their community.
Beyond external recognition, many teachers also described internal motivation as crucial
to their practice. Teachers emphasised that their enthusiasm and engagement directly
influence student motivation. One teacher explained that when teacher feel ownership
over their teaching and are proud of what they deliver, students are more likely to respond
positively, see appendix DSI5.3. Limited teaching time with a class makes it harder for
teachers to build relationships and motivate students (Wagner, 2021). Therefore, it is not
only important to recognise the effort involved in makerspace teaching, but also to include
the class’s regular teacher in the process as they already have a relation to the students.
For many teachers, the most meaningful motivator is not external reward but internal sat­
isfaction, especially when students are visibly engaged, curious, and appreciative of the
experience. However, teachers are also aware that students rarely express their sat­
isfaction, but only raise their voice if something is not perfect. Therefore, teachers are
focussing on how the students engage in the activities instead as an indicator of their
satisfaction (appendix DSI5.3). Using external teachers can make it difficult for teachers
to feel internal satisfaction as they are not in charge of the teaching.
“For me, it is motivational when I feel ownership of the curriculum and when
my efforts are recognised ­ Eddie Zavallos, design teacher at Copenhagen
International School, see appendix DSI5.3.
In general, teacher motivation is a significant factor for them to be open and willing to
try out new initiatives in their practice, develop their pedagogical approaches, and evolve
as a teacher. The teachers’ attitude towards the curriculum is contagious, so when the
teacher is motivated to try new approaches, so are the students. This increases students
engagement, which in turn reinforces teacher motivation in a positive feedback loop.
57
==PAGE:71==
Key takeaway: Teacher motivation is closely tied to ownership, recognition, and
feeling valued, both by peers and the broader school community. Visibility and
appreciation of their professional efforts foster engagement and openness to new
practices.
Design implication: The solution should highlight and communicate teachers’ ped­
agogical work, support peer recognition, and enable flexible, personalised contribu­
tions that promote ownership.
Criteria for success: Teachers feel their work is visible, respected, and impact­
ful, leading to increased motivation, willingness to share, and pride in curriculum
development.
5.3.2
Balancing Flexibility and Ownership in Curriculum Development
Integrating makerspace teaching in schools requires more than just access to the equip­
ment; it requires thoughtful curriculum design, adaptable materials, and strong profes­
sional collaboration. For teachers to bring maker activities into their classroom in a mean­
ingful way, they must be able to align these activities with subject goals, student needs,
and existing time constraints. However, as both the literature and the interviews reveal,
many teachers struggle with lesson planning due to limited time, inflexible resources, and
lack of opportunities to collaborate meaningfully with colleagues.
This section explores how the practical realities of teaching shape the development of
curriculum for makerspace integration. It examines teachers’ experiences with creating
their own materials, the limitations of adapting pre­made online curricula, and the role
of collaboration in shaping high quality learning experiences. Understanding these dy­
namics is critical for designing solutions that support teachers in ways that respect their
time, professional judgement, and classroom realities to make makerspace integration
possible.
Development and Integration of Teaching Materials in Curriculum
As a teacher, you can get far by defining clear goals and utilising pedagogical teaching
material in the curriculum (T. I. Hansen & Skovmand, 2012). Teaching materials are any­
thing used to represent the content of a subject and can be divided into three categories:
1) didactic teaching materials are developed with a specific teaching purpose, e.g. book
systems, and learning games 2) semantic teaching materials are the content, e.g. texts,
movies, and objects, and 3) functional teaching materials are the tools used to work with
the content, e.g. pen and paper, the blackboard, and computers (T. I. Hansen & Skov­
mand, 2012). Curriculum emerges when teaching materials are placed within a structured
didactic context, aligned with learning goals and pedagogical strategies.
Teachers limited preparation time means that they are not able to develop curriculum from
scratch but have to rely on pre­made curricula and teaching materials when planning their
lessons. However,Ackermann (2011) warns against relying on didactic digital tools and
educational software that merely replicate textbook instruction or gamify drill­based learn­
ing. Experienced teachers have built a personal material bank and can therefore spend
less time preparing lessons or can use their experience to improvise lessons (Böwadt
et al., 2019; Böwadt & Vaaben, 2021). Designing low­preparation activities collected in a
resource bank may help bridge this gap, enabling teachers to focus on facilitation rather
than logistical control (O’Brien et al., 2016). However, it is important that the teaching ma­
terial invites critical thinking to maintain professionalism (Stenersen Hovdenak & Wiese,
58
==PAGE:72==
2017).
In Danish teacher education there is no strong tradition for working systematically with
teaching materials (T. I. Hansen & Skovmand, 2012). This is surprising, considering that
teachers rely on teaching materials in their daily practice. However, there are strong
subject­specific traditions regarding the type of teaching materials used, and teachers
are highly dependent on what they have available (T. I. Hansen & Skovmand, 2012).
All interviewed teachers reported access to pre­made curricula and teaching materials
through various online portals. One teacher explained (appendix DSI5.2), that in public
schools, municipalities decides which portals are available, though teachers can submit
requests or preferences through their subject representative. Although teachers are often
encouraged to utilise online curriculum, many express a lack of ownership and describe
the online curriculum as shallow or disconnected from the classroom (Böwadt et al., 2019;
O’Brien et al., 2016).
In the interviews, teachers report mixed experiences with existing online curriculum. Al­
though some appreciate the accessibility and variety of themes, most teachers rarely use
them directly due to lack of alignment with classroom needs and teaching preferences,
see appendix DSI5.1­5.3. This aligns with the story from curriculum providers, explaining
how teachers usually use curriculum directly the first time to get familiar with the content
and adapting it the following times to fit their needs, see appendix DSI6.1 and DSI6.3.
Curriculum providers are building curriculum with a linear progression to support learning
objectives and are therefore recommending teachers to use them directly but are at the
same time acknowledging that teachers are the experts on what will work best for their
specific class (LIFE Fonden, 2018b). Modifying pre­made content is often described as
time consuming, especially when the structure is too rigid or overly detailed. Teachers
need flexibility in the methods and activities to feel ownership of the curriculum. If the
curriculum is too complete, it does not encourage these modification, that is required to
adapt it to the class (appendix DSI5.3).
“You can not just apply the online curriculum, you have to be very critical, so
you end up spending a lot of time adapting the curriculum to your specific
class” ­ Selma Bulut, pre­service teacher at University College Copenhagen,
see appendix DSI5.1.
Several teachers emphasised the importance of flexibility and ownership in curriculum de­
velopment. They prefer materials that offer a clear structure but leave room for adapting
it to a personal teaching style and students’ needs, see appendix DSI5.1 and DSI5.3.
Teachers express that they are missing opportunities for developing their own teach­
ing materials and professional growth (Böwadt et al., 2019). In recent discussions, pre­
service teacher Selma Bulut (2025) advocates for more elective courses in the teacher
education, specifically with a focus on developing curriculum and teaching material to
prepare teachers for the reality of teaching practice. In a follow­up interview, she men­
tions that the pre­made curriculum is often full of stereotypes and activities are often very
stationary and in front of the computer screen. However, she finds the themes are al­
ways up to date and reflect the current situation in the world, making them more relevant
for students (appendix DSI5.1). Further, she points out that online curriculum is hard to
modify to the needs of your students and has previously called for publishers to include
pre­service teachers in the development of curriculum (Bulut, 2023, 2025).
In the follow­up interview, Selma Bulut explains that she feels curriculum developers lack
insights into the everyday reality of teaching, see appendix DSI5.1. She often finds that
pre­made curricula require heavy adaption to meet her classroom’s needs. For this rea­
59
==PAGE:73==
son she advocates for more direct involvement of teachers in curriculum development
and suggests collaboration with pre­service teachers. However, curriculum developers
offer a contrasting perspective. Almost all employees have a background as a teacher
with multiple years of experience, see appendix DSI6.1 and DSI6.3. Further, in­service
teachers are involved in the development along with the latest didactic research, to make
sure the curriculum follows the evolution of teaching practice (LIFE Fonden, 2018b). At
LIFE, they are involving 4­6 teachers in the development process to give insights and
ideas for the content. The goal is to have teachers from diverse backgrounds that are
extraordinary reflective of their own teaching practice and have good insights into others’,
see appendix DSI6.3. Although this is a small group, and therefore not representative
of all teachers, their involvement helps ensure that curricula reflect classroom realities
and remain practically useful. However, LIFE is often told that they are overly optimistic
about what realistically can be done in a single lesson. Their rationale is: ‘rather too much
than too little content as you can always skip activities’. Engaging the teachers in the de­
velopment will also help build ownership, and they can function as ambassadors for the
curriculum, spreading it to their colleagues (appendix DSI6.3).
Curriculum is typically expected to offer multimodality, accessibility, student activation,
and teacher facilitation guides (T. I. Hansen & Skovmand, 2012). Some teachers prefer
fully developed, ready­to­use curricula with extensive teaching guides, others want to be
inspired and challenged by the material and develop their own curriculum. One teacher
noted in the interviews that sometimes it is necessary to take the easy route by using pre­
made curriculum, but he prefers to modify it, see appendix DSI5.3. He then continued
explaining how planning can feel like a waste of time when unexpected things happen
in the classroom forcing you to diverge from the planned activities. The idea of a “good”
curriculum depends on the teacher, the situation, and the context. As teachers are all
different in their preferences, curriculum developers are often designing for three types of
teachers, the controlling who want to develop their curriculum themselves, the supporting
who wants to be inspired, and the autonomous type who are following pre­made curricula
(T. I. Hansen & Skovmand, 2012).
Despite these efforts, there is still a gap between institutional resources and classroom
realities, especially regarding time management, differentiation, and student engagement.
Teachers express a need for a solution that provides both inspiration and adaptability
without overwhelming them.
Key takeaway: Teachers value online teaching materials as inspiration but often
find them too rigid or shallow. They need adaptable, high­quality resources that
support ownership, critical thinking, and alignment with classroom needs.
Design implication: The solution should offer modular, customisable curriculum
components that can be quickly adapted or used as­is, while also encouraging pro­
fessional growth through flexible structure and pedagogical depth.
Criteria for success: Teachers report that materials are easy to modify, align with
their students’ needs, and save preparation time without sacrificing quality or au­
tonomy.
Enabling Meaningful Teacher Collaboration
As previously described, adapting curriculum based on the class’s needs is a time con­
suming task. In the statistical study conducted by Frederiksen et al. (2017), 43% of new
teachers describe curriculum planning as difficult and the most important factors for set­
60
==PAGE:74==
tling into the teacher role are collective curriculum planning and guidance from colleagues.
When implementing a makerspace in schools, collaborative curriculum planning becomes
important not only for new teachers, but for all teachers, as this involves new teaching
practices, didactic considerations, and technical requirements. Collaborative planning
and co­teaching are not only easing the workload but also enriching the interdisciplinary
potential of maker lessons, as different teachers bring unique perspectives and skill sets
to the table (Hughes et al., 2022).
The empirical data reveal that teachers operate within a tightly constrained environment
where limited time, rigid structures, and curricular requirements heavily influence their
ability to collaborate when planning and adapting teaching activities. Although formal
structures for collaboration exist in most schools, teachers describe this as uneven in both
scope and depth. Collaboration was in some cases described as procedural, focussing
more on ensuring compliance with school policies and not engaging in pedagogical de­
velopment. For instance, one teacher described that they have specific subject teams;
in the maths team, they talk about the status regarding the year plan and if they need
to make adjustments, while in other subjects, they talk about didactic considerations and
share ideas for activities, see appendix DSI5.2. The weekly meetings typically serve to
distribute tasks, align with administrative requirements, and ensure compliance with cur­
riculum frameworks as it includes all teachers in the grade level. However, the actual
development of lesson content and planning activities is most often done individually due
to the burden of coordinating collaborative planning outside the regular meeting time (ap­
pendix DSI5.2).
“It takes a lot of time when you have to plan something together, so it is just
easier to plan it myself” ­ Jonas Baun, teacher at Virum Skole, see appendix
DSI5.2.
When testing the new subject Technology Comprehension in Denmark, the lack of collab­
oration was mentioned as one of the biggest challenges (Wagner, 2021). The teachers
selected for the pilot project all had previous experience with the maker technologies, but
found it difficult to translate it into meaningful curriculum. They were missing a network of
other teachers who had similar challenges to help find solutions and inspire ideas. The
absence of meaningful collaboration left many feeling overwhelmed and isolated. Teacher
collaboration is especially critical given the complexity of maker­based instruction. The
teachers in a study conducted by Hughes et al. (2022) reflected on the challenge of work­
ing alone, noting how much more effective and enjoyable the process became when other
teachers were involved. Teachers often expressed that they lacked the confidence or ca­
pacity to engage fully with maker practices on their own, but that partnerships with more
experienced colleagues “fuelled their knowledge” and helped them build competence over
time (Demirata & Sadik, 2023; Hughes et al., 2022). Role model teachers played an in­
strumental role in this process by guiding peers, facilitating cultural change within their
schools, and enhancing the overall quality of maker­based teaching (Demirata & Sadik,
2023).
Collaboration plays a vital role in easing the burden of curriculum planning, especially
when rooted in pedagogical reflection and shared practice. Teachers see the value in col­
laboration and peer inspiration, but they highlight the difficulty related to coordination and
practical controversies. Despite recognising the potential value of collaboration, some feel
it is not realistically supported by available time in between teaching (appendix DSI5.2).
Multiple teachers expressed interest in a platform or setting where meaningful collabora­
tion could occur, especially if such environments allowed for sharing resources, exchang­
61
==PAGE:75==
