ing curriculum, or co­develop materials. Sharing is a key part of being a maker. It invites
constructive feedback, strengthens local ties, and helps both students and teachers take
pride in their creative processes (Hughes et al., 2022). Teachers who share experiences
and expertise support each other both pedagogically and socially. This strengthens the
maker culture at both classroom and school levels (Demirata & Sadik, 2023). Fostering a
sustainable maker culture in schools depends not only on tools and pedagogy, but also on
strong professional communities rooted in collaboration, mutual support, and shared pur­
pose. Research consistently shows that collaboration among teachers, administrators,
and staff with diverse areas of expertise in STEM significantly strengthens the implemen­
tation of maker education initiatives (Hughes et al., 2022).
Overall, lack of time, flexibility, and shared preparation culture directly impacts how likely
teachers are to experiment with new teaching formats such as makerspace activities.
Even when teachers express curiosity or enthusiasm, they often default to familiar prac­
tices that guarantee control, efficiency, and curriculum coverage. Ultimately, cultivating
a thriving maker culture requires a community mindset. It is not the responsibility of one
teacher alone, but a shared, ongoing effort supported by intentional collaboration, lead­
ership, and sustained engagement at all levels of the school ecosystem.
Key takeaway: Teachers value collaboration for easing planning and building con­
fidence, but face significant controversies due to time constraints and existing meet­
ing structures.
Design implication: The solution should meaningfully support collaborative plan­
ning by enabling inspiration, resource sharing, and co­development of curriculum in
ways that justify the time investment.
Criteria for success: Teachers feel part of a shared community, report reduced
planning burden, and regularly draw inspiration or collaborate with colleagues.
5.3.3
Student Learning Possibilities in Makerspaces
The previous section focused on the professional conditions necessary for curriculum
development and collaboration among teachers. However, it is equally important to un­
derstand how these efforts translate into meaningful learning experiences for students.
Makerspaces offer a rich environment for learning that extends beyond individual skill de­
velopment. These settings support collaborative, social learning where students engage
in shared activities, exchange ideas, and reflect on their creative processes together with
peers and teachers (Demirata & Sadik, 2023). The following section explores the learning
opportunities that emerge from makerspace integration, highlighting how these environ­
ments support student motivation, skill development, and creative expression.
Bridging School Knowledge to Reality through Problem-Based Projects
Within formal education, makerspaces are increasingly recognised as a way to bridge sub­
ject areas and foster authentic, interdisciplinary learning that connects classroom knowl­
edge to real­world relevance (Hughes et al., 2022). Traditional education has been criti­
cised for emphasising decontextualised knowledge, leaving students unable to apply the­
oretical concepts to meaningful situations (O’Brien et al., 2016; Sheffield et al., 2019). In
contrast, maker education emphasises situated and authentic learning, where students
engage with real­life problems and community issues through hands­on creation (Hughes
et al., 2022). Several teachers highlighted how experimentation helps students grasp dif­
ficult concepts and connect theory to reality, see appendix DSI4.3 and DSI4.8. Working
62
==PAGE:76==
with tangible projects helps students connect abstract academic concepts with practical
applications (Harron et al., 2022; Hughes et al., 2022), and deepen their understanding
by situating it within meaningful hands­on experiences (Demirata & Sadik, 2023; Sheffield
et al., 2019). This aligns with Ackermann (2001) notion of cognitive development through
distancing, where students move beyond immediate tasks and begin abstracting, contex­
tualising, and mentally manipulating ideas.
At the core of makerspace learning are problem­based projects, which drive students to
engage deeply with interdisciplinary challenges. Informed by constructionist and inquiry­
driven approaches, maker pedagogies promote curiosity, iteration, and critical thinking
through open­ended tasks that require interdisciplinary knowledge (Ackermann, 2011;
Hughes et al., 2022). Projects rooted in local or social issues promote conversations about
equity and social justice, embedding education in a broader social context (Hughes et al.,
2022). As Ackermann (2011) suggests, today’s students thrive in fast­paced, interactive
environments and makerspaces respond to this by supporting deep, creative engagement
through long­term projects. By encouraging students to examine, design, and produce
solutions to real­world issues, makerspaces cultivate both technical and social­emotional
skills in ways that are difficult to achieve through traditional instruction (Kellam et al.,
2021).
Problem­based projects in makerspaces have the potential to strengthen the connection
between formal education and students’ experiences. As Papert emphasised, construc­
tionist learning environments empower students to create, test, and refine tangible and
personally meaningful artefacts. In this process, students gain disciplinary knowledge,
confidence and agency, while deepening their relationship to both content and commu­
nity (Demirata & Sadik, 2023; Harron et al., 2022).
Key takeaway: Makerspaces connect abstract concepts to real­world applications
through hands­on, problem­based projects.
Design implication: Provide examples and templates for projects that align with
curriculum objectives and tackle authentic, relatable problems.
Criteria for success: Teachers can easily see how maker activities supports
deeper learning and curricular relevance.
STEM and 21st Century Skills in Makerspaces
In elementary education, increasing emphasis is placed on equipping students with the
competencies needed to navigate complex and rapidly changing societies (Ackermann,
2011; Sheffield et al., 2019). Beyond connecting classroom knowledge to real world prob­
lems, makerspaces cultivate the competencies students need to thrive in both academic
and future professional environments. Makerspaces provide a unique context for devel­
oping STEM and 21st century skills through meaningful, problem­based projects. Unlike
traditional instructional environments, these settings encourage students to take initiative,
collaborate with peers, and iterate on ideas, thereby fostering critical skills such as creativ­
ity, problem­solving, and systems thinking. These competencies align with the broader
21st century skills (Hughes et al., 2022).
In makerspaces, students are encouraged to explore ideas, take risks, make mistakes,
and iterate. This process mirrors the innovation cycle central to STEM and nurtures inde­
pendent thinking and divergent approaches to problem­solving (Murai et al., 2023). This
approach fosters not only disciplinary understanding but also creativity and personal ex­
63
==PAGE:77==
pression. Computational thinking, as a part of this process, further supports students to
approach problems logically and iteratively, preparing them for an uncertain and complex
future (Ackermann, 2011) and reinforcing habits aligned with both scientific and design­
based inquiry (Murai et al., 2023).
Makerspaces foster socio­emotional competencies essential for successful collaboration
and inclusive learning. In a study conducted by Sheffield et al. (2019), students reflected
on their acceptance of others’ strengths and weaknesses, highlighting how the social
structure of making supports empathy, teamwork, and shared responsibility. These com­
petencies, often referred to as “soft skills”, are increasingly recognised as essential in
STEM education. At the Big Bang Conference, a presentation on STEM education sug­
gested that diversity within student groups can enhance creativity as each student brings
different perspectives according to their academic abilities, learning style, and interests,
see appendix DSI4.3. The presenters also emphasised that frequently changing group
constellations can further enhance the students’ abilities to collaborate across diverse
peers and accept each other’s differences. Mixed groups create opportunities for all stu­
dents to contribute in different ways and learn from each other’s perspectives.
These competencies are not only recognised in student learning but also in teacher de­
velopment. As Sheffield et al. (2019) observed, pre­service teachers recognise the value
of makerspace environments. They also reported becoming more attuned to the learning
needs of their students, reinforcing the idea that maker pedagogies promote reflection not
just in students but also in teachers. In the school context, makerspaces have proved to
be effective in supporting STEM engagement as well as identity development. The col­
laborative nature offers a sociocultural space for students to see themselves as capable
STEM thinkers (Kellam et al., 2021)
Key takeaway: Makerspaces foster critical 21st century competencies like creativ­
ity, collaboration, and systems thinking through interdisciplinary STEM challenges.
Design implication: Tag activities with the specific skills they develop and include
reflection prompts to help teachers make these skills visible in their teaching.
Criteria for success: Teachers can confidently select activities that address both
disciplinary and interdisciplinary competencies.
Inclusion of Student's Special Needs and Diversity in Makerspace Education
Centring learning activities around hands­on, exploratory, and meaningful projects, mak­
erspaces accommodate varied preferences for tactile, visual, and iterative learning, while
also activating intrinsic motivation through autonomy and creativity (Harron et al., 2022;
Sheffield et al., 2019). Emphasising learning outcomes related to specific subjects can
be difficult for teachers in maker activities (Hughes et al., 2022), as students learn not
by absorbing fixed content, but by constructing their understanding through the design
and production of tangible artefacts. This fosters ownership, confidence, and sustained
interest in students (O’Brien et al., 2016).
Contrary to the perception that today’s students lack focus or depth, Ackermann (2011)
argues that when students are genuinely interested, they are capable of deep and sus­
tained engagement. This immersive learning is common in maker settings, which not only
acknowledge, but celebrate individual learning trajectories, shaped by background, per­
sonality, and interest (Ackermann, 2011). Teachers in the study conducted by O’Brien
et al. (2016) have observed that maker activities appeal to students with a variety of
64
==PAGE:78==
strengths, not only those traditionally successful in academic science or mathematics,
as the creative nature of maker activities can offer an alternative route into complex con­
tent. Similar observations are made in the interviews, where teachers and curriculum
developers reported that students who typically struggle in traditional academic settings
often thrive in hands­on, creative activities, particularly when there is no single right an­
swer and they can express themselves through making (appendix DSI2.1, DSI6.1, and
DSI6.3). This shift in class dynamics can create new participation opportunities, not only
for students with different learning styles and academic abilities, but also for students with
special needs. To further support inclusion and student legitimacy, it is important that all
students are assigned a meaningful role within the activity, ensuring that each individual
can contribute according to their abilities (Nielsen, 2025)
“Our experience with the engineering day is that students who usually struggle
in school get the opportunity to join in their own way, making teaching more
inclusive. Some struggle with not having a right answer, but that is how life is,
so we think it is okay.” ­ David Russel, Education consultant at Engineer the
Future, see appendix 6.1.
Teachers report that the inclusion of students with special needs is one of their most diffi­
cult tasks (Böwadt et al., 2019; Böwadt & Vaaben, 2021). These students often consume
a lot of the teacher’s limited time by disrupting the classroom dynamics. There are too
many children in the classroom, all with different needs (Böwadt et al., 2019) and, in
general, 25% of Danish students in public schools have some kind of learning disorder
(Frederiksen et al., 2017).
In many educational settings, there is a tendency to view maker activities as extensions or
rewards for early finishers and only appropriate for gifted or high­achieving students (Har­
ron et al., 2022). However, this view conflicts with the foundational assumption of maker
education; all students, regardless of prior achievement, can construct knowledge mean­
ingfully through hands­on engagement. Several studies report moments where students
who were not perceived as academically strong surprised teachers with their creativity,
problem­solving, and rapid engagement in maker tasks (Sheffield et al., 2019). Acker­
mann (2001) highlights the importance of underlying beliefs about learning and how it is
shaping pedagogical decisions. If intelligence is seen as fixed, there is a risk of reserving
the learning opportunities for only a select group. Contrarily, if knowledge is viewed as
something actively constructed by each student, as Papert and Piaget proposed, then all
students must be given the chance to engage in the makerspace.
Ensuring inclusion in makerspace education presents several challenges. Teachers em­
phasise the importance of differentiating tasks so that both high­performing and struggling
students remain engaged throughout the process (appendix DSI6.3). A common concern
is that some students may become discouraged in the iterative nature of maker activities,
especially when tasks are too abstract or lack clear checkpoints. Further, the perceived
difficulty of tasks have a high influence on student engagement (Nuutila et al., 2021). High
perceived difficulty can undermine motivation and task performance, while moderate diffi­
culty may sustain engagement. To support inclusion, teachers can adjust success criteria
to match student’s individual prerequisites, ensuring that all students experience a sense
of progress and accomplishment while being encouraged to reflect on their choices and
design decisions (appendix 6.3). For example, while open ended topics allow for creative
freedom, they can make it difficult to apply consistent subject theory across all students.
At the Big Bang conference, a curriculum was presented that covered all of 9th grade
syllabus in mathematics but could successfully be adapted to younger students by adjust­
65
==PAGE:79==
ing theoretical expectations, see appendix DSI4.5. Additional presentations emphasised
that problem­based projects can deeply engage talented students by challenging them in
explaining their reflections and decisions during the process, see appendix DSI4.2 and
DSI4.4.
Recognising this diversity requires teachers to embrace flexible pathways and support stu­
dents moving at different paces. Makerspaces are particularly well­suited to this due to
their inherent openness, which encourages students to shape their own direction within a
project, building on personal strengths, interests, and prior experiences (appendix DSI4.2).
Demirata and Sadik (2023) notes, that when student are given the opportunity to develop
their projects with limited teacher direction, it strengthens the development of student au­
tonomy. This freedom to explore and make decisions enhances motivation and deepens
engagement (O’Brien et al., 2016). By embracing diverse learning styles and offering stu­
dents ownership over their process, makerspaces cultivate a more inclusive, motivating,
and personally meaningful learning experience.
Key takeaway: Makerspaces foster inclusive learning by supporting diverse learn­
ing styles, allowing differentiated participation, and enhancing motivation and au­
tonomy, particularly for students who may struggle in traditional classroom settings.
Design implication:
Offer adaptable, multimodal activity formats with built­in
strategies for differentiation, flexible pacing, and reflective facilitation. Ensure ac­
cessibility for students with special needs and varying academic profiles.
Criteria for success: Teachers can adapt activities to support students with a wide
range of abilities and needs, observe increased engagement and participation, and
feel equipped to create inclusive learning experiences.
Equity and Accessibility of Makerspaces Fosters a Sense of Belonging
While makerspaces offer great potential for, creativity, STEM engagement, and trans­
formative learning, equity remains a critical concern. Ensuring that maker education is
accessible to all students, regardless of gender, race, or socioeconomic status, is essen­
tial if the maker movement is to contribute meaningfully to broader educational and social
goals.
Research highlights that women and girls continue to be under­represented in science
and technology fields due to a combination of structural, cultural, and economic contro­
versies (Sheffield et al., 2019). Makerspaces, with their emphasis on open­ended ex­
ploration and hands­on activities, have the potential to disrupt these patterns. Without a
deliberate focus on equity, makerspaces risk reinforcing existing inequities by dispropor­
tionately benefiting students from privileged backgrounds (Kellam et al., 2021). Studies
have shown that even when makerspaces are physically accessible, their cultures may
reflect dominant norms that make women and other marginalised students feel excluded
or intimidated (Kellam et al., 2021; Sheffield et al., 2019). Despite these challenges, some
female learners reported increased confidence and positive learning outcomes when par­
ticipating in maker activities, suggesting that inclusive pedagogical strategies can mitigate
these controversies when thoughtfully implemented (Kellam et al., 2021).
As Kellam et al. (2021) argue, inclusion in makerspaces must be understood not merely
as physical access, but as a pedagogical practice that invites all students into the learning
process, not just those already confident in STEM. Makerspaces, when aligned with in­
clusive values, can serve as bridges between formal and informal learning, creating new
66
==PAGE:80==
pathways for students who might otherwise be excluded from STEM trajectories. O’Brien
et al. (2016) similarly emphasises the importance of bringing maker education into the
classrooms rather than limiting it to out­of­school programs, which often cater to a nar­
row segment of the population. Inclusive maker cultures begin with teacher preparation.
Teachers must be equipped to recognise bias, foster equity, and design activities that
reflect and respect diverse perspectives and experiences (O’Brien et al., 2016).
Insights from a related special course on inclusion and sense of belonging in makerspaces
conducted as a part of the Gnist initiative further reinforce this perspective (Nielsen, 2025).
Teachers and makerspace staff highlighted that inclusive design is not limited to peda­
gogy, but also encompasses the physical setup and communicative environment of the
space. Key strategies included the use of clear signage, task cards with visual guidance,
and open access to differentiated materials. These adjustments helped students with spe­
cial needs navigate makerspaces independently and foster a greater sense of ownership
and inclusion (Nielsen, 2025).
Key takeaway: Makerspaces must actively address equity and inclusion to avoid
reinforcing existing social disparities. Belonging is fostered not just through access
to equipment, but through culturally responsive pedagogies and inclusive learning
environments.
Design implication: Ensure the tool supports inclusive practices by offering strate­
gies for equitable participation, culturally aware facilitation, and diverse role models.
Teacher guidance should include how to recognise and mitigate bias in maker cul­
ture.
Criteria for success: Teachers feel prepared to support under­represented stu­
dents, students report a sense of belonging and confidence in the makerspace, and
participation reflects the diversity of the classroom.
5.3.4
Teacher Roles, Identity, and Confidence in Makerspaces
Building on the broader teaching context, this section delves into the pedagogical founda­
tions of makerspace education by examinating the development of a maker mindset, how
it affects the teacher’s role as a facilitator of open­ended, student­centred learning environ­
ments, and the importance of building confidence when working with unfamiliar equipment
and methods. As digital fabrication technologies such as 3D printers and laser cutters be­
come more prevalent in schools, they offer new opportunities for teachers and students
to create custom learning tools. However, to make use of these opportunities, teachers
need concrete, practice­oriented examples of how to integrate these technologies in ways
that align both with curriculum goals and with their own professional competencies across
pedagogy, content, technology, and context (Harron et al., 2022; Wagner, 2021).
The Maker Mindset and Identity
Research highlights the importance of teachers having a “maker identity”, as it plays a
role in how the maker pedagogies will be integrated into practice. Hughes et al. (2022)
demonstrate how broadening one’s understanding of what it means to be a “maker” can
open new ways to engage students in making by offering multiple entry points. The study
suggests, that possession of a maker identity not only facilitates the integration, but also
supports the development of meaningful and effective curriculum (Hughes et al., 2022).
This is in contrast to earlier research, which found that personal creativity is not necessarily
linked to becoming an effective maker teacher (Wang & Shan, 2019). Wang and Shan
67
==PAGE:81==
(2019) found, that teacher’s cannot transform their own creativity into teaching practice
to support the development of student’s creativity. Hughes et al. (2022) argues that when
teachers align personally with the core maker values, it becomes easier to integrate it into
pedagogical practice in a coherent and authentic way.
“I’m not one to go to a book or follow a lesson plan and go, ‘oh, okay, I’m going
to use this’. I was never a photocopy guy.” (Hughes et al., 2022)
The culture of making promotes exploration, play, and creativity as pathways to deeper
understanding, while offering opportunities for learners to express and develop their think­
ing across multiple modalities (Kellam et al., 2021). Hughes et al. (2022) identifies three
significant aspects for emerging maker teachers, 1) personal and professional identifica­
tion with maker values, 2) proficiency with interdisciplinary curricula, and 3) a multidimen­
sional maker culture to support teachers’ development. Being a “maker” is not enough
to become a “maker teacher” in itself. The teacher must scaffold inquiry driven learning
opportunities and actively model maker values by embracing failures, curiosity, collabo­
ration, and iterative problem­solving (Hughes et al., 2022).
Key takeaway:
Teachers who develop a personal maker identity are better
equipped to integrate maker pedagogies into teaching.
Design implication: Include reflective elements or prompts that help teachers see
themselves as makers and connect their values to maker practices.
Criteria for success: Teachers describe increased identification with the maker
role and see relevance to their existing teaching identity.
New Role and Competencies for the Maker Teacher
Facilitating teaching in a makerspace requires a redefinition of the teacher’s role, mov­
ing away from traditional instruction­based teaching towards a more student­centred and
exploratory approach, with an emphasis on the learning process rather than the final re­
sult. In the pilot project integrating Technology Comprehension, it has been a challenge for
teachers to find themselves in this new role while also struggling with new subject content,
theory, and technologies (Wagner, 2021). This transition can be particularly challenging
for teachers who are used to being the expert in the room, causing them to feel unpre­
pared (Hughes et al., 2022). What it means to feel prepared as a teacher varies. For
some it involves having pre­made examples to show the students, for others it means an­
ticipating and solving all the potential problems in advance (Harron et al., 2022; O’Brien
et al., 2016).
The idea that the teacher must always have the right answers is increasingly seen as
outdated; instead, the teacher’s role is to learn alongside students and construct knowl­
edge together (O. Rasmussen & Rewers, 2025). In makerspace education, teachers
must embrace a facilitation mindset that fosters inquiry, tolerates ambiguity, and allows
students to be independent and experiment. Teachers will be learning alongside students
and their role is to guide the students accordingly to overcome unpredictable obstacles in
the process (Demirata & Sadik, 2023). In line with constructivist thinkers like Piaget and
Papert, Ackermann (2001) emphasises that learning should be about finding one’s voice,
exchanging ideas with others, and expressing it in relation to the world, rather than simply
internalising others’ knowledge.
While this shift in the teachers role is supported by theory, interviews reveal is also raises
practical implementation concerns, especially around the question of who is responsible
68
==PAGE:82==
for facilitating teaching in the makerspace. At centralised makerspaces, external teachers
typically lead instruction, while the regular teacher is responsible for classroom manage­
ment, see appendix DSI2.1. However, this division of roles can be problematic, if the
regular teacher is not actively engaged in the facilitation, as they are the one who holds
the pedagogical relationship with the students. LIFE Fonden is currently exploring co­
teaching models, where external and regular teachers share facilitation responsibilities,
see appendix DSI6.3. However, unclear roles and expectations can lead to poor coordi­
nation, especially for students with special needs.
Although Shively et al. (2021) suggests having a professional in charge of the mak­
erspace, teachers emphasise that it should be the regular teacher that lead the teach­
ing due to their relation to the students and ability to adapt curriculum to their needs,
while external teachers can support with their technical knowledge (appendix DSI5.4).
This arrangement was generally well received by teachers, who appreciated being able
to observe and learn without the pressure of direct responsibility. However, some also
noted that this model can unintentionally reinforce the idea that makerspace teaching is
“specialist work”, rather than something all teachers can learn to do. While useful as a
stepping stone, this approach does not by itself lead to increased confidence or ownership
unless it is paired with active involvement and reflection. Findings from a related special
course further emphasise that placing full responsibility for planning and facilitating mak­
erspace education on the regular teacher raises the threshold for adoption, as it demands
greater initiative, confidence, and creativity (O. Rasmussen & Rewers, 2025). In the long
term, the goal is for the regular teacher to be in charge of facilitating the integration of the
makerspace in their teaching.
Successfully adopting the new teacher role requires teachers to develop new skills, mind­
sets, and practices across several domains. Implementing makerspaces effectively re­
quires a diverse set of competencies that go well beyond traditional teaching skills. This
includes pedagogical and content knowledge, as well as technological proficiency, adapt­
ability, and a mindset oriented toward collaboration and continuous learning. Demirata
and Sadik (2023) identifies four broad categories for teacher competencies required for
effective makerspace teaching: 1) instructional competencies include the ability to add
authentic content, adapt instruction, and test projects beforehand, 2) social competen­
cies emphasise the importance of building meaningful relationships with diverse students
and collaborating with colleagues to foster a collaborative classroom culture, 3) personal
competencies involve commitment, self­awareness, and serving as a role model, and 4)
technical competencies which require fluency in digital skills such as 3D design, coding,
and electronics. Across all four domains, teachers must be lifelong learners, open to
collaboration with colleagues and students to foster a sense of shared ownership in the
learning environment, and capable of adapting to students’ varied interests and back­
grounds. Teachers should guide, rather than control, the process, allowing students the
freedom to learn through their own experience and failure (Demirata & Sadik, 2023).
In practice, these demands can be difficult to fulfil. Teachers may experience insecurity,
especially in the early stages of adopting makerspace pedagogies. It is therefore impor­
tant, to provide explicit instructional activities to help teachers understand their role in
the makerspace (O’Brien et al., 2016). Ultimately, the effective facilitation of makerspace
teaching depends on a combination of pedagogical adaptability, interpersonal skills, and
a willingness to share ownership of the learning process with the students. Successful
facilitation requires a collaborative mindset, where teachers support students in think­
ing critically, reflecting, and making decisions, rather than providing all the answers (O.
Rasmussen & Rewers, 2025). Teachers who integrate makerspace pedagogies often
69
==PAGE:83==
demonstrate a deep familiarity with the curriculum and can fluidly bridge subjects through
hands­on, design driven projects (Hughes et al., 2022). However, many teachers still feel
uncertain about how to structure the curriculum for maker activities (Demirata & Sadik,
2023).
Key takeaway: Makerspace teaching requires teachers to adopt a facilitative role
and develop a broad set of instructional, technical, social, and personal competen­
cies to not rely in external support. This shift challenges traditional notions of ex­
pertise and can raise the threshold for adoption without proper support, especially
for teachers used to being the expert.
Design implication: The solution should support teachers in gradually adopting
the maker teacher role by offering co­facilitation strategies, reflective activities, and
practical tools for developing facilitation skills. It should foster a collaborative mind­
set, encourage shared ownership with students, and help teachers gain confidence
through guided practice.
Criteria for success: Teachers feel supported in transitioning to a facilitative role,
show increased confidence in guiding open­ended learning, and report growth in
their ability to integrate makerspace practices into the curriculum. External support
is seen as a stepping stone rather than a dependency.
Building Confidence and Supporting Teachers in Adopting Makerspaces
While developing the necessary competencies is essential for facilitating makerspace
teaching, it is equally important to address the confidence and practical conditions that
shape whether teachers feel capable of putting these competencies into action. Access
to makerspace technologies alone is insufficient for ensuring meaningful integration into
teaching practice. Research consistently highlights that teachers’ pedagogical beliefs and
their sense of self­efficacy are central to the adoption of new technologies in the class­
room (Harron et al., 2022). Nuutila et al. (2021) identify perceived difficulty as a distinct
motivational construct that can independently affect engagement. This suggests that even
confident teachers may hesitate to use new maker technologies if it seems overly complex
or ambiguous. Confidence in one’s ability to teach with emerging tools must therefore be
cultivated alongside technical skills and competencies.
Many teachers in the interviews expressed both enthusiasm and hesitation when dis­
cussing makerspace technologies. Although the potential to engage students and en­
hance learning is clear, several participants voiced concerns about safety, technical com­
plexity, and the expectation of being solely responsible for unfamiliar equipment, see ap­
pendix DSI5.2. There is a widespread belief that deep knowledge of the equipment is
required before a teacher can confidently facilitate its use in class by students. Teachers
worry about technical breakdowns, misuse by students, or not being able to troubleshoot
on the spot. One teacher explicitly requested some sort of hotline to be available at all
times, see appendix DSI5.2. Student safety was also raised as a key concern. Teachers
described students as “wild” and unpredictable, expressing their hesitation in letting them
operate expensive or potentially dangerous equipment without close supervision. There
were questions about whether students could be allowed in the makerspace alone, or
if a teacher always have to oversee technical operations, see appendix DSI5.2. These
concerns underline the need for clear safety procedures, supervision protocols, and well­
defined teacher roles when implementing makerspaces in schools.
Allowing the teachers to gain experience with the technology is crucial for successful im­
70
==PAGE:84==
plementation. Getting first hand experience with the challenges that can emerge in maker
activities serves a valuable purpose. Sheffield et al. (2019) found that teachers who ex­
perienced these challenges themselves became more empathetic to student’s difficulties
and was better equipped to develop supportive strategies. Participating in maker activi­
ties as a teacher can enhance self­efficacy, design confidence and sense of belonging in
the makerspace (Kellam et al., 2021). Similar findings was made in the related special
course; experiencing makerspace activities first­hand gave teachers valuable insights into
how students might struggle or succeed, and promoted a more empathetic and respon­
sive approach (Nielsen, 2025). It is therefore important, that teachers gain the founda­
tional knowledge about the equipment, safety procedures, and adopt the maker mindset,
where teachers and students learn together. Teachers need strategies for how to ad­
dress failures as a part of the learning process. Therefore, institutes like CFU are already
offering workshops, where teachers can come to their makerspace and try out the differ­
ent equipment, see appendix DSI6.2. This way, the teachers gain first hand experience,
learn about the possibilities with the technologies, and define strategies for overcoming
challenges. Support structures such as co­teaching models or access to knowledgable
colleagues can further reinforce teacher confidence in facilitating makerspace education.
Building confidence also involves reflecting on ones motivation for teaching. Demirata
and Sadik (2023) notes that lack of clarity around teaching purpose can demotivate teach­
ers and undermine their willingness to embrace new pedagogical and didactic methods.
Therefore, it is important to understand, why teachers have chosen their profession to
build the resilience and openness needed to engage with unfamiliar technologies. Fos­
tering teacher confidence is both a cognitive and emotional process, grounded in experi­
ence, self­reflection, and sustained support from a professional learning community.
Key takeaway: Building confidence in makerspace teaching requires more than
technical skills. It depends on first­hand experience, emotional readiness, and sup­
portive structures.
Design implication: Support teacher confidence through hands­on experiences,
clear safety procedures, and co­teaching opportunities, while encouraging reflection
on teaching motivation and mindset.
Criteria for success: Teachers feel prepared and motivated to facilitate mak­
erspace activities, report reduced anxiety about using unfamiliar tools, and show
increased willingness to experiment and learn alongside students.
5.3.5
Professional Frameworks and Collaborative Environments
As teachers begin to redefine their role and build confidence in adopting makerspace
teaching, they also need professional tools and collaborative environments to support
their continued development.
This section explores the foundational frameworks and
support structures that can help teachers navigate the complex intersection of technol­
ogy, pedagogy, and subject content. First, the TPACK model is introduced as a way of
understanding the knowledge required for meaningful integration of makerspaces in edu­
cation. Following that, the section highlights how professional learning communities and
networks can help sustain this development by fostering shares practices, mutual support,
and build a strong maker culture.
71
==PAGE:85==
The TPACK Framework for Integrating Makerspaces
As makerspace teaching demands a blend of new competencies and confidence, it be­
comes essential to support teachers with tools that help them understand and navigate
these demands. Integrating makerspaces in education requires more than just technical
skills, it requires a holistic understanding of how equipment, pedagogy and content work
together in a classroom context. The Technological Pedagogical and Content Knowledge
(TPACK) framework provides a structured way to understand and support this complex­
ity. It conceptualises the intersecting knowledge domains that teachers need for effective
integration of technology in education (Demirata & Sadik, 2023; Harron et al., 2022). In
this sense, it serves as both a diagnostic tool to identify where teachers may need support
and a development framework for guiding professional growth.
The framework originated from Shulman’s Pedagogical Content Knowledge (PCK), ex­
panded to include technological and contextual dimensions (Demirata & Sadik, 2023;
Harron et al., 2022). This gives four core domains: content knowledge (CK), pedagogical
knowledge (PK), technological knowledge (TK), and contextual knowledge (XK), com­
bined into overlapping competencies as illustrated in Figure 5.6. This integrated view
of teacher knowledge is especially relevant in makerspace education, where curriculum,
equipment selection, and pedagogy are deeply interdependent. The TPACK framework
is important because it helps researchers and teachers conceptualise what knowledge
is needed to integrate technology and makerspaces into their classrooms (Harron et al.,
2022).
Figure 5.6: The TPACK framework outlining the four knowledge domains and the overlapping
competencies (Harron et al., 2022)
72
==PAGE:86==
Studies have found that professional development focused on TPACK leads to more co­
herent planning and richer integration of technologies, especially when teachers are plan­
ning lessons together (Harron et al., 2022). The strength of TPACK lies in its ability to
guide teachers in blending their technological, pedagogical, and content knowledge in a
coherent way. In the context of makerspaces, this helps teachers make informed deci­
sions about tools, teaching methods, and curriculum alignment, strengthening both their
confidence and classroom practice. By drawing on these interconnected domains, teach­
ers can develop a more coherent understanding of their practice and create more effective
learning experiences for students (Demirata & Sadik, 2023).
Key takeaway: Teachers need support in blending technological, pedagogical, con­
tent, and contextual knowledge to design meaningful learning experiences.
Design implication: Structure the solution to support TPACK principles e.g., by
tagging activities with relevant content areas (CK), types of technologies used (TK),
pedagogical approach (PK), and classroom or contextual conditions (XK), to help
teachers identify teaching materials that match their goals and local conditions.
Criteria for success: Teachers can filter and select activities that align with their
own expertise and local teaching context.
Building Professional Maker Communities
Although frameworks like TPACK provide a structured understanding of the knowledge
domains required for effective makerspace integration, this knowledge does not develop in
isolation. It is shaped and strengthened through ongoing collaboration, peer learning, and
shared reflection. Building on the collaborative practices already present in schools and
discussed in section 5.3.2, professional maker communities offer a space for deeper en­
gagement. Here teachers can exchange resources, develop confidence, and participate
in creative, critical dialogue across schools. Such communities are essential for support­
ing the sustained and meaningful integration of maker pedagogies on a national level. In
the pilot project with Technology Comprehension, teachers report a lack of communities
as one of the main challenges. Findings from the pilot project reveals, that establishment
of a strong network as a key factor for teachers engagement in the project, and necessary
to gain confidence with the new subject content and methods (Wagner, 2021).
Establishing and maintaining these communities, however, is not a straightforward task.
In an interview with LIFE Fonden, representatives described their difficulties in foster­
ing a vibrant teacher community, see appendix DSI6.3. They created a Facebook forum
intended as a space for teachers to share experiences and seek support related to cur­
riculum use. Yet, despite having over 1.700 members, engagement has been minimal;
since January 2025, only two teachers have posted questions related to curriculum, each
receiving few responses (LIFE Fonden, 2025). Although the online forum was meant to
facilitate sharing knowledge and experiences, it primarily serves as a channel to promote
new curriculum content. There are only a few examples of teachers engaging in discus­
sions and knowledge sharing, e.g. the best growth condition for seeds.
“It is extremely difficult to keep communities alive” ­ Malene Hjortboe Ander­
sen, consultant in digital didactics at LIFE Fonden, see appendix DSI6.3.
According to LIFE, time constraints are a major barrier for maintaining communities. Teach­
ers tend to only consult the platform when they encounter problems instead of sharing
their entire experience with curriculum (appendix DSI6.3). Typically, it is LIFE Fonden
73
==PAGE:87==
itself that responds with advise or thanking teachers to bring the problems to their atten­
tion. This underlines the importance of asking whether a new community is truly needed,
or whether a solution could tap into existing networks. Both options offer advantages, but
the challenge remains especially relevant for a new initiative like Gnist Education.
Despite these challenges, the value of active professional learning communities remain
clear. Regular participation in professional learning communities supports consistency
in maker practice and allows educators to develop shared curricular approaches, solve
problems together, and give and receive ongoing feedback (Hughes et al., 2022). More­
over, maker communities can extend beyond teachers to include students, families, other
schools, and even companies. When teachers organise exhibitions, competitions, and
public showcases of student work, they help build a broader sense of community own­
ership over the learning process (Demirata & Sadik, 2023). These events give students
a chance to take pride in their work, connect with their surroundings, and engage with
an authentic audience, which is an essential aspect of the maker mindset (Hughes et al.,
2022).
Key takeaway: Professional communities are essential for sustained integration of
maker education, but building active engagement is challenging due to teacher’s
time constraints and unclear incentives.
Design implication: A solution should either tap into existing communities or offer
clear value and low­barrier opportunities for participation, such as shared curricu­
lum, collaborative events, or peer feedback.
Criteria for success: Teachers report gaining inspiration, feeling supported, and
participating in shared dialogue with peers through the community.
5.3.6
Pedagogical and Didactic Considerations in Makerspaces
The integration of digital tools into education opens up exciting possibilities, but also intro­
duces new pedagogical challenges. While digital materials offer new ways to process and
represent knowledge, they can also cause distractions, especially when the technology
itself becomes more engaging than the content or task at hand (T. I. Hansen & Skov­
mand, 2012). The usability of digital tools is therefore contingent not just on access, but
also on teacher competencies, institutional resources, and thoughtful didactic planning.
This tension between possibility and practicality is at the heart of makerspace pedagogy.
Digital natives, according to Ackermann (2011), often move fluidly between ideation and
sharing. This behaviour of sharing before thinking reflects a different kind of learning
logic. In a makerspace context, this opens opportunities for socially driven learning and
collective construction of knowledge, but it also challenges traditional assumptions about
sequencing, structure, and the teacher’s role in guiding reflection.
Makerspaces offer new opportunities for learning, but they also challenge traditional teach­
ing methods. This section discusses didactic and pedagogical considerations that are
essential to support diverse students in these environments, including how to balance
structure and freedom, ensure inclusion, and reconsider assessment and learning goals.
Scaffolding and Structure
One of the central pedagogical tensions in makerspace education lies in balancing stu­
dent autonomy with sufficient structure to support meaningful learning. This structure can
take many forms; for some teachers it means to have a step­by­step guide or providing
74
==PAGE:88==
students with examples, for others it means asking guiding questions to help students
discover subject theory independently (O’Brien et al., 2016). Although maker pedagogies
emphasise open­ended exploration and student­driven inquiry, research highlights that
well­designed scaffolding is essential, especially when students are new to creative and
iterative processes (O’Brien et al., 2016).
To support this, several design process models have been proposed as pedagogical scaf­
folds in the Gnist Curriculum Design Guide developed by Viktor Buch (2025) as part of
his master’s thesis. While these models vary in structure, they share key stages such as
defining problems, idea generation, prototyping, testing, and reflection. When developing
curriculum for makerspace education, it is important to align activities with these stages to
assist teachers in organising content, formulate learning objectives, and develop proper
assessment strategies. Design processes play a critical role in creating progression and
cohesion, ensuring that all activities are intentional and pedagogically grounded. Fur­
thermore, using well­known design models can strengthen interdisciplinary teaching by
creating unity across subjects. The Curriculum Design Guide emphasise that design pro­
cesses are not only procedural but also serve motivational and meta­cognitive functions.
Clearly communicating the stages of the design process helps student stay oriented and
resilient, particularly when encountering failure or uncertainty. Rather than enforcing rigid
templates, teachers can use flexible process models to guide reflection, set intermediate
goals, and reduce ambiguity in open­ended projects (Buch, 2025).
Students are more likely to engage in activities they perceive as manageable and are less
likely to seek help if they believe it exceeds their capabilities (Nuutila et al., 2021). Stu­
dents should therefore be allowed to define their own goals, iteratively shaping what they
want to do and what they need to learn to achieve it (Murai et al., 2023). However, the
absence of instructional guidance can leave students adrift, particularly in school settings
where students are accustomed to more directive teaching models. Early motivational de­
cline can negatively impact students engagement and performance (Nuutila et al., 2021).
Teachers therefore play a crucial role as facilitators, providing structure through mod­
elling, guided questioning, and managing frustration when projects do not go as planned
(O’Brien et al., 2016; Sheffield et al., 2019).
The line between scaffolding and over­structuring can be fine. Murai et al. (2023) found,
that providing students with maker examples can be interpreted as prescriptive rather than
suggestive. This illustrates a core challenge in makerspace pedagogy; too little structure
may lead to confusion or superficial outcomes, while too much can stifle creativity and limit
student agency. Effective scaffolding in makerspaces requires a dynamic and responsive
approach. Teachers must be attuned to students’ needs, stepping in to guide reflection
or reframe failure, while also knowing when to step back to allow students to make their
own discoveries.
75
==PAGE:89==
Key takeaway: Students need freedom to explore but also benefit from structured
guidance, especially when new to making. Design processes can serve as both
pedagogical scaffolds and motivational tools, helping students and teachers navi­
gate open­ended projects, while reflecting on their learning.
Design implication: Provide flexible scaffolding tools, such as guiding questions,
design templates, and reflection prompts. Tie activities to recognisable stages in
the design process to support progression, facilitate interdisciplinary planning, and
build student resilience.
Criteria for success: Teachers feel confident supporting diverse students without
over­directing or constraining creativity. Students understand where they are in the
design process, stay motivated, and reflect meaningfully on their work.
Assessment of Learning Outcomes in Makerspaces
One of the defining pedagogical shifts in maker education is the movement away from
correctness toward creativity, iteration, and open­ended inquiry. In contrast to traditional
classrooms, where outcomes are often predetermined and evaluated against rigid bench­
marks, makerspaces foster a culture in which exploration, experimentation, and failure are
encouraged as essential parts of the learning process. In general, teachers find it difficult
to evaluate the learning outcome of their teaching (Frederiksen et al., 2017). In a mak­
erspace setting, this becomes even more difficult due to the nature of maker activities.
Assessment in makerspace environments challenges traditional notions of evaluation by
emphasising process over product, and formative reflection over summative judgment
(Demirata & Sadik, 2023). Rather than focusing solely on measurable outputs or stan­
dardised rubrics, maker pedagogy invites educators to consider a broader and more nu­
anced understanding of what counts as evidence of learning. As Hughes et al. (2022)
notes, teachers are encouraged to shift their assessment practices toward more flexible
methods, such as student reflections, dialogue, observations, photographs, and record­
ings to better capture the dynamic, iterative processes students engage in. In this ap­
proach, the focus is on students demonstrating their knowledge and learning by applying
it in practice rather than following a textbook (Harron et al., 2022; O’Brien et al., 2016).
This view is echoed by Demirata and Sadik (2023), who emphasises that assessment in
makerspaces should not generate stress or emphasise correctness, but instead focus on
growth and reflection. Students should be guided to understand failure as a natural and
valuable part of learning, not as a mark of deficiency.
“You HAVE to make mistakes ­ they make you smarter. The more mistakes
you’ve made, the more you know. And I know a lot, so you can only imagine
how many mistakes I have made.” ­ Martin Trier Højfeldt, Lab manager at DTU
Skylab in Nielsen (2025)
The iterative nature of maker learning, where failure is not only tolerated but expected,
offers a “safe haven” for students to revisit and refine their ideas. This pedagogical ap­
proach supports resilience and persistence, key factors in both motivation and long­term
learning success (Ackermann, 2011). Kellam et al. (2021) emphasise, when students are
not punished for mistakes, they are more likely to take creative risks, explore alternatives,
and learn deeply through the process of trial and error. Here, teachers play a crucial role
in modelling this mindset. Parallel play, where the teacher also explores the possibilities
of technologies in the makerspace, can encourage students to ask questions and further
explore the possibilities themselves (O’Brien et al., 2016).
76
==PAGE:90==
In many studies, teachers highlighted the need for project­based evaluation to track stu­
dent learning (Demirata & Sadik, 2023; Harron et al., 2022). It can be difficult for teachers
to apply consistent criteria for the assessment, as students often work on individualised
projects (Hughes et al., 2022; Murai et al., 2023). The assessment should take place
through conversations rather than formal tests (O’Brien et al., 2016) as student thoughts
are invisible to the teacher during the design process, adding complexity to the assess­
ment of learning outcomes. This requires teachers to develop sensitivity to indirect indica­
tors of understanding, such as body language, engagement levels, or iterative refinements
(Murai et al., 2023; O’Brien et al., 2016). However, these challenges can also be opportu­
nities. As Hughes et al. (2022) argue, maker pedagogy pushes teachers to broaden their
interpretation of valid demonstrations of learning, whether through physical artefacts, pre­
sentations, or multimodal outputs. This expanded view encourages teachers to recognise
learning as a dynamic, socially constructed process, rather than a static outcome.
These theoretical perspectives were reflected in practical approaches.
Insights from
teacher presentations at the Big Bang Conference offered examples of how assessment
can be approached in maker projects, see appendix DSI4.4. In one case, the teacher
used open­ended questions to prompt student reflection on design decisions and encour­
age the use of theoretical arguments. In interviews, curriculum developers emphasised
the importance of drawing attention to both subject specific competencies and design
considerations during assessment of student’s learning outcome, see appendix DSI6.3.
These examples illustrate how effective assessment in makerspaces requires a rethinking
of traditional evaluation strategies, emphasising process, dialogue, and student agency
in order to capture the complexity of creative learning.
Key takeaway: Assessment in makerspaces challenges traditional evaluation prac­
tices by emphasising process, iteration, and reflection over correctness. Learning in
makerspaces is best assessed through reflection, dialogue, and process­oriented
documentation rather than standardised tests. Teachers need support to identify
learning in open­ended, individualised projects where outcomes are not always vis­
ible.
Design implication: Provide teachers with flexible, formative assessment strate­
gies that promote reflection and dialogue, such as open­ended prompts, observa­
tion cues, and tools for documenting progress and design reasoning. Normalise
failure and creative risk­taking by highlighting learning through iterations.
Criteria for success: Teachers report greater confidence in assessing learning
without rigid rubrics, recognise learning in diverse formats, and observe students
engaging in reflective and persistent problem­solving.
5.4
Findings related to the Business Model and
Implementation Plan
Ideas turned into concepts, turned into projects, and perhaps turned into an actual busi­
ness, will eventually have to formulate a clear business model and implementation plan
in order to not only asses, but also to make a clear frame and mental model for action­
taking. With these in hand, a startup such as Gnist Education will have a much stronger
foundation to build on top of, and hopefully succeed in becoming; first a viable business;
second a sustainable business; and third a potential scalable business. (Teece, 2010).
77
==PAGE:91==
5.4.1
Business model
Engineers, that are trying to use a framework for designing a business model, could be
considered equivalent to a business analyst trying to design and build a technical proto­
type. It is clear to see from a standpoint of pure profession, that both would have it much
easier doing the other ones job. However, in the Design & Innovation program at DTU we
are ever so often trying to bridge the gap of these two generically put professions by doing
user­centric or user­involved design or even sociotechnical design, coupled with the task
of filling out a business model canvas on the side. It makes sense, that design­engineers
should also understand the aspects of business model design and business model in­
novation, as their endeavours in creating outstanding technological solutions will quickly
perish under in the light of a badly articulated business model. On occasion it happens,
that a technology­push product makes it to the market, and sometimes these radical and
disruptive innovation types are the source for great technological paradigm shifts, just like
the petrol engine and the car or the internet and the iPhone or Artificial intelligence. De­
spite that, it is hard to believe, that the inventors of these have not had a particular notion
of the value proposition and the cost­structure. In the article ”Business Model Innovation:
Opportunities and Barriers”(H. Chesbrough, 2010), Henry Chesbrough states ”that tech­
nology by itself does not have any form of objective value until commercialised in some
way via a business model”(H. Chesbrough, 2010), and Teece follows up by stating:
“Technological innovation does not guarantee business success ­ new product
development efforts should be coupled with a business model defining their
’go to market’ and ’capturing value’ strategies.” ­ David J. Teece, Business
Models, Business Strategy and Innovation Teece (2010)p.183
Building on this, it makes sense to first outline the intrinsic parts of the business model as
a concept in order for us to then design a valid proposal of such to Gnist Education.
What is a business model
Linder and Cantrell (2000) argue that: ”When people speak about ’business models’, they
could be speaking about three distinct things: ’components of business models’, ’real op­
erating business models’, and what we call ’change models’” ­ (Linder & Cantrell, 2000).
This points to the mental image of business models has historically up until this point
in time been a somewhat arbitrary concept. Luckily, they elaborate their argument and
state, that ”a business model strictly speaking, is the organisation’s core logic for creating
value”, and a change model is ”the core logic for how a firm will change over time in order
to remain profitable” ­ (Linder & Cantrell, 2000). Moreover, as a part of the ”components of
business model”, they make it evidently more clear, when deconstructing the concept into
seven components of the business model, such as: Pricing Model, Revenue Model, Inter­
net enabled commerce relationships and Value Proposition to name a few. Chesbrough
and Rosenbloom defines their logic in the same time­period to be a set of functions the
business model must fulfil in order to be successful (H. W. Chesbrough, 2003). Today the
distinction between components of the business model and the business model in general
are more prevalent in light of not only Linders and Cantrell dissection of the term but also
after Osterwalder, Pigneur and Tucci’s clarifying paper on the matter from 2005(Oster­
walder et al., 2005). In this review of scientific publications related to business models,
they systematically map the ontological aspects of the business model concepts to the
contributing author, concluding that the authors rarely build on top of each other, and thus
the research moves much slower, than what it could be, up until this point (Osterwalder et
al., 2005). In addition, they also extrapolate the theory, that ”the business model concept
helps externalising, mapping and storing knowledge about the value creation logic of a
78
==PAGE:92==
company”(Osterwalder et al., 2005). Indicating at the business model canvas, which we
already know today. In the same time­period Chesbrough however, still frames business
models subjectively in line with some of the component of the business model outlined
in (Linder & Cantrell, 2000). When analysing Open Source Software Business Models
(H. W. Chesbrough & Appleyard, 2007), Chesbrough uses the business model, when in
fact he describes revenue models.
After years of research on the topic, Osterwalder and Pigneur publishes the handbook
”Business Model Generation” (Osterwalder & Pigneur, 2010), where the classic business
model canvas and its use is described detail. In the book, they pinpoint and define the
business model as ”the rationale of how a venture creates, and captures value” (Oster­
walder et al., 2005). Teece defines it as a model that ”articulates the logic and provides
data and other evidence that demonstrates how a business creates and delivers value
to customers”(Teece, 2010). He continues, and states, that ”it also outlines the archi­
tecture of revenues costs, and profits associated with the business enterprise delivering
that value.” architecture of the value creation”(Teece, 2010). The two are expressing
the same point however Teece does it with more clarity and context. In other words, a
business model articulates what the offerings are, how are they differentiated between
customers, and how and by whom the offerings are paid for. To elaborate on the point,
Teece also remarks what the business model is not. He argues, that a business model
is not a financial projection in spread sheet or computer model, however the business
model can become imbedded in these (Teece, 2010). Teece holds fast in the conceptual
components of the business model like Osterwalder,
Key takeaway: A business model is the structural blueprint linking value proposition
to revenue capture. The business model, although historically uses interchangeably
with components such as ”pricing model” and ”revenue model” are still key elements
of the business model definitions, that are wide used to day.
Design implication: The embedded models, such as pricing models and revenue
models should be taken into account when designing the business model for Gnist
Education.
Criteria for success: The design of the business model can be deemed successful,
when it fulfils the needs of the customer in terms of price and offering and can in
detail outline the activities needed internally to provide the offerings.
5.4.2
Types of Technological Platforms
The concept of the platform within Gnist Education has evolved organically, encompassing
both physical product architecture and digital interfaces. Yet, as the organisation matures,
the need to anchor this notion in strategic and theoretical clarity becomes essential. This
chapter explores how Gnist Education can transition from the dual­platform identity to a
platform­based business ecosystem (Jacobides et al., 2018), drawing on contemporary
literature in strategic management, innovation, and business model design.
Product platforms are the original format of technological platforms in engineering design.
They consist of components with shared architecture and matching interfaces, which en­
ables companies to efficiently develop variants of the same product type having differ­
ent functional outcomes, and are often referred to as families of related products (Ulrich,
1995). (Martin & Ishii, 2002) show how companies structure product platforms to max­
imise reuse and adaptability to enable variety while keeping costs down. In contrast, the
79
==PAGE:93==
‘economics and management perspective’ extends the platform idea beyond the bound­
aries of the company itself. Here, a technological platform is extended to one of two type of
platform, which (Gawer & Cusumano, 2014) refer to as external platforms in comparison
to the internal platforms, also described by (Gawer & Cusumano, 2014) that corresponds
to the product platforms of engineering design. The external platform type incorporates
products, services, or technologies, that act as a base to which the owner of the platform
open up to external innovators, so they can build their product, service or technologies
on top (Gawer & Cusumano, 2014).
This categorisation of technological platforms or industry platforms (Gawer & Cusumano,
2014) helps with clarifying, that platforms can be understood as two types which are in­
herently basing their differences on a shared common idea, which is encompassed in the
notion of ’industry platforms’ (Gawer & Cusumano, 2014). (Jacobides et al., 2018) and
(Panico & Cennamo, 2022) emphasise further, that platforms are embedded in platform
ecosystems, networks of complementors and users that co­create value under shared
rules. The platform owner sets the rules of participation, through a set of standards and
policies (Parker et al., 2017) and designs the common interface of the platform and pric­
ing structures tailored and maybe differentiated between the user and the complementor
(Gawer & Cusumano, 2014). As a result, this also determines how open or closed the
platform is, and how easy it is for others to join the ecosystem(Gawer & Cusumano, 2014).
Digital Platforms
A digital platform is a type of external platform (Rochet & Tirole, 2003), that connects
two or more user groups (e.g. teachers, parents and students skoleintra.dk, or 3d­print
designers and makers makerworld.com via an interoperable online interface. This essen­
tially creates the either two­sided market, as mentioned in the last section, or if more than
two, a multi­sided market, also known as multi­sided platform (Rochet & Tirole, 2003).
An inherent problem exist within the platform business model, as it relies on the users
finding it at attracting to consume the goods of the producers, and the producers finding
it equally attracting to produce the goods the users consume. According to (Caillaud &
Jullien, 2003), intermediation service providers, which platform owners essentially are,
must solve the classic “chicken­and­egg problem” by simultaneously attracting both pro­
ducers and consumers to enable network effects and positive feedback loops (Gawer &
Cusumano, 2014). They argue, that ”efficient market structures emerge in equilibrium”,
and that platform owners should have the motivation to offer both sides non­exclusive
services as this facilitates competition and allows the owners to exercise power over the
market. (Rochet & Tirole, 2003) address this notion of the ’chicken­and­egg problem”, and
analyse different pricing strategies to mitigate the risk associated with the multi­sided plat­
forms, including digital internet based platforms. They come to the conclusion, that with
effective price subsidisation of the parties on the platform based on the decomposition
of the parties and their offering, can combat the problem. They emphasise that platform
pricing is not a single price but a structure across sides, and that one side may be treated
as a strategic loss to boost adoption on the other side (Caillaud & Jullien, 2003).
As mentioned Platforms make use of what is called network effects, that are either direct
or indirect. Usually a platform make use of indirect network effects, that is when attraction
to the platform happens as more users join the platform(Panico & Cennamo, 2022). Indi­
rect network effects are an essential part of platform business model (Caillaud & Jullien,
2003). Caillaud and Jullien (2003) note that platform ecosystems create more value as
the number of complementors grows, however (Panico & Cennamo, 2022) warn, that re­
turns decline if scale outweighs quality of products or services. Thus, design of a platform
80
==PAGE:94==
business model must foster both quantity and quality of participants (Panico & Cennamo,
2022). In the beginning of a platform’s life, (Parker et al., 2017) argues, that indirect
network effects among complementors (external developers) are crucial in overcoming
”chicken­and­egg problem” and onboarding consumers. Attracting attention to the plat­
form from not only the consumer side, but also the complementor side is paramount for a
successful platform launch.
Key takeaway: Digital platforms serve as matchmakers between multiple sides
and can leverage network effects. Strategic partnerships on both sides, can have
a great impact on how successful the launch of the platform will be. Furthermore,
a proper setup can amplify the value for all participants as the ecosystem grows
Pricing and governance are two­sided or multi­sided, and subsidising one group to
grow the other, is how the overall platform might cover their costs.
Design implication: If a platform business model is to be developed, it should have
clear stated incentives. As an example, allow easy content creation and sharing (to
attract complementors) and simple user onboarding. Pricing should be strategically
included in the business model to attract both sides of the platform. Moreover,
standards and access controls should be governed closely to maintain trust and
platform quality among the platform parties.
Criteria for success: If a platform business model is to be developed, it should
reveal clear differences in operations, cost­structure and revenue model in order to
be deemed a success.
5.4.3
Ecosystems and Value Co-Creation
Platforms can exist and operate within a broader ecosystem of independent and interde­
pendent actors such as suppliers, complementors, users, or institutions (Jacobides et al.,
2018). An Ecosystem is a especially valuable to consider, when one; parts to a product
or service cannot be assembled without coordination across producers or adherence to a
standard system, and two; the consumption of joint parts creates more value, than con­
sumption of separate parts (Jacobides et al., 2018). For a platform, it can be important
to consider the entire ecosystem of which it operates within, in order for it to mitigate the
risk of being compromised by by­passing complementors who co­create with other and
sell directly to the customer(Wirtz, 2024). By allowing for ecosystems to happen both
products or services can enhance their value by the means of co­creation across the en­
tire value­chain (Wirtz, 2024). For this to happen, rules of engagement with the entire
ecosystem is critical(Jacobides et al., 2018).
The co­creative nature of ecosystems reflects service­dominant logic, where value arises
through interaction rather than unilateral delivery (Vargo & Lusch, 2008). A teacher might
adapt a curriculum module and share feedback, while a complementor modifies a hard­
ware or curriculum kit together with another complementor, thereby enhancing what the
platform makes possible. The platform becomes a space for ongoing collaborative ex­
change, not a closed product pipeline. Chesbrough’s open innovation framework(H. W.
Chesbrough, 2003) re­inforces the service dominant logic which suggests that companies
must combine external and internal paths to market, and balance open contribution with
governance mechanisms ((H. W. Chesbrough & Appleyard, 2007); (Parker & Van Alstyne,
2018)).
As a final note, a platform can also be integrated into an existing ecosystem. (Björkdahl
et al., 2024) explores the challenges of this platform adoption, and denotes it as platform
81
==PAGE:95==
grafting. A platform designed to be specifically integrated into an existing ecosystem such
as the healthcare system or school system can poses significant challenges (Björkdahl et
al., 2024). (Björkdahl et al., 2024) proposes the framework of dynamic capabilities(Teece,
2017) as an assessment tool to guide the managerial process of platform grafting.
Key takeaway: Ecosystems are enablers of value co­creation across the supply­
chain. Platforms do not necessarily need to adhere to an ecosystem in order to
succeed, if they support both access and motivation to the complimentors. How­
ever, as a platform integrating into an existing ecosystem, challenges to integration
are significant, and managing these is paramount for a successful implementation.
Design implication: The business model, if also implementing the notion of it’s
own potential ecosystem should nurture recurring collaboration, and design digital
interfaces, workflows for collaboration, resulting in moving actors from onboarding
to ongoing participation.
Criteria for success: If the solution designed for business model and implemen­
tation plan shall be viewed as a success in relation to ecosystems and value co­
creation. They should integrate the notion of the barriers and opportunities related
to the potential self­created ecosystem and the ecosystem in which the business
enters.
5.4.4
Cost and Pricing Models
Cost and pricing strategies are key mechanisms of any sustainable business model whether
the business is dealing with a one­sided or multi­sided model. The pricing of goods needs
to follow the value, of what it delivers, but more so, it also needs to match the competitors
and lastly it needs to cover costs of the produced goods (Helmold, 2022). These three
parameters makes up the pillars of the 3C Pricing model(Helmold, 2022), which Helmond
explains as a strategic pricing framework. The aim of the use of the model, is to bring
more value to the customer, than their competitors, at a lower cost (Helmold, 2022). For
the customer, the perceived value of the good one is trying to sell is crucial to understand,
in order to estimate the right price. They will look at your product at make a perceived
judgement, of where it scores in relation to competitors and substitutors(Helmold, 2022).
(Helmold, 2022) denotes, that no demand exist above this value or price. For the Com­
petitor, an analysis of their products, brand and prices in relation to your product, brand
and price is equally important to know. The information the competitors data can creates
the right price logics (Helmold, 2022). Moreover, the cost is again equally as important
as the other two. Costs are the companies expenses going into the design, creation and
delivery of the product or service. (Helmold, 2022) denotes, that, there is no profit below
the price of total cost. Understanding these three is essentiel for a company to name
the right price, and different pricing strategies can position the company relative to it’s
competitors in the market, and as a result frames the perceived value of the companies
product (Helmold, 2022).
There are essentially three different models to which pricing can be determined, and they
all directly link­up to the 3C Pricing model. Traditionally, pricing can be based on the
margin needed to cover the manufacturing cost of the developed goods, this is referred to
as cost­based pricing (Helmold, 2022). Then there is value­based pricing. Value­based
pricing is a pricing strategy relying mostly if not fully on the perceived value of the product
(Helmold, 2022). An example of this could be fashion, where prices follows the emotions
the product evokes (Helmold, 2022). Then their is competition­based pricing (Lucidity,
82
==PAGE:96==
2025), and although Helmond does not directly mention this it is still imbedded in his 3C
model. Competition­ or competitive­based pricing is a pricing strategy, that a company
can use, if they want to position themselves strategically compared to it’s competitors,
and thus assert it’s perceived value with it’s customer (Lucidity, 2025). Both cost­based
and competitive­based pricing are easier to evaluate, why many companies use one or a
combination of both to price their goods (Hinterhuber, 2008).
In relation to platforms, the pricing mechanism adds complexity. As noted, platforms of­
ten subsidise one side to grow the other (Rochet & Tirole, 2003). (Panico & Cennamo,
2022) models this intrinsic pricing­strategy by the use of mathematical formulas, that de­
scribes the complimenters and users relation to the price. As a result, it shows how the
platform must balance user prices and complementor prices. The company determines
first the price strategy and the price of the complementors’ goods are determined by the
complementors thereafter in relation to the overall value the platform creates (Panico &
Cennamo, 2022).
Key takeaway: Price is a hard measure to define, however in a platform business
model, the price strategy is determined by the company and price is determined
by the complementors in relation to the overall value and platform dynamics, and
not just production costs. In practice, this means adopting value­based pricing one
the complementor side and competitive­based pricing on the other. Over all cost,
should of course be covered.
Design implication: For the design of the business model, the pricing mechanisms
are complex as we just found out. However, considering a mix of value­based pric­
ing and competitive­based pricing could lead to a better solution overall.
Criteria for success: The success criteria for the design of the business model in
relation to the pricing strategy, would be to consider prices of competitors on the
user side and value­based pricing on the complementor side. However, as it is
not within the scope of this project to investigate the complementor side in depth,
the prices related to these would be an estimate, if a platform business model is
designed.
5.4.5
Implementation Plan
Legal Framework of Danish Primary schools
The primary education system in Denmark can largely be divided into two institutional
segments: public schools (folkeskoler) and private schools (frie grundskoler). The pub­
lic schools are closely governed by the municipalities they are a part of, and more su­
perficially watched over by the Danish government, who brings forward new ideas and
initiatives to strengthen the public schools in general nationwide. Public schools are un­
der the jurisdiction of the Folkeskoleloven (Børne­ og Undervisningsministeriet, 2024),
which defines municipal ownership and local governing bodies, obligated curriculum, and
requirements to teachers and other school personnel.
The private school as a term extends across multiple different smaller sub categories,
ranging from immigrant schools to progressive free schools (Børne­ og Undervisningsmin­
isteriet, 2025a). The two most common types of private schools are the smaller private
schools (friskoler) and the large private schools (private schools. These schools are self­
owned institutions with more autonomous rigour when deciding on the curriculum and
other school activities in comparison to the municipality owned public schools. Broadly
83
==PAGE:97==
