speaking, they can decide on their own agenda as long as it complies with the overall gov­
erning body for private schools, the (Børne­ og Undervisningsministeriet, 2019).’Friskoleloven’
is the common framework for all self­owned primary schools outside the municipal sector.
The private schools are permitted to adhere to this legal framework, in order to secure
educational quality for every school child, and for them to have the same prerequisites
in their continued education. Stå­mål med kravene (Børne­ og Undervisningsministeriet,
2019) is described in part 2 of the first paragraph in ’Friskoleloven’. This legislation is the
benchmark­principle for all self­owned schools, to secure quality alignment with the public
school.

Economic Structure and Funding Logic
Public and private school operate under two structurally different financing models. Public
schools are financed indirectly through a state­to­municipality block grant system (Under­
visningsministeriet, 2008). The municipality receives a disbursement from the state to
cover all cost in the municipality, which also includes primary public education (Under­
visningsministeriet, 2008). There are no direct per­student subsidies budgeting. How­
ever, instead the budgets are managed centrally by the municipal education department,
which allocate funds across schools according to local priorities, staffing needs, and in­
frastructure plans, however, with respect to the strategic and legislative framework of the
Danish Government. This financing model grants freedom to the municipality to spend
their money as they see fit. However, municipality, that is overburdened by its resident’s
socio­economic status might find it especially difficult to allocate these resources, why
some students might fall short depending on the municipality they live in .
Private schools’ decision making process are localised and led by boards and school
leaders. This enables procurement processes to faster In contrast to the public schools,
the private schools receive direct operational support from the Danish state through the
taximeter system. Subsidies are based on full­time student equivalents (årselever) and
allocated through activity­specific channels.

The private schools are disbursed each year on a monthly rate based on the schools stu­
dent enrolment data prior to the 5th of September (Børne­ og Undervisningsministeriet,
2019). Subsidies aim to cover approximately 76% (Folketinget, 2022) of the average op­
erational cost per student calculated from the public school expenditure data three years
prior with the complementary inflation adjustment. The remaining 24% is financed pri­
vately, typically via tuition fees from parents, but also on singular occasions by the mu­
nicipality (Folketinget, 2022). These numbers are confirmed with the school Principal of
Gladsaxe Privatskole, (see appendix PS13.1 ).
Public schools fall under the legal domain of ’Udbudsloven’ (Udbudsloven Senere aen­
dringer til forskriften, n.d.) and related EU directives (Udbudsloven Senere aendringer
til forskriften, n.d.). This implies, that purchases above statutory thresholds require ten­
dering from the decision­making level above that said threshold. Larger procurement
decisions are centrally managed by municipal education departments or procurement of­
fices that cuts across different municipal departments. The procurement framework is
described in (Brix, 2024), which outlines the tiered system of where vendors need to sub­
mit a request, in order for them to be taken into consideration. agreements. This also
denotes, that the standardised budget allocations to the public school restricts their flexi­
bility to make impulsive decision on the individual school level, see Table 5.4.
In comparison, the private schools’ procurement process is also subject to ’Udbudsloven’
(Udbudsloven Senere aendringer til forskriften, n.d.), however it is the same thresholds
that applies to the public schools, which is only applicable above the statutory threshold,
or unless it is specific earmarked funds or EU grants (Udbudsloven Senere aendringer til
forskriften, n.d.). The decisions are made intermittently by the school board and principal,
and strategic investments are negotiated case­by­case, often with vendors directly (Brix,
2024). At smaller rates, the chances of being considered as a potential vendor is higher,
and as it is mentioned in Brix (2024), private institution can be strategical and clever
gateway into the public market, because if it works there on a day to day basis, it often
times also works in the equivalent public institutions.

In regards to VAT exposure, public schools are VAT neutral in accordance with ’Mom­
sloven’ (Skatteministeriet, 2019), which permits them to pay VAT from VAT­liable sources.
However, because they are under municipal administration all purchases can partially be
recovered in accordance to ’Bekendtgørelse om momsrefusionsordning for kommuner
og regioner’ (Indenrigs­ og Sundhedsministeriet, 2025) channels afterwards, just like any
other VAT required company would. As a result, VAT does not affect net­procurement
costs, and thus pricing sensitivity is relatively low. The selection of a specific vendor
however will depend on overall policy alignment and the value of the offering.
Private schools are also VAT neutral entities (Skatteministeriet, 2019), however, because
the school is self­owned, the school cannot recover the VAT from purchases at VAT liable
companies. Thus, VAT is treated as a ’real, unrecoverable cost, and applies to everything
that is VAT neutral like themselves, such as other educational course/service providers
(Skatteministeriet, 2019). Without VAT recovery mechanisms, total price including VAT
must be absorbed by the school’s budget (see Table 5.5).

The differences can significantly influence procurement decisions. Whereas public schools
evaluate based on function and regulatory fit, private schools weigh offerings against the
value of competitors substitutes . In combination the procurement regulation and VAT
treatment defines the strategic boundaries for each school. For vendors and decision­
makers alike, recognising and adapting to these divergent logics is essential to sustain­
able engagement and educational innovation in Denmark’s schooling landscape.
Key takeaway: The legal, financial, and procurement framework of public and pri­
vate schools in Denmark are institutionally distinct. A business model and imple­
mentation strategy that treat them as a single market, risks misalignment with how
schools actually fund and adopt services. Furthermore, Full compliance to legal,
ethical and procurement requirements should also be considered.
Design implication: The implementation plan, should consider the procurement
process and legal framework of both school types. However, the private schools will
most likely enable faster traction, and be able to validate Gnist Education’s value­
proposition quicker. In parallel or right after, the implementation plan can add in the
public­sector, when the offering is ready for a larger scale integration.
Criteria for success: The business model and implementation plan can be deemed
successful, when the business model enable adoption across both school types, but
also shows, that focusing on the private school sector is primary focus to begin with,
adopting a price sensitive approach. First then, when credibility is established, could
this translate into trust to secure the inclusion of the public schools.
The Role of School Management
A new service or product is not adopted, just because it is exist. It must align with the
schools core values. Principals are tasked with interpreting what a new approach means
in relation to staff identity, community expectations, and broader educational discourse.
School leaders function as translators who manage multiple logics and relational dynam­
ics. Their capacity to guide innovation depends on their ability to build trust, sustain narra­
tive coherence, and create conditions in which change is not only feasible but meaningful
In public schools, the school management operate within frameworks shaped by national
curriculum guidelines, collective agreements, and procurement procedures defined by lo­
cal authorities(Børne­ og Undervisningsministeriet, 2024). These structures provide ad­
ministrative clarity and legal protection, yet they often restrict the extent to which lead­
ers can respond flexibly or quickly to pedagogical opportunities. The 2013 reform, for
instance, granted school leaders greater discretion in allocating teacher working hours,
but simultaneously introduced intensified accountability mechanisms rooted in goal­based
management (Storgaard & Frederiksen, 2023).
In private schools, the overall school management must maintain alignment with the
87
==PAGE:101==
school’s educational philosophy, which may be grounded in models such as Montes­
sori, Steiner, or democratic traditions (Børne­ og Undervisningsministeriet, 2025a). In
addition, they must remain responsive to parental expectations and school­board gover­
nance structures. As Piil (n.d.) explains, leaders in these environments often act working
with available resources to solve complex problems in ways that uphold both the school’s
pedagogical identity and its public reputation.
Leadership is enacted not only through formal decision­making but also through rela­
tional practice. Transformational leadership approaches that involve motivational framing,
individual support, and collaborative reflection have demonstrated success in facilitating
teacher engagement with change. Laursen and Weiss (2025) report that transformational
leadership significantly improved teachers’ use of digital platforms, especially when lead­
ers were able to narrate the change in a way that made pedagogical sense. This finding
indicates that leadership influence is built on interpretive coherence rather than formal
authority alone.
Key takeaway: The role of the school principles is to make sure, that the school
operates within the legal requirements, but also, that the schools actors aligns with
overall the overall values. Moreover, the principal can also be an important actor in
change making situations.
Design implication: The design of the implementation strategy, should take direct
account of the school principal. The principal has decision­making power, especially
in private schools, why securing alignment will be paramount.
Criteria for success: The implementation plan is successful, when it addresses
the importance of the school principal, and the power the position poses.
The role of Parents
In public schools, parental influence is more but still significant. The parental engagement
is shaped by municipal inclusion policies and broader community expectations (Skole og
Forældre, 2015). In an introductory to a report from the Copenhagen municipality Køben­
havns Kommune (2019), the challenges, that arises in the collaboration between the par­
ents and schools are schools. Here, they state general challenges, that the school often
points out. The misalignment on the ’parents own role in relation to the child in school’,
their ’responsibility­taking and natural curiosity to the child in school’, and ’lingnuistic and
cultural differences i the overall parent group (Københavns Kommune, 2019). Parents
view their on own role primarily from their own point of view, and responsibility and legit­
imacy are negotiated. These spaces are marked by tension and negotiation, reflecting
broader societal narratives about identity, parenting, and institutional trust. These inter­
actions carry emotional weight and reflect broader social and institutional tensions.
In private schools, parental involvement often reflects distinct value systems ranging from
religious to progressive or alternative pedagogies. Parents may act as co­governors,
shaping curricular openness and steering decision­making. Kristoffersson (2023) notes
that democratic governance structures can produce tension, as teachers encounter ide­
ologically driven parental input that may conflict with pedagogical priorities.
Recent efforts, such as the VELUX FONDEN’s Parent Communities initiative, have aimed
to make collaboration more inclusive by designing engagement models sensitive to cul­
tural diversity and avoiding deficit assumptions (VELUX FONDEN, 2020). Such interven­
tions recognise that parental participation requires contextual grounding.
88
==PAGE:102==
Not all innovations are embraced. Parents may reject tools perceived as overly digital,
impersonal, or mismatched with children’s needs. Ludeke et al. (2021) found that child
outcomes are shaped by parental expectations and early relational scaffolding innovation
without this support may lack uptake.
Karlson and Landersø (2024) document a decline in educational mobility in Denmark, with
parental background increasingly determining student outcomes. This raises concerns
about whether innovation promotes equity or risks reinforcing existing disparities. In public
schools, pluralistic expectations must be navigated; in private schools, coherence with the
parental ethos often guides acceptance.
Effective innovation involves parents as pedagogical contributors, not passive stakehold­
ers. It requires attention to local narratives, responsiveness to informal dynamics, and
recognition of non­school actors as educators. Dannesboe (2023) notes a trend toward
institutionalising parenthood through learning­centered agendas, where families support
learning beyond school hours. This shift reflects distributed pedagogical responsibility
and underscores the importance of social embedding.
Key takeaway: The engagement from parents in public schools are less significant
than that of the private school parents. Public school parents exert influence on the
school based on their world­view and social alignment with the school, which are
often more problematic, rather than constructive. Private school parents are more
invested in the school practices, but also have more to say. They have neatly picked
the school, its practices and values, which aligns with their world­view making it less
problematic but instead more constructive. Some parents can also be an inherent
part of the decision making with a seat at the school board, taking decisive actions,
on how the school operates to assert the best possible outcome for their children.
Design implication: The parents as an actor in the broader context and on the
development arena should be kept informed and engaged when needed. Strategi­
cally inform the parents early on in collaboration with the school management may
be more important to begin with rather than later on in the process, where an im­
plementation can seem out­right inferior to their world­view and believe system. On
public school it is more important to align with the strategical position of the munici­
pality rather than the parents, however parents can be an obligatory passage point
or gatekeepter in both the public and private sector if engaged with strategically at
the right time.
Criteria for success: Parents should be engaged with early on before eventual pi­
lot studies in the private school context and later in the public school context, where
demonstrations to the parents outside school boundaries can build an informal re­
lation to the concept where no imperative strings are attached. This could work in
general for the up­take among parents.
The Role of the Industry and Private Funders
Lego Education by extension of LEGO Group , offers building set for educational purpose
to schools through traditional third party vendors aimed at schools. The teachers and
students can engage in the learning activities after the acquisition of the LEGO sets, and
find appropriate material on their web­based learning platform. Such activities are also
referred to as Public­Private Partnerships (PPP, 3P, or P3), and in 2018 Carlsen (2022)
describes, based on her research, that PPP’s first must be understood as ”forms of human
mutual engagement”, implying that contracts can be formulated on the basis of personal
89
==PAGE:103==
familiarisation, becoming what Carlsen (2022) denotes as familiar strangers. Partnership
agreements like these can eschew the over all procurement process to the public sector
(as explored earlier), instead of being founded on an objective rationale.
LEGO also builds partnerships to other institutions of the educational sector. As part of
LEGO ecosystem, the LEGO foundation. has since 2018 been partnering with every Uni­
versity College in Denmark in the project ”Playful learning”, extending their reach to the
pre­service teacher world. Such practices has been widely critiqued in public media in
recent years and is still a hot topic in he world of scientific scholars. Since Carlsen (2022)
study on private­public partnerships, a shift in perspective have taken place, and are now
not only directing their focus at the private companies themselves but also their affili­
ated private funding organisations. This is expressed by P. Rasmussen (2022), where
he underscores the principal private companies and funds are increasingly taking over
operations, that were formerly undertaken by the public sector. Furthermore he expands
the field of educational research to include four main type of actors; ’Public authorities’,
’funders’, ’producers’, and ’users’. These actors implies, that they are important charac­
ters to have in mind, when trying to understand the intertwined mechanisms the Danish
education system and it’s relation to funds. Rasmussen elaborates on the four types of ac­
tors, and explain their internal relationship. For the actor ’Funders’, Rasmussen clarifies,
that the educational research is funded through public institutions, and private founda­
tions established by private businesses P. Rasmussen (2022). Moreover, he denotes,
that Co­funding is widespread, and that private funders can be part of extensive public­
private research programs P. Rasmussen (2022). The private foundations are thus an
inherit part of the greater public research field, and can some time participate in public
educational research, if it aligns with strategic interest of their founding businesses. In
the article Rasmussen indicates, that private funders play an increasingly more centric
role in education research and especially on the topic of primary schoolsP. Rasmussen
(2022). Rasmussen points at the private foundations of big corporations such as Novo
Nordisk, LEGO and Velux, and their involvement in procuring for future STEM­oriented
professionals, which he notes also aligns with national policy P. Rasmussen (2022).
In a news­article brought to the public in January 2024 by DR (Danish Broadcasting Corpo­
ration)(Danish Broadcasting Corporation, 2024) School leaders and students expresses
a notion of fear that private funds will be to big of a part in the public schools’ economy.
The news­article states how school renovation, trips with over night stay, books and play­
grounds are increasingly dealt with through private funding. Dorte Andreasen, (former)
vice principal of ’Skolelederforeningen’ is in the news­article expressing her concern, and
says:
”I do worry about a possible inequality. Some schools will be better at prepar­
ing applications for the funds than others,” ­ Dorte Andreasen, (former) vice
principal of ’Skolelederforeningen’(Danish Broadcasting Corporation, 2024)
This aligns with our speculations and assumption, that when Villum Fonden closed the
bid for makerspace funding at the end of 2023, and had ”only” managed to procure mak­
erspaces to one­third of every Danish Municipality, there were no mentioning of the other
two­thirds. Some municipalities probably already had makerspaces or Fablabs at schools,
some might not have found them relevant, and some might not have had the right capac­
ity to prepare an application. This however is still to this point only speculations, and will
need a more thorough investigation to make any conclusive remark on this point.
In conclusion, from Rasmussen P. Rasmussen (2022), we get the indication, the public
authorities, universities and individual research institutions excelling in educational re­
90
==PAGE:104==
search, are broadly speaking shaping the research field on education in collaboration,
and that not users are not only teachers, pre­service.
Key takeaway: The role of the industry and private funders play an increasingly
more important role, in the Danish education system. The funders originating from
big corporations such as Novo Nordisk or LEGO deposit assets into their private
funds to support the educational bandwidth. The actors with most influence shape
and expand their reach across all systems level of the educational arena, making
them both translators in schools and in the politcal framework.
Design implication: The design and development of a business model and imple­
mentation plan will take a note of their existence, and regard them as key actors on
the arena, with a considerable amount of influence. As a small player, it could be
wise to play along rather than trying to disrupt.
Criteria for success: The success criteria for this aspect will focus on how well the
insights from these actors are incorporated into the business model. They are first
of all competitors or substitutors, that are heavily invested in the market already.
Their prices are almost not to compete with, and the product they offer is top tier.
5.5
New potential controversies, negotiations and obligatory
passage points
Across all three focus areas, potential controversies, negotiations and obligatory passage
points for implementing a mobile makerspace at schools have emerged. Despite the
growing enthusiasm for makerspaces, there is a range of practical, pedagogical, and
systematic controversies that needs to be solved for successful integration. Harron et al.
(2022) suggests, that all schools should have some fabrication machines and materials
available to support the ongoing digitalisation of the world, ensuring all students become
critical of technology in line with the new subject Technology Comprehension in the Danish
elementary education.
Drawing from systems engineering, this sections outlines the main potential controversies,
negotiations and obligatory passage points at the three system levels: artefact, complex,
and engineering. Understanding how these levels interact is key to design a holistic so­
lution that is feasible, desirable, and sustainable.
5.5.1
Artefact System Level
At the artefact level, controversies for implementing makerspaces at schools come from
the everyday realities of teaching practice, including teacher’s confidence, classroom dy­
namics, and how students and teachers interact with open­ended learning environments.
Furthermore, technical challenges such as equipment availability, maintenance and func­
tionality can lead to limitations in the overall usability of the makerspace, and negatively
impact the learning opportunities.
A key barrier is teachers’ lack of confidence in their pedagogical and technical skills
needed to facilitate maker activities (Harron et al., 2022). Many feel unprepared to guide
students through open­ended, interdisciplinary tasks involving digital fabrication tools such
as 3D printers or laser cutters. Even when teachers express enthusiasm for maker peda­
gogy, the perceived workload and lack of familiarity with equipment lead to hesitation and
reduced willingness to experiment (Harron et al., 2022; Wagner, 2021). For students,
gaps in technical or conceptual understanding can hinder engagement which requires
91
==PAGE:105==
more instructional scaffolding than some teachers feel able to provide. Teachers note,
that students often do not possess the technical or computational background needed to
engage deeply with maker­based activities (Harron et al., 2022).
Compared to 3D printing, laser cutting is perceived to have higher technical controver­
sies for both teachers and students. Additional technical challenges with equipment can
likewise, jeopardise the outcome of meaningful learning opportunities, as bottlenecks and
slow equipment can dampen the excitement for teachers and students (Welbourn, 2019).
This increases the need for specialised training and and new ways of providing user­
friendly and flexible systems and equipment. Participants also noted that limited instruc­
tion time and shared access to equipment constrain meaningful integration into teaching
(Harron et al., 2022). Additional technical challenges with equipment efficiency, ability
and reliability
Makerspaces often promote excitement and high engagement among students, but this
can also challenge classroom management. Teachers report difficulty maintaining struc­
ture in student­driven environments, especially when being the sole teacher in the class­
room (Sheffield et al., 2019). Teachers do not feel comfortable being responsible for
expensive equipment, especially in open environments where supervision is difficult (ap­
pendix DSI5.2). This concern is closely tied to student safety and are questioning whether
student can work independently in such environments which can cause challenges for the
flow in class. This open­ended and collaborative nature of maker activities therefore de­
mands a shift in both teaching style, classroom culture and considerations regarding the
spatial layout of the makerspace.
Assessment practices also pose a challenges at the artefact level. Traditional evalua­
tion methods are often not suited for the process­oriented iterative nature of maker­based
learning. Teachers struggle to make student thinking visible during the design process
and to assess learning that emerge through experimentation and failures. This increases
the demand for formative, reflective assessment approaches that many teachers are not
accustomed to using. The lack of clear, easy­to­use methods for evaluating open­ended
projects can undermine teachers’ confidence and limit their ability to recognise learning
outcomes in a makerspace context. Parallel play and dialogue­based assessment strate­
gies help foster a more exploratory classroom culture, encouraging students to take cre­
ative risks and embrace failures as a part of the learning process (Hughes et al., 2022;
O’Brien et al., 2016)
Despite these challenges, there are also meaningful opportunities in makerspaces. When
teachers are supported in building confidence and competence, they report increased
motivation and ownership over their practice. This support can be through collaboration
with colleagues, professional recognition, or specific strategies for facilitating teaching. In
addition, the implementation of a diverse range of flexible equipment can further support
meaningful learning activities that can be aligned with teachers competencies. Smart
ways of improving equipment usability, can allow for new opportunities as industry­level
tools and equipment can easier be reflected in a school setting. The spatial layout of
the makerspace should likewise be considered, ensuring the creation of a welcoming
environment that support students and teachers sense of belonging, while allowing for
an optimised usability flow. The business model will have to consider these opportunities
and make us of them as levers.
To fully leverage the opportunities of the artefact level, implementation efforts should focus
on empowering teachers and students with practical and flexible tools and equipment that
align with their everyday reality. Supporting professional identity development, promot­
92
==PAGE:106==
ing reflective methods, managing time constraints, and providing inspirational resources
can help bridge the gap between maker pedagogy and classroom practice. Confidence
and motivation grow when teachers feel respected, prepared, and supported in their pro­
fessional development. In consequence, the value of the offering targeting the schools’
primary users, i.e. students and the teacher, should be very carious of getting this right. It
is paramount, that the teachers and students, not only see the benefits of all the positives
of makerspace­led teaching and learning, but are also equipped to the task. Students
will most likely be very excited to begin with, but what will happen in the long run? Look­
ing at existing solutions and their current utilisation rate might, the problem could seem
to extend further from our initial assumptions, which could reflect poorly on the continu­
ous use of makerspaces in general. The business model should look into, how the value
proposition is most convincingly delivered to the these users not just in the short term,
but especially long term. Fostering their continuos engagement with the makerspace and
maker­teaching can be considered as an ’obligatory passage point’ for the business model
to figure out.
5.5.2
Complex System Level
At the complex system level, the implementation of makerspaces is shaped by broader
institutional, curricular, and administrative structures. A recurring barrier is the misalign­
ment between the open­ended, interdisciplinary nature of maker education and the rigid
framework of school systems (appendix DSI6.3). Standardised curricula and national
testing often leave little room for exploratory or project­based learning formats (Harron
et al., 2022; Hughes et al., 2022). Here, limited preparation time is a major constraint
for teachers, especially for developing teaching material and planning curriculum outside
standard requirements. Overloaded schedules and frequent procedural meetings further
limit teachers’ capacity to engage in creative planning or professional development (ap­
pendix DSI5.2).
A key barrier is the perceived lack of support for teachers. Many feel isolated in the effort
to adopt maker pedagogy, with minimal support from schools and few opportunities for
professional development (Hughes et al., 2022). This isolation reflects deeper systemic
issues, such as organisational inertia and the absence of shares implementation strate­
gies. Without defined collaboration structures, teachers are often left to facilitate teaching
alone, making it difficult to manage open­ended activities, support diverse student needs,
and supervise equipment use simultaneously (Harron et al., 2022). In addition, maker
activities are sometimes faced with equipment bottlenecks, when consumer­grade equip­
ment is utilised, as they have not been designed to be used for large classroom activities
(Fontichiaro, 2020). Suggestions for co­teaching models or external support are rarely
formalised into school planning, leaving many teachers to navigate these challenges in­
dependently (appendix DSI5.2 and DSI6.3).
In many cases, school­based maker initiatives are driven by individual teachers rather
than supported systemically. This lack of institutional backing limits the scalability and
sustainability of makerspace integration. Teachers frequently report the absence of clear
policies, insufficient administrative support, and few opportunities for collaboration as ma­
jor constraints (Hughes et al., 2022). Without formal recognition or leadership support,
experimentation with new pedagogical formats becomes an individual risk rather than
a shared priority. Studies also highlighted that there are a broader structural resistance
within education systems, which continues to prioritise predictability and performance over
open­ended creative approaches (Harron et al., 2022).
93
==PAGE:107==
However, opportunities at the complex system level also exists. Some schools are be­
ginning to recognise the value of makerspaces in promoting deeper engagement and
transversal skills. When implementation is supported through thoughtful scheduling, dis­
tributed responsibility, and recognition of teacher efforts, schools can better integrate
maker pedagogy into their broader educational strategies. Structured professional devel­
opment programs, where teachers engage in maker activities themselves, offer a valu­
able opportunity for building confidence, community and pedagogical alignment (DSI4.1).
These formats help teachers anticipate classroom challenges, experience the shift into
the maker mindset, and reduce the perceived risks of experimentation. Institutional sup­
port and alignment of priorities for formative assessment, interdisciplinary planning, and
co­teaching models can help embed makerspaces more sustainably within existing school
structures.
5.5.3
Engineering System Level
At the engineering level, a major challenge is the cost and complexity of acquiring and
maintaining fabrication technologies such as 3D printers and laser cutters. These tools re­
quire not only financial investment but also careful planning for installation, maintenance,
and safe operation (Harron et al., 2022). Constructing a makerspace requires adequate
ventilation, spatial separation, and adherence to safety regulations, all of which demand
planning and investment that many schools find difficult to accommodate.
Furthermore, adhering to specific rules in different municipalities, can limit the overall use
and activities offered in the makerspace. This can lead to variability in how the mak­
erspace should be designed and operated throughout Denmark, as certain initiatives can
conflict with local educational preferences and needs.
While these controversies are significant, they also highlight opportunities for design solu­
tions that reduce complexity and support safe scalable integration. Mobile makerspaces
that are pre­configured for safety, ease of use, and adaptability can help address some
of these engineering level constraints. Providing shared access models, modular setup,
and embedded guidance systems may lower entry controversies and make advanced
tools more feasible for everyday school use.
5.6
Updated Development Arena (As-Is)
Based on the findings from DS­I, the As­Is development arena has been revised to re­
flect a more nuanced understanding of the current makerspace situation, as illustrated in
Figure 5.7. The full scale model can be found in appendix J­DA3. Compares to the initial
version, the updated model incorporated newly identified actors, clarified responsibilities,
and refined interactions across system levels. Notably, several areas have been shaded
to indicate the narrowing of the scope moving into the development phase. Actor net­
works have been expanded, showing a clearer mapping of interdependencies, especially
between school­level actors and external support structures. These updates provide a
stronger empirical foundation for defining targeted interventions in the To­Be development
arenas.
94
==PAGE:108==
Figure 5.7: Updated As­Is development arena based on the findings from DS­I. The full scale
model can be seen in appendix J­DA3
5.7
Updated Displaced Development Arena (To-be)
Following the insights gained in DS­I, the To­Be development arena has been refined to
reflect a more realistic and evidence­based vision for the future implementation of mak­
erspaces in Danish elementary schools, see Figure 5.8. The full scale model can be
found in appendix J­DA4. Compared to the initial assumption­based model, the updated
version incorporates clearer depiction of actor roles, decision­making hierarchies, and the
necessary support structures across system levels.
Several areas that were previously assumed to be accessible are now shaded to illustrate
the narrowing of the scope. In particular, the updated arena highlights more nuanced in­
teractions between schools and external actors, especially regarding funding, implemen­
tation logistics, and pedagogical support. The Gnist Ecosystem remains central but is
now more tightly integrated with realistic actor networks and feedback loops based on
actual stakeholder input.
95
==PAGE:109==
Figure 5.8: Updated To­Be displaced arena based on the findings from DS­I. The full scale model
can be seen in appendix J­DA4
This updated model provides a more grounded foundation for the development of targeted
solutions and implementation strategies and ensures that future interventions are aligned
with both user needs and systemic considerations.
5.8
Design Brief
Based on the insights through DS­I, a number of design implications have been identified,
that influence a schools decision on integrating a makerspace. These serve as an outline
for the development of viable solutions, covering both the physical space, empowering
teachers, and a viable business proposal. The initial problem statement have been up­
dated to a design brief statement, which will be used to develop the mobile makerspace
concept for Gnist Education in the Prescriptive Study.
Design Brief Statement:
How might we improve the current makerspace situation at schools by providing a
full solution package, including:
• A physical environment that accommodate diverse learning opportunities, by
offering effective and user­friendly makerspace equipment.
• Preparing teachers to take on the task of facilitating makerspace activities and
integrate it in their practice.
• A business model and implementation plan targeting the schools, that cur­
rently have no access to a makerspace.
5.8.1

This specification forms the foundation for the upcoming Prescriptive Study, where con­
cepts and prototypes will be developed and tested. The outlined requirements will serve
as guiding principles to ensure that design decisions remain aligned with the actual con­
98
==PAGE:112==
ditions, preferences, and constraints of reality. By addressing both systemic challenges
and individual needs, the solutions aim to make makerspace education more accessible,
sustainable, and professionally meaningful.
99
==PAGE:113==
6
Prescriptive Study
The Prescriptive Study builds directly on the insights identified in Descriptive Study I. While
DS­I explored barriers, motivations, and needs related to the integration of makerspaces
in schools, the purpose of PS is to develop solutions that respond to these findings. The
design specification presented at the end of DS­I serves as a bridge between analysis and
intervention, offering both a methodological and conceptual foundation for the subsequent
development process.
Overview of Methods and Activities
In this phase, the following methods and activities were applied throughout the develop­
ment process to ensure that the proposed solution align with practical needs.
• Co­creation workshops
• Concept development and evaluation
• Prototype testing with users
• Iterative Prototyping
• Soft Systems Methodology
Research Questions Covered in this Phase
RQ2: How might a detailed design of the Gnist Education concept be developed to
integrate the makerspace in a Danish school setting?
100
==PAGE:114==
6.1
Approach of Methods
To explore how a detailed design of the Gnist Education concept might be developed
to integrate the makerspace in a Danish school setting (RQ2), certain methods and ap­
proaches were used. This include the development of multiple prototype iterations based
on the requirements and criteria identified in the design specification. Co­creation and
user testing were furthermore used to refine the development based on user insights.
This ensured that the concept was developed to reflect the school setting, and allow for
a relevant MVP test in DS­II.
6.1.1
Build-Measure-Learn and Iterative Prototyping
Build­Measure­Learn will throughout the Prescriptive Study serve as a structural frame­
work for the development of a MVP for the Gnist Education concept. It will be supported
by continuous iterations to refine the MVP. These iterations will be created in accordance
with the UX lifecycle model, where multiple iterations will be developed and evaluated on
observations and user test to gather relevant insights based on user feedback.
6.1.2
Co-creation Workshops
Through the prescriptive phase, several co­creation workshops have been conducted.
The focus of these have been to gather insights from the users regarding the development
of solution. Each workshop is specifically designed based on the context, purpose, and
involved participants, as outlined in Table 6.1. Each workshop approach and outcome will
be described further within the development context.
