6.1.4
Testing Sessions with Students
During the development process, a series of test sessions have been made with students
to gain insights in how the makerspace can fit into a teaching context, focussing on the
physical layout of the space, development of teaching materials, and investigating the
value it can bring from a business perspective. The approach in each session is de­
scribed in the following sections. The insights from the tests will be described later in the
development of each of the three focus areas, where it will be put into context.
4th grade Lundtofte Skole (part 1) - Perceived Difficulty and Innovation Game
The first testing session is conducted with a 4th grade class from Lundtofte Skole, who
are invited to DTU Skylab to participate in the initial use of the Gnist Makerspace. At
the time, the makerspace had not yet been fully equipped, and a laser cutter had to be
temporarily borrowed for the test. Due to space limitations inside the container, the class
is split in two groups, half in the makerspace and half in a classroom setting. After lunch,
the groups will switch, so all students go through both activities. The classroom activities
are developed and facilitated in collaboration with Nielsen (2025), and the makerspace
activities are handled by Dau et al. (2025). This section will describe the approach of
the classroom activities, while the makerspace activities will be described in the next. All
details of the test structure can be found in appendix PS1.1.
Research suggests that students’ willingness to engage in learning activities is influenced
as much by their perceived competence as by the actual difficult of the task (Nuutila et
102
==PAGE:116==
al., 2021). When working with unfamiliar tools or concepts, initial motivation may be high
due to novelty, but students may also hesitate to engage if they assume the task exceeds
their abilities. To explore this, students were presented with four artefacts representing
different technologies and each linked to a school subject:
• A small copper wire sculpture with LEDs soldered to it. This can be linked to teaching
electrical circuits in science.
• A tangram puzzle made with a laser cutter, which can help build a foundational
understanding of geometry in mathematics.
• A robot that could be programmed to move in a specific way. This can be used to
teach programming and computational thinking as a part of Technology Compre­
hension.
• A 3D printed elastic powered car which can be used to demonstrate energy transfers
and motion in physics.
Each artefact is briefly introduced along with an explanation of how it is made and why
it would be relevant in a school setting. Students are then asked to assess how difficult
they believed it would be to create each artefact themselves, using coloured post­it notes,
and indicate which artefact they would most like to try in class. This evaluation is carried
out with two separate groups of students, one before participating in the makerspace
activity and one after. The aim is to examine whether hands­on experience with maker
technologies would influence student’s perception of difficulty.
To further explore student’s sense of belonging in a maker environment, they are in­
troduced to a hands­on activity in the Innovation Game with the purpose of assessing
whether a short creative task can foster students’ confidence and identification with in­
novation practices. Students first receive a brief introduction to innovation, emphasising
that innovation does not always require groundbreaking inventions but can involve cre­
ative combinations of existing technologies to solve problems. Then, working in pairs,
students are randomly assigned two technologies and asked to imagine a new inven­
tion that combined them. They sketch their ideas and build low­fidelity prototypes using
cardboard, sticks, modelling clay, and other simple materials. At the end of the session,
each group presents their invention to the class. Before and after the activity, students
are asked whether they could imagine themselves working with innovation or becoming
inventors.
4th grade Lundtofte Skole (part 2) - Can It Fly
The second part of the testing session with students from Lundtofte Skole focussed on
exploring how students navigate and engage with a structures learning experience inside
the Gnist Makerspace container. The activities is developed and facilitated by Dau et al.
(2025) as a part of their bachelor thesis. The primary aim of the test is to examine how
students participate in and flow through a multi­station setup. Particular attention is given
to identify potential bottlenecks in student movement and the role of thematic staging
elements in enhancing student engagement. The testing session is structured around
five sequential activity stations:
1. Design your wings
Students begin by sketching and outlining wing designs on paper using simple tools
such as markers and rulers. The station is intentionally designed to resemble a
traditional classroom setting, helping to ease students into the process with familiar
materials and expectations.
103
==PAGE:117==
2. Cut your wings
The student’s drawings are then digitalised and prepared for laser cutting. This is
the only step to take place inside the container, where interior have been decorated
to simulate realistic workshop environment, including mock 3D printers, posters, and
examples of finished planes.
3. Decorate your plane
At this station, students can find materials to personalise their design. This activity
also functions as a buffer to manage waiting times for the laser cutter.
4. Assemble your plane
Students assembles their planes from pre­cut parts and adds their personalised
wings. Stations three and four can be used interchangeably depending on which
station is available, allowing flexibility in flow of students.
5. Test your plane
Finally, students test their planes’ flight distance and experiment with different throw­
ing techniques or weight distributions.
5th grade Gladsaxe Privatskole - Introduction to 3D Print and Modelling
This testing session is conducted with 5th grade from Gladsaxe Privatskole over four
lessons and is held in their usual classroom at the school. All details of the test structure
can be found in appendix PS2.1. The purpose of the session is to explore students’ per­
ception of 3D printing technologies, find a benchmark of learning possibilities for students,
and gain insights into students’ abilities in using 3D modelling software.
This is the first meeting with this student group, and therefore the session will begin with
a short introduction to the project and the team. Then, a questionnaire is given to the
children as a benchmark of their understanding of makerspaces. This is given to the
students before any explanations to avoid bias. To ensure all students understand each
question, they are given an answering sheet without questions. By only showing the
questions on the classroom screen, all students have to pay attention and go in the same
pace. After the initial questionnaire, an explanation of makerspaces is given to ensure all
students have the same understanding, regardless of their answers in the questionnaire.
Examples of initial questions are listed below, and the full questionnaire can be found in
appendix PS2.1 page 2.
• Do you know what a makerspace is?
• Have you ever been in a makerspace before?
• Would you like to try to visit and work in a makerspace?
To explore the discourses around makerspace equipment and what they find most inter­
esting about it, an additional questionnaire will be given to the students. Students have
to consider if they are most interested in how the equipment works, what they can do, or
why they are used. Then, students are introduced to three different kinds of 3D printers
to capture their initial reaction to the equipment. Students will be allowed to take a closer
look at the printers before having a class discussion about each printer. Field notes will
be taken by an observer to capture students’ comments and behaviour when observing
the machines and during the class discussion. Then, each student will choose which type
of printer they like the most and explain their choice. The students will choose a printer
by physically moving to it, to ensure all students are participating.
After lunch, the students will be introduced to TinkerCAD where they have to complete
104
==PAGE:118==
selected tutorials to learn how to use the software. The chosen tutorial will take students
through all the basic functions in TinkerCAD. This specific tutorial is a combination of
smaller ones and are explaining everything you need to know to use the software, covering
theory on 3D modelling, and giving small exercises on how to navigate the camera, modify
objects size and shape, and use the alignment tool.
If they finish early, they will be allowed to make a free model. This will give valuable
insight into students’ engagement and skill level, thereby helping to define the appropriate
difficulty level of modelling tasks for beginners.
To evaluate potential shifts in the students’ interests, understanding, or motivation towards
makerspace activities, the initial questionnaire will be administered again in the end of the
session. It will also serve to conclude the session and provide an opportunity for students
to give feedback or any final comments.
7th grade Gladsaxe Privatskole - Design Your Own Game Piece
This testing session is a full day with 7th grade from Gladsaxe Privatskole. The class is
invited to Skylab, where they will use the Gnist Makerspace. This is the first test in the
container, after it has been fully equipped with 3D printers and a laser cutter. Students
will make a small 3D model and then print it in the container. All details of the session can
be found in appendix PS3.1.
The purpose of this testing session is to get insights regarding the scaffolding require­
ments in a design process, investigate which type of printers and software that gives the
best results, and explore how this exercise might also serve to increase teacher confi­
dent in facilitating maker­based activities. To do this, the teacher was asked to participate
alongside the students and reflect on how the activity could function as a part of the pro­
fessional development course.
First, the students are shortly introduced to the project before they are presented with the
task of designing and modelling their own game piece. The game piece should represent
each student to make a more personal connection to the task, and requirements are set
for the dimensions to ensure short printing times. To support students’ independent work,
a short and concise tutorial has been selected. This tutorial is optional for the students
to complete, but can help them get started if they feel insecure about using the software.
Further, the students will be shown where they can find additional tutorials as help, or they
can simply start designing their game piece in a learning­by­doing process. All the game
pieces will be printed during the lunch break, so the students can bring them home at the
end of the day.
Throughout the session, observations will focus on the students’ need for support and their
level of engagement throughout the design process. During the lunch break, the teacher
will be interviewed about her reflections on the activity’s relevance and usefulness within
the context of the professional development course. At the end of the session, a short
reflection round will be held to gather students’ feedback and impressions.
6.2
Dividing the full Gnist concept into focus areas
As identified through DS­I, several factors are at play when a school have to decide
whether to implement a makerspace. In the design brief, 3 distinctive solutions have
been defined for the full Gnist Makerspace concept. The next sessions will focus on de­
veloping solutions for each of these aspects. All three processes have been conducted in
parallel, and the main responsibility have been distributed between the group members.
105
==PAGE:119==
6.3
Development of an Optimised Mobile Makerspace to fit
Classroom-Suited Learning Opportunities
This section explores the challenges related to fitting classroom­suited learning into a
makerspace and proposes certain design choices and solution that can optimise the over­
all user experience in a mobile makerspace. DS­I revealed that even though makerspaces
can support the students learning opportunities, certain barriers and technical challenges
can hinder certain aspects of integrating the makerspace in a school setting. To address
these challenges a mobile makerspace will be developed in accordance with the require­
ments and criteria identified in the design specification, so that a MVP can be build and
measured in DS­II.
6.3.1
Identified Gaps and Starting Point for the Detailed Makerspace
Development
Key themes across both literature and empirical data include the importance of balancing
learning outcomes, efficiency, and accessibility. Multiple barriers and challenges can be
seen in existing makerspaces, which negatively impact the integration of makerspace ac­
tivities and learning opportunities in a school setting. To overcome these barriers, equip­
ment choice, technical challenges, knowledge requirements, and overall flexibility should
be considered when developing a makerspace.
The design specification developed at the end of DS­I outlined clear requirements and cri­
teria for designing a successful and effective mobile makerspace. The key requirements
and criteria include the integration of a diverse set of user­friendly and efficient equipment
that can foster progressive skill development. Additionally, streamlined administrative ac­
cess is to be considered in the integration of the makerspace to ensure that certain stan­
dards and guideline are followed. Moreover, as the Gnist Education concept is centered
around a mobile container, the equipment used in the makerspace should be suitable for
transportation, and support the reconfigurable interior design.
To address these challenges the physical makerspace will be developed based on a pre­
viously created concept, by Gnist Education, of a mobile makerspace situated inside a
container as seen in Figure 6.1. This concept was created before the start of this project,
meaning that certain design choices was already considered. These included a modular
wall mount system, ventilation, and general aesthetics such as acoustic panels, and LED
lighting.
Figure 6.1: CAD model showing the conceptualised idea for the Gnist Education makerspace
container (From previous work).
106
==PAGE:120==
However, as this was a conceptualised idea, meant that a lot of design challenges were
still missing before it could be converted into a detailed and classroom ready makerspace.
Therefore, the starting point for this project, were to develop a detailed design of a mobile
makerspace based on the design requirements identified in DS­I. The following sections
will give a thorough description of each aspect in the development of the detailed mobile
makerspace and design choices for each developed solution.
6.3.2
Equipment and Software Selection
As revealed in DS­I, the choice of equipment in a makerspace setting is a critical design
decision that can affect not only the learning outcome, but also the accessibility of dif­
ferent activities. Choosing the right equipment, should therefore be carefully considered,
based on the requirements and criteria identified in the design specification. Throughout
this section, the selection of equipment and associated software will be described, and
analysed, to find the best suited tools for the makerspace.
Makerspace Equipment Selection
As a makerspace cannot thrive using only one type of equipment, as identified in DS­I, a
wide range of equipment were acquired to support a broad list of makerspace activities.
An equipment survey (appendix DSI1.1) was used along with a theme workshop (ap­
pendix DS1.2) to determine the first set of equipment. These decisions were furthermore,
backed up with observations made when visiting two well established makerspaces, see
appendix DSI6.2 and DSI2. The following list show the selection of equipment and why
they were chosen:
• 3D printers: The 3D printing technology is widely considered to be one of the most
popular makerspace equipment, as it support a wide range of makerspace activi­
ties. The equipment survey showed that teachers are more excited to work with 3D
printers than any other type of equipment. This high demand for 3D printers, means
that it will be a great addition to the makerspace.
• Laser cutter: The laser cutter technology is also a well established equipment used
throughout various makerspaces, and is still in high demand when asking teachers.
Furthermore, the laser cutter can be used with various materials, reflecting a broad
and flexible use case that support a wide range of learning opportunities. The laser
cutter will therefore be a great addition to the makerspace.
• Vinyl cutter: Although vinyl cutters do not have the same user demand and wow­
factor as 3D printers or laser cutters, it was still found to be one of the most used
equipments at established makerspaces. As the learning curve, and required skill
level is relatively low compared to 3D printers and laser cutter, vinyl cutters can
be used by a broader user­group allowing younger students to experience what a
makerspace has to offer.
• Heat press: The heat press was chosen as an extension of the vinyl cutter, as they
are typically used together. The vinyl cutter and heat press are often used together
in a makerspace as they share many of the use cases such as fabric press. The
heat press, like the vinyl cutter, can also be used quite easily as it does not require
a high technical skill level, and is therefore a great addition to the makerspace.
Common for all of the chosen equipment is that they all reflect industry­level manufac­
turing processes, which allow students to develop 21st­century skills through hands­on
experience. These four pieces of equipment can for that reason be used in a broad range
of activities, ensuring a flexible makerspace that can adapt to the needs of its users.
107
==PAGE:121==
Focus on 3D Printers
Although a wide range of equipment have been selected to be included in the makerspace,
this project will generally be focused on 3D printers for a number of reasons. First of all,
3D printers, as stated earlier, are among some of the most popular equipment in a mak­
erspace and continue to be in high demand from both students and teachers. Further­
more, it allows for a wide range of learning opportunities, which align well with the creation
and exploration of various curriculum possibilities, which will be discussed in later sec­
tions. However, as revealed in DS­I the 3D printers face many barriers and challenges in
a makerspace setting and risk creating bottlenecks and general technical issues. In ad­
dition, 3D printers are shown in many cases to be slow and unsuited for classroom use,
it will therefore be interesting to see if the usability of this equipment can be improved.
This will allow for a makerspace that is both efficient, and can meet the demand from both
students and teachers.
Another reason for focusing on 3D printers, is that, findings from the literature, and em­
pirical observations, show that most other equipment such as laser cutters, vinyl cutters,
and heat press, typically works without many technical challenges or efficiency issues. In
other words, other type of equipment do not show as many areas of improvements as 3D
printers. This is also reflected in the user test Can It Fly (subsubsection 6.1.4) with 4th
grade from Lundstofte Skole done by Dau et al. (2025), which show and validate the inte­
gration of laser cutters in the mobile makerspace, see appendix PS1.4. The four chosen
pieces of equipment described earlier will still be used in the development of the mak­
erspace, but most of the design choices and solutions will revolve around the 3D printers,
regarding its usability, efficiency, and how to improve upon these aspects.
3D Printer Selection
Choosing the right 3D printer is an important design decision as it can have implications
on overall usability, flexibility, and efficiency in the makerspace. Visits to established mak­
erspaces revealed that most 3D printers used in a makerspace setting are either manu­
factured by Bambu Lab or Prusa, see appendix DSI6.2 and DSI2. A comparison between
three printers (two from Bambu Lab and one from Prusa) was created in order to find the
specific printers that should be integrated in the mobile makerspace. This comparison
was created to assess the printers according to design criteria identified in the design
specification, as seen in Table 6.3, ensuring the most suitable printers will be acquired.
Table 6.3: Comparison table of the three 3D printers that is being considered based on identified
criteria from Design Specification.
Bambu Lab: A1
mini
Bambu Lab:
P1S
Prusa: MK4
C1
Cost Efficiency
C1
User­Friendliness
C2
Equipment Efficiency and Reliability
C2
Modification possibilities
C3
Sofware integration
C6
Automatic Calibration
108
==PAGE:122==
Based on this comparison, Bambu Lab is generally revealed to be the most suitable man­
ufacture for the development of the mobile makerspace. More specifically, two different
printers from Bambu Lab was chosen, each with its own advantages and disadvantages:
• Bambu Lab A1 mini: Very affordable, cost efficient and have a compact size that is
suitable for a small makerspace. Furthermore, it can easily be modified to improve
overall usability and functionality. However, the small print plate limits the print sizes.
• Bambu Lab P1s: Enclosed build chamber, which makes it more safe in a school
setting, and have a large print plate allowing larger print sizes. However, it is more
expensive than the A1 Mini and does not have the same modification possibilities.
These printers will be used to further develop the mobile makerspace as will be described
in later sections where certain areas for improvements will be explored.
Software and CAD Modelling Selection and Opportunities
Before the 3D printer can be utilised to its full potential in the makerspace, certain consid­
erations should be made to find suitable software that fit with the chosen printers. First
of all, 3D CAD software is required in order to fabricate the desired models, and is the
first step in the manufacturing process that the learning activities will be based around.
A comparison between different 3D CAD software was previously done in another Gnist
Educaiton master thesis (Modelling the digital value chain for an educational makerspace)
by Lobón Trujillo (2025). In this comparison, three CAD programs (TinkerCAD, OnShape,
and Fusion 360) were assessed on accessibility, scalability, functionality and ease of use,
to find the most suitable CAD program in a school setting. Based on this comparison,
TinkerCAD will be used throughout this project to align equipment use and curriculum
activities.
Another important software consideration is the 3D slicer, as this will convert the model
created in TinkerCAD into a 3D executable file called a G­code. As the chosen print­
ers are manufactured by Bambu Lab, the obvious choice would be Bambu Studio as it
is specifically developed for these printers. However, some Danish schools might not
allow students to download any programs onto their personal computers due to certain
GDPR rules as identified in DS­I. To address this challenge, cloud based 3D slicers were
investigated to find suitable solutions. This investigation led to the discovery of a service
platform called SimplyPrint®, which can slice models directly from TinkerCAD. However,
further exploration revealed certain limits, as integrating Bambu Lab printers was under
development. It was therefore never used as the main slicer, however different use cases
were identified which could improve the general usability and solve the bottleneck issues
regarding 3D printers, which will be described in more detail in the next section.
6.3.3
Improving Equipment Usability with Service Platforms and
Modifications
As described earlier SimplyPrint® was discovered to be more than just a cloud based 3D
slicer. More specifically the entire platform allow a makerspace to create a 3D print farm
that can be run and managed through their service platform (SimplyPrint, 2025a). This
opens up a range of new opportunities that can improve the overall usability of using 3D
printers:
• Printer control and management: Accurately control each individal 3D printer re­
motely and monitor the printing process.
109
==PAGE:123==
• AutoPrint: Allow for continuously printing that is run on autopilot, meaning that man­
ual processes such as removing and starting new prints can be done automatically.
Although this require some modification to the 3D printer.
• Queue management: Allows for file management and printer matching, ensuring
that all files are considered and are not forgotten in the process.
• AI failure detection: Using the 3D printers camera, AI can notify if print failures are
detected, meaning the print can be stopped or cancelled before the printing process
is done.
• Filament usage overview: Store information about the specific filament used, and
track the overall usage, ensuring that new spools can be changed before they run
out.
• Remote access to 3D printers: As the platform is run on a cloud service, means
that it can be accessed remotely. This allows for remote monitoring and printer
control.
These features allow for a streamlined process in the makerspace, and can enhance the
user experience, efficiency, and usability regarding 3D printers. However, as stated ear­
lier, SimplyPrint® is still developing a solution that allows Bambu Lab printed files to be
sliced on their platform. This would allow a direct connection between TinkerCAD and
SimplyPrint® and bypass the need of downloading any programs onto personal comput­
ers. For now, makerspace facilitators will use Bambu Studio to slice and upload to the
SimplyPrint® queue, ensuring that students will not be forced to download any programs.
Implementation of SimplyPrint®
in order to implement and merge the SimplyPrint® service platform into the makerspace,
certain physical and software related requirements must first be met, see appendix PS7.1.
Especially the implementation of a Raspberry Pi is a crucial requirement, as this is re­
sponsible for the remote accessibility of the 3D printers and general connection to Sim­
plyPrint®. Furthermore, the Raspberry Pi must be programmed to run the specific OS
for the integration of Bambu Lab printers and Simply Print, which must be done manually
through Raspberry Pi Imager seen in Figure 6.2.
Figure 6.2: Setting up the OS for SimplyPrint® to run through the Raspberry Pi (also seen in
appendix PS7.1).
A few steps must then be followed, to enable the connection to SimplyPrint® (SimplyPrint,
2025b), see appendix PS7.2. First of all, the OS must be burned onto an SD card and
inserted into the Raspberry pi with internet connection. It can then be accessed through
110
==PAGE:124==
a web browser to add and connect each individual 3D printer to SimplyPrint® as seen in
Figure 6.3
Figure 6.3: Setting up a 3D printer through the Rasperry Pi (also seen in appendix PS7.2).
Finally, the 3D printers can now be accessed through www.simplyprint.io, where each
individual printer can be monitored and controlled through the web browser as seen in
Figure 6.4.
Figure 6.4: Web browser page from SimplyPrint® where the 3D printer can be monitored and
controlled (also seen in appendix PS7.2).
Integrating SimplyPrint® in the makerspace should now allow for a new range of oppor­
tunities, that should positively impact the overall usability regarding 3D printers.
Implementation of Swapmod
Through the exploration of SimplyPrint®, one key feature (AutoPrint) was identified which
could allow for an automated system that would have an overall positive impact on the
efficiency and utilisation rate of 3D printers. As described earlier, this feature should
be combined with some kind of modification to the 3D printer before it can be used to
its full potential. During the setup of SimplyPrint® a list of possible modifications that
would allow this feature was identified, where the Swapmod modification was one of them.
Prior knowledge of the Swapmod modification allowed it to quickly be implemented on the
111
==PAGE:125==
Bambu Lab A1 mini 3D printer, see appendix PS9.2. This implementation in combination
with SimplyPrint® opened up new possibilities for the 3D printer:
• Automated printing process: With the combination of Swapmod and the AutoPrint
feature from SimplyPrint®, means that print plates will automatically be switched
when a print is done, and a new will automatically begin thereafter.
• Overnight printing: The ability to switch print plates automatically, means that
printing can be done overnight, allowing for new prints to start in a timeslot which is
most often disregarded.
• Reduced manual handling: The Swapmod can hold up to 10 additional print plates
that will automatically be switched to start a new print process. This potentially
means that the printer can be run on autopilot up to 10 print cycles.
Combining the use of Swapmod will the implementation of SimplyPrint®, should allow for
an overall higher usability and utilisation.
6.3.4
Safety Considerations
As identified in the design specification and DS­I, certain safety considerations and pro­
cedures should be made that follows safety standards. To make sure that the devel­
oped makerspace can be used as a safe environment for students and teachers, certain
procedures were followed based on standards and recommendations made by Branche
Fællesskab Arbejdsmiljø (BFA Velfærd og Offentlig administration, 2024). These include:
• Process ventilation: A ventilation system was already considered previously when
the idea of the mobile makerspace was first conceptualised. Meaning, the mak­
erspace container arrived with a ventilation system already installed and ready for
use. However, as laser cutters have been acquired as one of the makerspace equip­
ment that will be used in this project, a direct process ventilation was implemented.
• CO2 based laser cutter: The laser cutter chosen for this makerspace, uses an
invisible CO2 laser as laser cutters using visible light can cause eye damage.
• Safe material usage: Only materials recommended by BFA will be used in manu­
facturing processes throughout the makerspace. More specifically, only PLA­based
filaments are used in the 3D printing process, as they do not require additional pro­
cess ventilation or ventilation cabinets to enclose the printers.
6.3.5
Makerspace Development and Iterative Prototyping
Based on the Build­Measure­Learn cycle, the mobile makerspace was developed through
iterations, to create a MVP that could be tested and measured in DS­II. Although the de­
velopment of the mobile makerspace has in general followed the UX process model (the
wheel) as stated earlier, additional iterative prototyping tools were used to better structure
the design cycle. More specifically, prototype planners and a protomap were used, which
are based on the work of C. A. Hansen (2021). The prototype planners allowed for struc­
turing multiple prototypes in each iteration of the makerspace, which helped in identifying
areas for improvement, which could be refined in later iterations, an example can be seen
in Figure 6.5.
112
==PAGE:126==
Figure 6.5: Exampel of a prototype planner, used to explore initial shelve and interior setup of the
first mobile makerspace iteration (also seen in appendix PS8.1).
In addition, a protomap was created to visualise the development of the makerspace
iteration over the five week long development phase, as seen in Figure 6.6. It is divided
into four main development areas, Equipment, Interior, Software, and Transportability,
which are based on requirements and criteria stated in the design specification.
113
==PAGE:127==
Figure 6.6: A prototmap that shows a timeline of all design considerations and how they are
integrated in the three makerspace iterations over time (also seen in appendix H).
The protomap allowed for a structured overview, which ensured that all areas in the de­
velopment were addressed. As seen in Figure 6.6, each individual solution and design
choice are integrated in the middle of the map, which visualise the three iteration of the
mobile makerspace. In general, three main makerspace iterations were developed and
evaluated using observations and user testing. Each of these iterations will be described
in relation to the main design elements and solutions, which assisted in the development.
In addition, smaller elements designed for the makerspace, based on either design intu­
ition or directly influencing factors from previous iterations, will be presented.
First Makerspace Iteration
The first makerspace iteration had the overall purpose of creating a setup that could be
used as a initial starting point for the rest of the development as the container arrived
almost empty as seen in Figure 6.7. The container as explained earlier, had already been
through a couple of conceptualised design iterations before the start of this project. That
meant that the interior elements such as acoustic panels, ventilation system, a glass door,
and a window, were already installed at arrival. The only other interior elements, were an
electric radiator, and breaker panel. In order for this to be converted into a functional
makerspace, meant that a lot of interior design choices had to be made.
114
==PAGE:128==
Figure 6.7: The mobile makerspace container at arrival, and the starting point of the development
phase (also seen in appendix PS8.1).
In order to start somewhere, the conceptualised CAD model, created previously by Gnist
Education, was used as inspiration for the initial setup. This was done after the pro­
curement of equipment, in order to understand how many shelves were needed. As the
modular rail system allowed for quick mounting and dismounting of the shelves, meant
that the first prototype had an overall low fidelity, and was used to explore the possibilities
of the mounting system as seen in Figure 6.8. Throughout this prototype, it was found
that the 25mm long bolts, which had originally been used, were too long and resulted in
unfastened shelves, that could risk falling down under heavy equipment, see appendix
PS8.1. These bolt were then exchanged to a shorter 20mm, ensuring that the shelves
would be well anchored and safe.
Figure 6.8: The first prototype iteration under construction (left), and after shelves were mounted
(right)(also seen in appendix PS8.1).
Equipment was mounted on the secured shelves, shortly after the first prototype as seen
in Figure 6.9. This setup, reflected the conceptualised CAD model with 3D printers in
front, laser cutters in the back, and a shelf on the back wall which in this case were used
for the recently acquired vinyl cutter, and heat press, see appendix PS8.2.
115
==PAGE:129==
Figure 6.9: The first prototype iteration with equipment(also seen in appendix PS8.2).
Multiple insights were gained from internal observations and feedback gained from show­
casing the first iteration at the DTU startup day event. It was revealed, that the mak­
erspace could feel a bit cluttered, and that there generally was not enough space that
could allow for students to work inside the container. In addition, the large windows could
attract unwanted attention when the makerspace is not in use, because of the expensive
equipment being showcased on the shelves. These insight were used to further refine
the makerspace in the second iteration.
Second Makerspace Iteration
The second makerspace iteration had the overall purpose of refining the areas of im­
provements identified through the first iteration. Furthermore, some key features were
implemented to improve the overall equipment usability, such as the Swapmod and Sim­
plyPrint® combination. Finally, this iteration were tested and validated with a user test
that reflected the makerspace in a real school context described in subsubsection 6.1.4.
To test and validate the Swapmod and SimplyPrint® solution in the user test with 7th
grade from Gladsaxe Privatskole, one of the A1 mini were used without the Swapmod
modification, to act as a benchmark, see appendix PS9.3. Additionally, a quick mock­up
of a print plate collection system were implemented, using a plastic basket situated on a
mounted shelf, which can be seen in use, in Figure 6.10. This test was furthermore used
to identify certain, areas for improvements as it was the first time testing the solution in a
user setting.
116
==PAGE:130==
Figure 6.10: The Swapmod modification in use(also seen in appendix PS9.3).
Although this prototype successfully integrated the solution in a user test, certain limits
with SimplyPrint® was also identified. First of all, with the current setup, only one printer
can be connected due to memory limitations on the Rapsberry pi model 3b. Furthermore,
certain consideration regarding slicing 3D files are crucial for the AutoPrint feature to work
successfully. Specifically the colour selection in Bambu Studio can interfere with certain
settings in SimplyPrint®, which causes the AutoPrint function to stop. The colour selection
was therefore instead done through SimplyPrint® in the future iterations and tests. Finally
the print plate collection system, ended up as a fine addition to the makerspace as it
was quick and cheap to implement. These insights allowed the AutoPrint solutions to be
refined and improved in the third iteration.
For the interior setup, a new workstation was created, with the intention of creating more
space where students and facilitators of the makerspace can work as seen in Figure 6.11.
Figure 6.11: The second prototype iteration setup(also seen in appendix PS9.4).
117
==PAGE:131==
This setup was tested by having students from 7th grade exploring the makerspace in
use, as seen inFigure 6.12. Insight was gained through observations, and validated on
how suitable the setup was for classroom use, and if the student felt comfortable and safe
in the makerspace environment, see appendix PS3.5 and PS9.4.
Figure 6.12: User test, which reveal flow issues and blockage of the container door (also seen in
appendix PS3.5 and PS9.4).
Insights revealed, that more workstation were required, and that the spatial layout of the
makerspace should allow for more open work areas. Additionally, when the students
entered the makerspace for the first time, they were so fascinated by the 3D printers that
they stopped moving. This meant that the entrance to the container quickly clogged up,
resulting in only a few students able to enter. The same thing was seen at the end of the
user test day when they had to pick up their 3D prints. To avoid this issue, moving the
3D printer further into the container should help the flow of students, and nudging them
to move all they way in, allowing more student to fit inside the container. Overall, These
areas for improvement was considered in the third and final iteration of the makerspace.
Additional elements to the second iteration is presented below. These elements were
designed and implemented based on intuition or directly influenced by missing needs
observed in the first iteration, and did not require additional testing (see appendix PS9.1).
• Shades: Semi transparent shades allow for shielding of direct sunlight while still
allowing natural light. Furthermore it shields the container for unwanted attention
and prying eyes when the makerspace is not in use.
• Excess filament collectors: Simple modification that collect all excess filament
that happen during 3D print, which ensures that no small plastic bits are scattered
throughout the makerspace floor.
Third Makerspace Iteration
The third makerspace iteration had the overall purpose of creating a makerspace that was
ready for a MVP test in a real school setting. This also meant that the iteration described
below, was developed based on areas of improvements identified through the second
iteration, but not validated during the development phase. The overall assessment and
validation, would first happen during a week long MVP test at Gladsaxe Privatskole, which
is described in more detail in DS­II. Three overall objectives, steered the development of
118
==PAGE:132==
the third iteration, connecting all 3D printers to SimplyPrint®, updating the interior setup
to better fit user needs, and making the mobile makerspace ready for transportation.
Insights from the second iteration revealed, that a successful integration of the AutoPrint
solution was possible in a classroom setting. However, as described earlier, certain areas
should be considered to improve the usability even further. To address these areas, the
Swapmod modification, was implemented on both A1 minis allowing the AutoPrint fea­
ture to work on both. Furthermore, the Raspberry pi was exchanged for a newer model
(Raspberry pi 4 model B Rev 1.5, 4GB memory), allowing all 3D printers to be connected
to SimplyPrint®, see appendix PS10.2. This meant that the queue feature, could be
utilised throughout, improving the overall user experience and usability, as 3D files could
be managed more easily. However, significant differences in usability between the two
types of printers were still noticeable as shown in the usability flow chart in Figure 6.13.
Figure 6.13: Usability flow chart, showing the difference between A1 mini swapmod combo (left),
and P1S (right).
The AutoPrint feature on the A1 mini significantly reduced the number of manual steps
119
==PAGE:133==
required to start the 3D printing process. With this feature, users only needed to upload
a sliced file to the queue system in order for the print to start automatically. The P1S
however, still require several manual steps before a print can be started. With the im­
plementation of AutoPrint, makerspace facilitators could potentially save a considerable
amount of time. This time and energy could then be redirected towards other areas in the
makerspace, thereby improving the overall usability and user experience.
The interior setup in the third iteration, was fundamentally changed, as seen in Fig­
ure 6.14, due to the challenge in flow identified in the user test with the 7th grade from
Galdsaxe Privatskole. This meant that all 3D printers were moved to the back wall in
order to nudge the student to move all the way into the container, see appendix PS10.3.
In addition, the vinyl cutter and heat press were removed, as the curriculum used in the
MVP test would be designed around the use of 3D printers and laser cutters. This allowed
for the creation of large open workstations next to the laser cutter, which can be used as
work areas for facilitators and users of the makerspace.
Figure 6.14: The third makerspace iteration interior setup (also seen in appendix PS10.3).
As this iteration is going to be tested at Gladsaxe Priatskole, meant that the mobile mak­
erspace had to be transported. Furthermore, allowing the makerspace to be used shortly
after arrival, meant that the equipment had to be shipped inside as this would ensure
minimal setup time at the school. This would also expose the equipment for excessive
shock and vibration under transportation, which might damage and render it unusable.
The original solution, was developed in a previous special course by Wiemann (2025),
where small shock absorbers were placed in each corner of the shelves. However, as
this solution were designed for a much different setup, with more narrow shelves, and
lighter equipment, a new approach had to be designed, see appendix PS10.4. Due to
time pressure, a quick solution were developed as time would not allow for a permanent
solution to be designed, before the mobile makerspace had to be moved. Straps and
foam were used to secure the equipment and absorb possible vibration and shock during
transport, as seen in Figure 6.15. This should allow equipment to be transported safely
inside the mobile makerspace.
120
==PAGE:134==
Figure 6.15: Strap and foam solution for transportation(also seen in appendix PS10.4).
Additional elements to the third iteration is presented below. These elements were de­
signed and implemented based on intuition or directly influenced by the second iteration
user test, and did not require additional testing (see appendix PS10.5):
• WiFi router and Rapberry pi box: Hides the WiFi router and Raspberry Pi, and
Streamline cable management, ensuring a clean makerspace setup.
• Fastening of electrical sockets: Streamline wire connections, and ensures that
they do not move around or bump into any equipment during transportation.
• Fastening of AMS to P1S: Fastening modification that ensures the AMS does not
move during transportation.
• Safety caps on rails: Ensures that no sharp corners are exposed, reducing the risk
of injures.
• New shelves: Additional shelve for storage, and for displaying models created in
the makerspace, which add to the overall environment and makes it feel less empty.
6.3.6
Assessment and Evaluation Proposals
As the third iteration, was developed before the final MVP test, meant that certain ar­
eas regarding, transportation, 3D efficiency, and overall flow still needed to be assessed
and validated in a real­world context. The following aspects would therefore be tested at
Gladsaxe Privatskole during the MVP test described in more detail in DS­II:
• Transportation: The solution using straps and foam should be sufficient in secur­
ing the equipment safely inside the mobile makerspace during transportation. This
should be assessed and validated by observing the container before and after trans­
port, to see if the solution has been successful.
• 3D printer efficiency and usability: Based on the flow chart analysis, it should be
expected that the A1 mini with the Swapmod modification, will be more efficient and
have a higher utilisation rate than the P1S. This should however be tested within
a classroom setting to gain a comprehensive understanding, to allow for further
exploration of the solution.
• Flow and spatial setup: The interior setup of the third iteration, should allow for a
better and more smooth flow in the makerspace, ensuring all equipment can easily
121
==PAGE:135==
be accessed. This should be tested with students and teachers using the space in
a real classroom setting to validate the setup.
6.4
Development of a Framework for Supporting Teacher's
Integration of the Gnist Makerspace Into Education
This section explores the key challenges related to facilitating teaching in makerspaces to
develop a solution that support teachers in their practice. While the Descriptive Study I re­
vealed widespread teacher interest and motivation, it also identified a number of structural,
pedagogical, and practical barriers that continue to hinder integration of makerspaces at
schools.
6.4.1
Identified Gaps and Proposed Solutions
Key themes across both literature and empirical data include a lack of time and auton­
omy for curriculum development, low teacher confidence in managing unfamiliar equip­
ment and open­ended learning formats, and concerns about inclusion, differentiation, and
safety. Although teachers value hands­on, interdisciplinary learning, they often lack ac­
cess to adaptable materials, collaborative platforms, and pedagogical support that aligns
with their everyday teaching realities.
The design specification developed at the end of DS­I outlined clear requirements for a so­
lution to support teachers in integrating makerspaces that is practical, flexible, confidence­
building, and grounded in real classroom contexts. However, there is still a gap between
these identified needs and current support offerings. Most existing makerspace programs
focus on students rather than teachers, and often rely on external facilitators, which can
unintentionally undermine teacher ownership and long­term sustainability.
To address these challenges, three initial concept directions have been developed. Each
concept targets a specific subset of the design requirements and seeks to bridge one or
more gaps identified in DS­I and thereby addresses different aspects of the core chal­
lenge; building confidence and competence for new maker teachers. The following sec­
tions will give a through description of each proposed concept as well as the reason for
them.
Concept 1: Teacher Training Workshop
Both literature and empirical findings highlight a low teacher confidence when working with
unfamiliar makerspace tools, pedagogies, and open­ended learning processes as one of
the main barriers for integrating makerspaces. To address this, the first concept proposes
a teacher training workshop designed specifically for new or inexperienced teachers. The
workshop introduces makerspace principles through hands­on activities based on pre­
defined curriculum materials aligned with national learning objectives. The goal of the
workshop is to create a low­risk, high­support environment where teachers can explore
makerspace tools, gain practical experience, and develop an initial understanding of con­
structionist pedagogy. By engaging with ready­to­use materials and example activities,
teachers can focus on the learning process and gain first hand experience with the cur­
riculum.
One of teachers’ main concerns is the level of technical skills required to use the mak­
erspace, and the workshop should support this through practical exercises. The workshop
should align with the TPACK framework, ensuring teachers gain the necessary pedagog­
ical, technical, and content knowledge required to apply it in a teaching context. Further,
the course should support a shift in mindset, helping teachers reframe failure as a learning
122
==PAGE:136==
opportunity and embrace a more exploratory approach to teaching. A key strength of this
approach is that it allows teachers to experience makerspace learning from the student
perspective by going through activities in a predefined curriculum. As highlighted in both
the literature and interviews, many teachers develop greater empathy and insight by per­
sonally encountering the same types of challenges that students face, such as iterative
failure, ambiguity, and self­guided problem solving. In addition, the workshop functions
as a scaffolded entry point into makerspace teaching. For teachers with limited STEM
backgrounds or no experience in constructionist or inquiry­based learning environments,
it provides a safe starting point with clear guidance and achievable outcomes.
To maximise the workshop’s impact, it should also include structured opportunities for
reflection and peer debriefing. The value of the experience does not lie only in com­
pleting the activities but in critically examining how and why they worked and through
collaboration transfer the insights to a teaching context. At the same time, it is impor­
tant to avoid over­scaffolding. While predefined curriculum is necessary for structuring
the workshop, too much structure may limit teachers’ creativity or reinforce dependence
on scripted lessons. Ideally, the workshop should transition from guided tasks to more
open­ended exploration, allowing teachers to begin developing their own adaptations by
the end.
Concept 2: Curriculum Configurator
The second concept proposes the development of a digital configurator platform that al­
lows teachers to browse, adapt, and assemble curricula from a structured resource bank.
This concept directly addresses multiple barriers identified in DS­I, including lack of time,
limited access to high­quality materials, and the need for differentiated instruction that
aligns with varying classroom contexts. The configurator is designed to promote teacher
autonomy and customisation, two elements repeatedly emphasised in both literature and
interviews as critical to teacher motivation and sustainable practice. By allowing teach­
ers to select, remix, and adjust existing activities, the configurator supports professional
agency while reducing the planning burden often associated with introducing new teach­
ing formats such as makerspaces.
An important strength of this concept is that it creates a continuum between structured
support and creative flexibility. Teachers can begin by using existing, predefined curricu­
lum and gradually transition toward creating or modifying activities to better fit their own
teaching style, subject requirements, or student needs. Each activity should be tied to de­
sign process stages, highlight the required skills and knowledge, and contain suggested
strategies for inclusion, differentiation, scaffolding, and assessment to assist teachers
in facilitating makerspace learning. The configurator will help address time­related con­
straints which is a recurring theme in both the empirical data and the literature. Teach­
ers frequently cited a lack of time to develop high­quality materials as a major barrier to
adopting makerspace pedagogies. By streamlining access to adaptable curriculum con­
tent aligned with national common objectives, the tool can reduce the initial planning effort
while maintaining pedagogical integrity.
Several important considerations should guide the design of the configurator. The user in­
terface must allow teachers to filter content by grade level, subject area, equipment avail­
ability, and required competencies to prevent cognitive overload and ensure relevance
to practice. A good balance between ease of use and depth of customisation is essen­
tial to support both novice and experienced teachers in creating curriculum specifically
suited to their needs. By enabling customisation over time, the configurator also support
professional growth as they move from simple implementers to creative innovators.
123
==PAGE:137==
Concept 3: Community Building and Experience Sharing Platform
The third concept focuses on creating a professional learning community platform, where
teachers can share experiences, reflect on their practice, and collaborate on makerspace
teaching. The community may take various forms, such as an online forum or in­person
meetups. This concept directly addresses one of the most pervasive issues identified in
DS­I; the isolation many teachers feel when implementing new pedagogical approaches,
especially in areas such as Technology Comprehension and maker education. Through­
out the literature and interviews, collaboration, mentorship, and informal peer exchange
were repeatedly emphasised as critical enablers of teacher confidence and innovation.
However, most teachers reported that these opportunities were infrequent, merely infor­
mative, or limited to existing teams within the same school. This concept seeks to extend
that support by creating a safe, structured, and inclusive space where teachers can reflect,
connect, and learn from one another.
A strong benefit of this concept is that it contributes to the long­term sustainability of mak­
erspace teaching. Rather than relying solely on external facilitators or one­off training
sessions, peer communities enable ongoing, practice­based professional development.
Drawing on insights from Hughes et al. (2022) and Demirata and Sadik (2023), the con­
cept emphasises the role of teacher­driven initiatives in building maker cultures through
mentorship, shared materials, and modelling. The community platform also reinforces
teacher identity as reflective practitioners and innovators. Sharing not only successful
projects but also failed attempts helps normalise the iterative, experimental mindset that
underpins maker education. This framing supports both personal growth and a cultural
shift in how experimentation is perceived in teaching practice.
However, communities do not thrive without intentional structure and facilitation. To avoid
the common pitfall of underused platforms, the space should include features like discus­
sion prompts, spotlighted projects, peer interviews, or guided themes. To support sus­
tained engagement, Gnist employees should take the facilitation role by initiating discus­
sions, selecting monthly themes, or curating contributions to maintain momentum and rel­
evance. Importantly, the platform should include both early adopters and hesitant teach­
ers from different schools, subjects, and backgrounds, to provide a range of experiences
and entry points that make the community inclusive and relatable for all.
Comparison and Alignment with Design Specification
The three concepts address distinct but complementary aspects of makerspace integra­
tion. Table 6.4 compares each concept against the design requirements identified in DS­I.
The aim is to assess how well each concept addresses the practical, pedagogical, and
structural needs expressed by teachers and highlighted in literature. The comparison
illustrates each concept’s initial alignment with the requirements: green check marks in­
dicate strong alignment, yellow dashes represents partial or potential alignment, and red
crosses indicate that the requirement is currently not addressed. As these concepts are
in an early exploratory stage, the comparison does not aim to select a final solution, but
rather to highlight strengths, limitations, and opportunities for further development of each
concept.

defined in DS­I.
