R/C10
Differentiation and assessment strategies
R11
Provide thematic example curriculum
C11
Multimodal activities for inclusive practice
R12
Support teacher collaboration
C12
Encourage experience sharing
R13
Transfer maker theory to teaching practice
C13
Support transition of the teacher’s role
R14
Hands­on experience with technologies
C14
Build teacher confidence in own skills
Notably, the teacher training workshop offers the strongest support for hands­on experi­
ence and support for teachers new to makerspace pedagogy. The curriculum configura­
tor excels in time efficiency and adaptability to fit curriculum to the specific context. The
community platform contributes most to long­term sustainability and shared practice. The
comparison reveals areas of complementarity as well as opportunities for further refine­
ment.
6.4.2
Selection and Refinement of Concept
To further develop and refine the design concepts, a co­creation workshop was conducted
with three teachers from Gladsaxe Privatskole. The purpose of the workshop was to
collaboratively explore the concepts further, gain insights on how they could be refined
to better suit their teaching practice, and selecting a concept for further development and
detailing.
Co-creation Strategy and Methodological Rationale
To refine the initial concept proposals and ground them in real­world teaching context, a
co­creation workshop is structured to gain practice­informed insights and collaboratively
shape a solution that supports teacher confidence and legitimacy in makerspace integra­
tion. This workshop employs selected design methods to support inclusive participation
and minimise facilitator bias. Details on the agenda and templates can be found in ap­
pendix PS4.1.
The workshop is initialised with participants sharing their experience with makerspaces
125
==PAGE:139==
and reflecting on their self­perceived identity and confidence as “maker teachers”, using
visual scales to establish a baseline for later comparison. The three concepts are intro­
duced only after the initial reflection to reduce anchoring effects.
To ensure equal and independent contributions from all participants, the brain writing
method is applied (Technology Leaving No One Behind, 2022). This methods collects in­
dividual feedback on each concept prior to group discussion. A detailed template guides
reflection on each concept’s strengths, potential challenges, contextual relevance, and
opportunities for further development. To select the most promising direction, participants
revisit the initial scales and discuss how their position would be influenced. The concept,
or combination of concepts, with the most positive perceived impact is selected for further
development and detailing.
To refine the concept into a concrete solution, a collaborative brainstorm is conducted
to identify essential elements, followed by a prioritisation activity. This results in a set
of teacher­defined design proposals aligned with the everyday realities of teaching. The
workshop concludes with a final reflection on expected shifts in identity and confidence,
offering insights into the anticipated impact of the refined solution.
Outcome of Co-creation Workshop
In the initial discussion, all three participants placed themselves high on the maker identity
scale but low on the confidence scale, see appendix PS4.2. One teacher noted that she
did not currently view herself as a maker teacher but was open to developing in that
direction. Another highlighted the strong alignment between maker activities and visual
arts, expressing interest in using the makerspace, although not feeling confident with the
technical equipment. The discussion revealed that key factors influencing their positions
on the scales included clarity about makerspace possibilities, expected workload, and
technical complexity. A high initial workload was acceptable, provided the long­term use
was manageable. However, if the equipment proved unreliable or too difficult to use,
teachers anticipated reverting to more familiar teaching methods. One teacher specifically
stated that without mandated involvement, she would be unlikely to invest the time needed
to develop technological fluency, despite being interested in using the makerspace.
During the brainwriting session, all three concepts received an equal amount of input,
see appendix PS4.3. Each concept was introduced with a single­sentence description to
allow participants creative freedom in how they interpreted and responded to the ideas.
The teacher training workshop was met with enthusiasm. The participants viewed it as a
valuable opportunity to gain hands­on experience, build familiarity with makerspace tools,
and reflect on how to implement the maker activities in practice. They emphasised the
importance of experiencing the activities from a student perspective to anticipate chal­
lenges and prepare strategies for student scaffolding. However, concerns were raised
about time constraints depending on the amount of sessions. Additionally, they noted a
potential gap between workshop learning and classroom implementation, highlighting the
need for structured support to ensure successful knowledge transfer to teaching practice.
The curriculum configurator was appreciated for its time­saving potential and customis­
ability. The participants valued the ability to select and adapt individual activities to match
their student’s needs and construct ready­to­use curricula. However, they stressed that
such a tool would only be effective if the teacher already possessed the necessary tech­
nical competencies. They were concerned that lack of technical knowledge could limit
teachers’ ability for executing the curriculum in practice, making the configurator redun­
dant. They also highlighted the challenge of interpretation others’ lessons plans, noting
126
==PAGE:140==
that it is time­consuming to understand someone else’s intentions. A suggested improve­
ment was to include tutorials for each activity, enabling teachers to refresh their under­
standing of specific tools and methods.
The community platform was seen as potentially helpful for quick advice and inspiration.
The participants related to their existing use of online forums for informal exchange. How­
ever, they expressed concerns about the likely low engagement, uncertain tone of inter­
actions, and the risk of becoming yet another task in their already busy schedules. They
also worried about conflicting opinions and varying skill levels creating confusion rather
than clarity. While the participants acknowledged its value as an inspirational resource,
they stated they would not rely on such a platform for core planning or decision­making.
Instead, they proposed shifting the platform’s focus from an open forum to a more curated
repository of tips, resources, and possibly tutorial videos.
When asked which concept to continue developing, there was a clear consensus. As
teachers relatively new to makerspace pedagogy, all three participants found the teacher
training workshop most relevant and impactful. They considered the curriculum config­
urator more suitable for experienced users, and the community platform as a secondary
support tool rather than a driver for teacher confidence. The teacher training workshop
was regarded as the most effective way to support teachers in building competence and
agency with makerspace teaching. In the final part of the co­creation session, the teach­
ers collaboratively identified and prioritised the most essential elements for an effective
teacher training workshop, see appendix PS4.4. The key takeaways are presented in the
box below, with the top four prioritised elements explained.
Finally, When asked how the implementation of the refined workshop would affect the
placement on the initial identity and confidence scales, all three participants moved in
a positive direction, especially the impact on their confidence level was significant, see
appendix PS4.2. This indicates, that implementation of a teacher training course is the
right place to start when supporting teacher’s integration of the Gnist Makerspace into
education.
127
==PAGE:141==
Key elements to include in a teacher training workshop:
1. Hands­on experience and teaching practice
Participants emphasised the importance of experiencing the activities both as
learners and facilitators. This dual perspective should strengthen their un­
derstanding of student challenges and support the transfer of knowledge to
practice.
2. Step­by­step introduction to the equipment
A thorough walk­through of the makerspace equipment was considered es­
sential. The participants wanted the training to start from the basics, even
covering features not directly related to the activities to ensure comprehen­
sive understanding and the ability to troubleshoot independently.
3. Realistic time frame and structure
Participants recommended a structure of two consecutive full days, followed
by a post­implementation online meeting. Day one should focus on tool intro­
duction and day two on pedagogical facilitation. The follow­up session should
allow teachers to reflect on their experience, share what worked, and identify
areas for improvement.
4. Multidisciplinary curriculum integration
The participants stressed that the workshop should support curriculum inte­
gration across all subjects, not just STEM. Since Technology Comprehension
is relevant to all disciplines, the workshop should reflect this by including mul­
tidisciplinary teaching examples.
6.4.3
From Teacher Training to Professional Development
While the proposed teacher training workshop was well received by the co­creation par­
ticipants, both empirical interviews and literature suggest that a single training session is
insufficient for the long­term integration of makerspaces into everyday teaching. Teach­
ers consistently express a need for ongoing inspiration, curriculum support, and time for
experimentation (Wagner, 2021). A major barrier to adopting maker pedagogies is uncer­
tainty about the relevant subject content and how it aligns with national common objec­
tives, particularly within new or exploratory subjects such as Technology Comprehension.
This uncertainty reduces teachers’ confidence in designing and implementing meaningful
learning experiences and discourages engagement with unfamiliar tools or open­ended
teaching formats.
Furthermore, there is limited value in providing teachers with predefined curricula that do
not account for the realities of their teaching contexts. Teachers describe such materials
as shallow and lacking personal relevance, noting that they prefer co­creation opportuni­
ties that allows them to adapt content in ways that promote ownership and professional
growth (Böwadt et al., 2019). As Hughes et al. (2022) argue, maker pedagogies are best
learned through iterative, practice­based professional development that enables teachers
to experiment, reflect, and adapt in real classroom settings.
At the Big Bang Conference, a workshop was held specifically designed to gather insights
from teachers for shaping meaningful courses for continuous education, see appendix
DSI4.1. Here, some of the highlighted elements included a mix between the overall pur­
pose and the actual teaching context to allow participants to reflect on how to apply the
theory in practice. Furthermore, participants emphasised that all teaching context is dif­
128
==PAGE:142==
ferent so a one­size­fits­all solution should be avoided.
Therefore, the teacher training concept must evolve into a more sustained and respon­
sive professional development model by combining it with the curriculum configurator to
support teachers in adapting curricula and transferring their newly gained knowledge to
their teaching practice.
Current Opportunities and Systemic Needs
Across Danish municipalities, there is a growing momentum toward professional devel­
opment in makerspace didactics. Over half of municipalities report either having already
adopted or currently exploring the implementation of professional development programs
or the broader integration of maker culture in general teaching practice (Buus, 2021). This
growing institutional interest presents a key opportunity for introducing a professional de­
velopment course grounded in both research and field needs.
Teachers increasingly express a need to stay current with both technological advance­
ments and evolving curricular standards (Demirata & Sadik, 2023; Hughes et al., 2022).
However, they also highlight limitations in existing professional development formats,
where workshops often fail to account for individual classroom context, subject areas or
teacher’s existing competencies. Teachers emphasise the importance of flexible, modular
formats that allow them to engage meaningfully over time, with the ability to test, reflect
and revise (appendix DSI4.1). Moreover, interviews and literature highlight the need for
professional development that also includes developing a maker mindset (Hughes et al.,
2022; Wagner, 2021).
To integrate effectively into this evolving landscape, new course formats should be de­
signed to complement and expand upon current efforts already in place. For instance, the
pilot study with Technology Comprehension provides a promising example. Here, teach­
ers participated in workshops that blend theoretical introduction with hands­on activities
in order to develop curriculum tailored to their own pedagogical context. The teachers in
the pilot project reported that the provided workshops had a positive influence for their
development and understanding of the subject content, as well as building a professional
network for sharing ideas and inspiration (Wagner, 2021).
Creating institutional structures to support this type of development is essential. Schools
that appoint a dedicated makerspace leader or facilitator can provide local expertise and
ensure ongoing peer support (Hughes et al., 2022). When thoughtfully designed, profes­
sional development becomes a critical lever for empowering teachers to navigate complex
pedagogical shifts and sustain high­quality maker learning in their classrooms. Embed­
ding in a wider culture of experimentation and shared learning, professional development
evolved beyond a skill­building activity and becomes a stepping stone for future commu­
nity building.
Proposed Format and Course Content
The refined model for teacher professional development is grounded in a set of core prin­
ciples, drawn from both Universal Design for Learning (UDL) (Rose, 2001) and the TPACK
framework (Harron et al., 2022). These principles address the broader pedagogical shifts
required for effective makerspace integration and reflect insights gathered from both lit­
erature and empirical studies:
• Sustained and scaffolded learning
Teachers benefit from a gradual development model, starting with basic tool use
and progressing toward curriculum adaption and facilitation (O’Brien et al., 2016).
129
==PAGE:143==
• Practice­based and iterative
Teachers need space to test, revise, and reflect on maker activities before imple­
mentation (Hughes et al., 2022).
• Teacher ownership and co­creation
Rather than relying on fixed curriculum packages, teachers should co­design or
adapt materials to suit their context (Böwadt et al., 2019)
• Interdisciplinary integration
The content should support connections across multiple subjects, not just STEM,
recognising that Technology Comprehension is relevant throughout the curriculum
(Wagner, 2021).
• Focus on mindset and facilitation
The aim is not to make teachers experts in all tools but to build confidence through
guided inquiry and reflective facilitation (Demirata & Sadik, 2023).
• Equity, accessibility, and inclusion
The professional development should be inclusive and empowering environments
for all learners to help mitigate intimidation barriers and bias (Kellam et al., 2021)
The proposed professional development model builds in the successful elements of the
original teacher training workshop while adding structure for long­term engagement and
skill­building.
The proposed format is heavily influenced by the findings from the co­
creation workshop as well as the findings in DS­I:
The first full day of the course should introduce the teachers to the makerspace tools
and guide them through concrete exercises and activities, also used for introducing stu­
dents to the tools. Teachers should be able to choose from the different technologies that
can be in the makerspace, such as 3D print, laser cutting, micro:bit, voice recording, elec­
tronics, etc. This way, teachers can spent their time efficiently focusing on the equipment
they are considering implementing in the curriculum. The purpose of the first day is not
to become experts with the technologies but to build confidence in using the equipment
(Hughes et al., 2022). Special attention is given to step­by­step introductions, starting
from the basics and going through potential troubleshooting.
The second full day of the course should focus on pedagogical strategies for facili­
tating maker activities, aligning with common objectives, and planning interdisciplinary
curricula. In smaller teams, the teachers will use the configurator to adapt existing curric­
ula by selecting appropriate activities for their class, or even create new activities with the
support from facilitators. Then, a few activities will be selected for the participant to try
and teach to each other, so they not only gain experience from the student perspective,
but also becomes familiar with the teaching methods. The goal is to increase legitimacy
in the makerspace and enable teachers to be flexible to alternate the instructions when
necessary (Demirata & Sadik, 2023).
Teachers might need time after the first day to reflect and get inspired by the opportunities
in the makerspace before they are ready to create the curriculum. Both course days
should be completed within the same week, as too much ample time in between risks
losing momentum in the progression. It might be challenging to fit the course just before
the participants are implementing their developed curricula, and teachers might want to
bring it back to their colleagues for suggestions. To support this, optional support materials
should be provided, including the curriculum configurator, tutorial videos, and material on
scaffolding strategies.
130
==PAGE:144==
The follow­up session should be scheduled shortly after course participants have ex­
ecuted the curriculum in practice, to encourage reflection on the process and future im­
provements. The session should include peer­sharing, feedback and optional coaching.
This will help teachers evolve and improve their maker pedagogy and support their tran­
sition into maker teachers.
This format for the professional development course mirrors successful approaches de­
scribed in international research, where specific content areas are followed by collabora­
tive planning and classroom experimentation (Harron et al., 2022; Hughes et al., 2022)
Embedding the Course in Existing Systems
The question of where and how the course should be implemented is critical to its suc­
cess. Interviews reveal that location significantly impacts the teachers’ ability to transfer
knowledge from course into practice, see appendix DSI6.2. If the course is held on­site
at the teacher’s own school, the learning feels more directly applicable. Teachers are
able to visualise implementation in their own classrooms and adapt activities to their spe­
cific needs. However, this limits the possibility of participants from multiple schools in the
same course or offering a course specifically for e.g. maths teachers or focused on a
specific grade level (Harron et al., 2022). Having participants from diverse backgrounds
increases the creativity as each have different insights and ideas to bring to the table. On
the other hand, if the course is held at a central location, it can be more difficult to transfer
knowledge to practice, as it can be difficult to imagine the same possibilities at the school.
However, this can foster a greater sense of freedom and experimentation, as the transfer
in location prepares the participants to be open to learn something new. This also allows
for hosting larger courses with a more diverse group of participants and the opportunity
to try out new equipment.
The Gnist Makerspace offers a variety of possibilities. It is possible to move the container
to the school to support the knowledge transfer and better imagine how it could be inte­
grated in the teaching and used by students. However, there is a limit for the duration the
container can stay on site, requiring the teachers to be ready to implement the curricu­
lum directly after the course. Teachers value ample time to further reflect and modify the
curriculum after the course before they are ready to execute it (Harron et al., 2022). The
mobility of the makerspace minimises the challenges of the centralised location, as the
teachers can learn to use the equipment inside the container, providing the exact same
setup and opportunities they will have when the makerspace is deployed at their school.
However, the container is rather small, and it is therefore limiting the amount of course
participants.
A hybrid model may offer the best solution, where the introduction to the equipment is held
at a large central location, making it possible to include many participants at once. This
will give the possibility to have all the different equipment available at once and allow the
teachers to choose which they are interested in learning. Then, when the focus is shifted
to the pedagogical strategies, the course can move into the container, making it easier for
the teachers to transfer their gained knowledge to school practice, as it will be the exact
same setup and possibilities they will have at their school for executing the curriculum.
6.4.4
Exploration of Curriculum Possibilities in the Gnist Makerspace
Through Testing Sessions with Students
Although this part of the thesis focuses on developing a professional development course
for teachers, scheduling constraints and the collaborative team setup limited opportu­
nities for continued co­creation with teachers. Instead, students were involved in test­
131
==PAGE:145==
ing selected activities in the makerspace to gain insights into their abilities, challenges,
and engagement with different formats and representations. A strong curriculum is a key
component of the professional development course and testing with students served as
practical trials of the maker activities, with a particular focus on how students respond to
different types of instructions that accommodate various learning preferences and help
scaffold complex tasks.
The following section outlines the insights from a series of classroom activities designed
for students and reflects on the implications for teachers who will later facilitate similar
activities. These small­scale trials help validate the type of support, structure, and flexibil­
ity that may be necessary to include in the final curriculum resources for the professional
development course.
Perceived Difficulty
The perceived difficulty of makerspace activities varied across testing sessions and was
not always aligned with actual task complexity or prior hands­on experience. This be­
came particularly evident in the first session, where students rated the difficulty of different
artefacts, see subsubsection 6.1.4. Contrary to expectations, all four artefacts were rated
more difficult by the student group that had completed the hands­on activity, see appendix
PS1.2. One possible explanation is that the experience increased students’ awareness
of the technical challenge involved, rather than making the task feel easier through famil­
iarity. However, the difference in the ratings could also be caused by several confounding
factors, including differing participants in the two groups, potential variation in prior knowl­
edge, and the timing of the post­test session later in the day. Despite these limitations,
a consistent result emerged across both groups. Programming was seen as the most
difficult task, yet also selected as the most interesting, suggesting that perceived difficulty
is not a limitation when the task aligns with student interest.
A similar dynamic appeared when introducing students to 3D print, see subsubsection 6.1.4.
Here, students encountered a tutorial in English with dense text and unclear progression.
For some students, the language barrier felt too high, and the lack of clear relevance in the
tutorial made them disengage early or immediately asked for help instead of attempting
the task independently, see appendix PS2.3. In this case, the perceived level of difficulty
was influenced not only by the content of the tutorial, but also its structure, length, and
lack of immediate payoff.
These findings indicate that perceived difficulty is shaped by more than technical com­
plexity alone. Students are influenced by contextual factors such as language, pacing,
perceived relevance, and personal interest. Importantly, even activities considered chal­
lenging can still be motivating when they feel meaningful.
The key to support students in makerspace activities is not necessarily to lower
the difficulty or complexity, but to ensure that tasks are well­scaffolded, personally
relevant and clearly structured.
Student Motivation and Engagement with Activities
Across testing sessions, student motivation was influenced by a combination of task struc­
ture, personal relevance, and the opportunity to create tangible outcomes. In the session
where students designed their own game piece, see subsubsection 6.1.4, students explic­
itly stated that creating a personalised, tangible object was a key motivator, see appendix
PS3.3.
132
==PAGE:146==
Similar findings were evident in the session where students were making plane wings, see
subsubsection 6.1.4. Here, a tangible boarding pass was given to the students to help
keep track of their progress through the different activity stations. Although the boarding
pass initially captured students’ attention, its novelty wore off once they received their
personal planes, see appendix PS1.4. In this session, the makerspace was decorated
with thematic artefacts to further enhance motivation. The themed setup occasionally
prompted conversations, but their overall impact on engagement was limited and difficult
to quantify.
Makerspace activities have high potential for diverse elements that appeal to various stu­
dents. This became particularly evident when students had to move between activity
stations, see appendix PS1.4. Some students clearly favoured the aesthetic and creative
aspect when decorating their planes, while others skipped this step and went straight
to testing performance. This highlights the need to design activities that accommodate
diverse interests to motivate students.
Notably, the Innovation Game session, see subsubsection 6.1.4, also showed a significant
shift in students’ self­perception, with more students imagining themselves working with
innovation after the activity, see appendix PS1.3. This suggests that short, low­risk maker
activities can foster a sense of belonging and creative confidence.
Student motivation is supported by opportunities for personal expression. Mak­
erspace activities that result in tangible, meaningful outcomes can increase engage­
ment, especially when they accommodate diverse student interests.
Scaffolding and Task Design
Students benefit from scaffolding strategies that accommodate various student prereq­
uisites, in terms of confidence level, prior experience, and working styles. This became
evident across the testing sessions, specifically in the can it fly session, see subsubsec­
tion 6.1.4. Here, students were encouraged to work at their own pace to avoid bottlenecks
at the makerspace station. However, some students were concerned about falling behind
while others showed more autonomy and confidence due to less time pressure, see ap­
pendix PS1.4. This suggests that pacing can be a critical factor in supporting student
participation.
The same testing session also highlighted the value of visual scaffolding. Students with
lower confidence relied heavily on examples of completed planes to guide their process.
Access to visual models helped these students understand what was expected, which
gave them a concrete starting point and allowed them to participate without feeling over­
whelmed, see appendix PS1.4. These observations underline the need for clear and
inclusive entry points, especially for students with limited prior experience with maker ac­
tivities.
Finally, definition of tasks plays a significant role for how students engage with activi­
ties. When introducing the students to 3D print, see subsubsection 6.1.4, some students
struggled to come up with ideas in the 3D modelling activity and lost momentum. Others
became frustrated by the tutorial that did not offer a clear product or endpoint, leading
to disengagement, see appendix PS2.3. This task was too open for students to engage
meaningfully. In contrast, when students were designing game pieces, see subsubsec­
tion 6.1.4, the balance between structure and freedom was more effective. Here, the
students were given a clearly defined task and design constraints, which helped them
progress in the design process while still allowing creative freedom, see appendix PS3.3.
133
==PAGE:147==
After the session, the teacher noted that the open­ended design task might not be suitable
for other student groups. Others might need more focus on subject theory with a clearly
defined goal, see appendix PS3.6. However, she also noted that too strict requirements
might risk compromising the creative aspect of the activity, as well as raising the demands
for the teacher in facilitating the activity.
Effective scaffolding in makerspace activities requires a balance between structure
and autonomy. Clear instructions, visual examples, and differentiated pacing can
support diverse learners. Teachers must be equipped to adjust both the task and
facilitation to match the needs of their students.
Learning-By-Doing and Through Observation
In two of the testing sessions, students were presented with TinkerCAD tutorials to support
them in learning the software. When introducing 5th grade to TinkerCAD, see subsubsec­
tion 6.1.4 the students went through a comprehensive tutorial, teaching them all the basics
of 3D modelling. For 7th grade making their own game pieces, see subsubsection 6.1.4,
they were shown where to find tutorials, but using them was optional. In both sessions, it
was clear, that students preferred a more hands­on, learning­by­doing approach, instead
of following the tutorials, see appendix PS2.3 and PS3.2. However, not completing the
tutorial made the design tasks more difficult for the students, as they did not know how
to perform simple modifications, causing them to frequently ask for help. As previously
described, the TinkerCAD tutorials had high perceived difficulty and were lacking mean­
ingful progression. Better designed tutorials might lead to a different outcome in students’
learning preference.
In the can it fly session, the importance of observation­based learning became evident.
Here, student observed how another group used the laser cutter, enabling them to carry
out many of the required steps independently, see appendix PS1.4. This enhanced the
flow and autonomy inside the makerspace. However, if students had to wait too long for
their turn, they quickly lost interest in following the process. This indicates that maintaining
momentum is critical for the students’ learning process.
Students often prefer hands­on and observation­based learning over step­by­step
tutorials. However, without foundational guidance, this can limit their progress and
confidence.
Facilitation and Practical Constraints
Across the testing sessions, it became evident that both the role of the facilitator and the
practical setup significantly influenced the success of activities. In the can it fly session,
students engaged in hands­on activities inside the makerspace container. This revealed
a clear need for more than one facilitator, see appendix PS1.4. Since the makerspace
can not contain a full class at once, and students are not allowed to be unsupervised, at
least two adults are required to facilitate the activities; one in the makerspace, and one in
the classroom.
Facilitation also played a key role in how digital tools were introduced. 5th grade was
introduced to TinkerCAD through a slideshow where each step was illustrated, see sub­
subsection 6.1.4. While the intention was to ensure clarity, the shared pace created frus­
trations. Some students jumped ahead or asked repeated questions, disrupting the flow,
see appendix PS2.3. In contrast, when introducing 7th grade, a live demonstration was
134
==PAGE:148==
used, see subsubsection 6.1.4. Although internet connection issues at the external loca­
tion created delays, students generally asked less questions but simply followed along,
see appendix PS3.2. Yet, few students still expressed frustration when forced to wait for
peers, reinforcing the need for flexible pacing.
Finally, after the game piece session, the teacher emphasised the importance of experi­
encing the full activity herself, in order to be able to facilitate the teaching, see appendix
PS3.6. Hands­on participation gave her the necessary experience and confidence to be
in charge of the facilitation, provided she would also gain experience with the actual print­
ing process. She suggests to include the activity in the professional development course
along with more defined tasks, so teachers will learn about the many different possibilities
with 3D print.
Successful makerspace implementation depends not only on well­designed tasks,
but also on facilitation strategies and logistical conditions. Sufficient staffing, flexible
pacing, and teacher familiarity with the process are key to ensuring both pedagogical
quality and classroom feasibility.
6.4.5
Design Principles for Gnist Curriculum
To ensure the Gnist Curriculum will align with the insights from the testing sessions with
students as well as be relevant to include as a part of the professional development
course, a list of design principles have been defined. These principles aim to balance
pedagogical effectiveness, student engagement, and practical implementation.
• Scaffolded complexity
Students thrive when tasks provide a clear entry point while gradually allowing for
increased complexity. Visual examples, structured tutorials, and defined task con­
straints support students with lower confidence, while still enabling autonomy for
more experienced students.
• Tangible and personal outcomes
Activities that result in a physical product significantly increase student motivation,
especially if they are personally meaningful. Giving students the opportunity to take
ownership of their design, and bring home a concrete result, enhances their en­
gagement and connection to the learning process.
• Balance between structure and creative freedom
Both students and teachers benefit from tasks that are neither too open­ended nor
overly prescriptive. Defined goals help maintain momentum and support alignment
with subject objectives, while room for personalisation fosters creative thinking and
student ownership.
• Support for diverse learning preferences
Activities should appeal to a variety of interests and learning styles, from visual
and hands­on students to those driven by aesthetics, competition, or technical chal­
lenges. This includes allowing students to work at different paces and incorporating
peer learning opportunities.
• Teacher adaptability and facilitation
Successful implementation relies on teacher familiarity with both the equipment,
related software, and the learning objectives. Hands­on teacher experience is es­
sential for confident facilitation. Curriculum materials must therefore include instruc­
135
==PAGE:149==
tions, differentiation options, and adaptable modules that can be tailored to different
student groups.
• Alignment with practical constraints of the Gnist Makerspace
The curriculum must be compatible with the physical setup of the makerspace, in­
cluding limited space, equipment availability, and required supervision ratios. This
necessitated thoughtful student flow planning, parallel activity options, and realistic
time allocation.
These principles for the foundation for the development of a modular, adaptable curricu­
lum, suitable to implement as a part of the professional development course. In the follow­
ing sections, this framework will be used to make a prototype of a makerspace curriculum
with embedded opportunities for differentiation and alignment with the Gnist infrastructure.
6.4.6
Development of a Modular Curriculum for Differentiated
Implementation
The development of the first modular Gnist Curriculum example is guided by the defined
design principles and shaped by the constrains of the final makerspace test at Gladsaxe
Privatskole, where six lessons have been allocated with 7th grade. The schools assigned
the lessons from the mathematics subject, and the participating teacher expressed a clear
interest in incorporating applied mathematics in the curriculum theme.
A key inspiration for the theme came from a curriculum presented at the Big Bang Con­
ference, where 9th grade students designed and built scale models of houses, see ap­
pendix DSI4.1. This concept was adapted to better suit the available time frame, age
group, and the Gnist design principles. It was reframed into a theme, where students
would design and build their own dream bedroom, integrating geometry, measurement,
and spatial reasoning in a hands­on context. This ensures a clear entry point, personal
connection, balanced creative freedom, and a tangible output.
While the selected subject and time frame reflect the specific testing opportunity at Glad­
saxe Privatskole, they did not define the curriculum’s overall scope. Rather, they served
as a practical reference point to validate whether the curriculum could be successfully
adapted to a real­world teaching context. Throughout the development process, the pri­
mary focus remains on designing a flexible and scalable curriculum structure that can
accommodate different grade levels, student prerequisites, classroom conditions, and
teacher preference in both content and methods.
To support this, the first step is to define a set of possible activities that can form the
foundation of such a modular curriculum.
Co-creation Workshop for Defining Possible Activities
To help develop an adjustable curriculum, a co­creation session is planned with a teacher
from Virum Skole. The purpose is to define a set of activities to build a full curriculum. All
the details of the process can be found in appendix PS5.1.
As there is only one participant, the facilitator will be an active part of the development
process, to support the discussion, clarify reasoning behind choices, and ensure the rele­
vance of proposed activities. However, active facilitator involvement will also influence the
direction of the discussion, which may affect the participant’s responses. This trade­off is
accepted to ensure process adaptability and maintain momentum.
The defined theme, time frame, and grade level will serves as a starting point for an initial
brainstorming session of which activities can be included in a curriculum. Then, three
136
==PAGE:150==
different design process frameworks will be presented based on the recommendations in
the Gnist Curriculum Guide by Buch (2025). This will allow the participant to choose the
framework he finds most intuitive instead of spending a lot of time explaining each part of
the process in detail. Based on the stages in the design process, an additional brainstorm
will be made to find possible activities for each stage. This will give a variety of activities
for teachers to choose from, when adapting the curriculum to their specific class, context,
and needs.
Each activity in the individual design stages will be discussed in terms of differentiation and
scaffolding opportunities, to highlight relevant factors to include in a teaching manual as
well as most appropriate teaching methods. Finally, selected activities from each design
stage will be described in more detail, including estimated duration, to form an initial lesson
plan outline.
Outcome of Co-creation Workshop
The workshop began with a discussion about the definition of “dream bedroom”. Here,
the participant raised the question whether it should be a remodelling of their current bed­
room or if it should be an imaginary room, see appendix PS5.2. Making their actual rooms
could function as a motivating factor, as it is possible to implement in reality based on the
produced model, and thereby become more relatable and meaningful for the students.
Furthermore, it would also ensure that students could not copy each other, and ensure
everyone participating actively in the activities. However, this would make it harder to dif­
ferentiate the difficulty level, and some students might find it unfair if their room is smaller
than others’. Alternatively, making an imaginary room would offer a higher degree of free­
dom and ensure equal opportunities for all. However, this would also make the activities
less meaningful for the students, as they would not be able to implement their designs in
reality. After this discussion, it was decided that the students would make their current
bedrooms as it better aligns with the defined design principles and provide a clear entry
point into the design process.
The following brainstorming session resulted a broad and diverse set of potential activities
mapped across the different stages of the Engineering Design Process (Sillasen, 2025).
The list below contains some of the ideas related to the “get ideas” stage of the design
process.
• Make a mood board defining the style in your room by cutting out pictures from
magazines or finding pictures online.
• Sketch your initial ideas for what your room should look like.
• List all your current furniture and consider if you want to keep them or exchange
with something new.
• Share your ideas with a partner and use their insights as inspiration
In total, 40 activities were generated covering a wide range of teaching approaches and
learning styles.
A complete list of proposed activities is included in appendix PS5.3.
Through an iterative dialogue, the individual activities were explored in terms of assessing
the potential learning experiences and how to best support students in progressing from
initial idea generation to the final construction of a physical model.
To test the applicability of the modular framework, 17 of the proposed activities were se­
lected and combined into a complete curriculum tailored to the 7th grade context at Glad­
saxe Privatskole, see appendix PS5.4. These were further refined with attention to dif­
137
==PAGE:151==
ferentiation, scaffolding, and alignment with learning objectives. The full list of activities,
tied to the individual stages of the process, not only supported the construction of a con­
crete curriculum but also provided a reusable foundation for future curriculum adaptations
across grade levels, classroom context, and teaching preferences.
6.4.7
Curriculum Considerations to Fit the Gnist Makerspace
Due to the limited size of the container and the amount of equipment, considerations
need to be made on how to ensure distribution of students in the makerspace. In this
curriculum, students will use the 3D printers to make their furniture, and use the laser
cutter to make the walls and floor for their bedroom. However, there is only one laser
cutter available in the container, and therefore only possible to have one student using it
at a time. Therefore, it is important to encourage students to work in different paces or
complete the activities in different order to minimise waiting time for the equipment. As
a part of their bachelor thesis, (Dau et al., 2025) have developed a template specifically
designed to support teachers in making these considerations. They initiated a co­creation
workshop where they could test their tool, while also giving valuable insights in how to best
structure the curriculum for the final test at Gladsaxe Privatskole.
Figure 6.16 below summarises the outcome of the workshop focused on students flow
between activities. The full test can be found in appendix PS6. In the curriculum, there
are two main paths of activities, one related to modelling furniture and one related to
cutting the walls. The students have to go through both paths to end up with a finished
model of their bedroom, but can freely choose in which order they will do the activities. The
outlined boxes indicate activities that physically takes place in the makerspace, although
only one of them involves students working with the equipment.
Figure 6.16: Flowchart of the curriculum activities indicating the different paths the students can
take in the design process.
138
==PAGE:152==
By presenting both paths simultaneous, students will be encouraged to work in their own
pace and thereby not all wanting to use the laser cutter at the same time. A queue system
was further implemented, so the students can continue working on other tasks, if the
laser cutter is not available. Finally, the activities related to painting the room was made
optional, indicated with the dashed lines. It is difficult to estimate the required time for the
maker activities, so having a related task ready for the students who finish early, will keep
them engaged throughout the curriculum duration.
Initial Outline of the Lesson Plan
Based on the defined activities and the flow considerations, an initial lesson plan was
developed. Below is a short outline of each lesson:
Lesson 1:
First, the students are introduced to the overall theme of the curriculum,
the purpose, and the learning objectives. Then, a common brainstorm in class will help
kickstart the creativity for the students, and they will individually choose which three pieces
of furniture they find the most essential, which will serve as personal success criteria for
the project. Finally, a short refreshing of the theory on unit conversion and scaling to
ensure all students know the mathematics involved in the curriculum.
Lesson 2:
In this lesson, the students will start by making a sketch of their dream bed­
room to help define what should be included and why it is important. Then, the students
will be guided into TinkerCAD where they will begin modelling their furniture or walls and
floor in scale 1:20. Students will be introduced to the limitations of the container and
equipment use.
Lesson 3:
In this lesson, students will continue making their furniture, walls, and floor
in TinkerCAD. In TinkerCAD they can find pre­made furniture that are easy for them to
adjust in size, but they are also allowed to make them from scratch. Whenever a piece
of furniture is finished, it will be sliced and put in the printer queue. If students finish
modelling their walls and floor they will add their name to the queue and when the laser
cutter is available go to the container for production.
Lesson 4:
This lesson will be on a new day during the test, and will therefore begin with
a round where students can tell how far they have come and if they have any issues they
need help with. Then, they can continue working on modelling furniture, walls, and floor.
Lesson 5:
In this lesson, the students will continue their work. However, it is expected
that some might be finished or waiting for their turn with the laser cutter. These students
will be presented with the optional activity where they will calculate the paint usage.
Lesson 6:
In the final lesson, the students will have time to make their finishing touches
on their models, before presenting them in class. The presentation will be in the form of
an exhibition, as this does not require the students to prepare a speech, but will allow
them to have a closer look at what their peers have created. Here, it is important that the
teacher ask the students questions related to their choices, e.g. “Why did you choose
to make a couch and not a bed?” or “How did you calculate the areas that need to be
painted?”.
The initial lesson plan was sent to the teacher who will participate in the final test to gather
feedback on the theme, activities, structure, and timing. She was very positive about the
139
==PAGE:153==
overall topic but found the curriculum too optimistic in terms of time allocation. Originally,
lessons 1 and 2 were scheduled in the morning and lesson 3 in the afternoon on the first
day, while the remaining lessons were scheduled after lunch on the second day. She noted
that this setup might disrupt students’ flow and hinder deep engagement, as they would
need to shift focus back and forth between subjects. To address this, she suggested to
expand the curriculum to include additional lessons, allowing for five consecutive lessons
on the first day. This would provide more time to each activity and support sustained
focus. The content of each lesson will not be changed, but the two extra lessons will be
similar to lesson 3 described above. The full lesson plan, including learning objectives
and facilitation strategies can be found in appendix C.
6.4.8
Curriculum Example: Design Your Own Dream Bedroom
The final Gnist curriculum example was developed through an iterative process based on
the insights and activities defined above. The curriculum is designed for a 7th grade class
over eight lessons and follows the design stages of the Engineering Design Process (Sil­
lasen, 2025). The curriculum integrates all 17 activities selected in the co­creation work­
shop, along with an additional activity for accessing TinkerCAD. However, some activities
are made optional due to time constraints.
This curriculum example is designed to be suitable for the final testing session with Glad­
saxe Privatskole. These students have prior experience with 3D modelling though pre­
vious tests. Therefore, the curriculum does not include activities to learn how to use the
software. Further, the curriculum includes mathematical subjects and it is expected that
students are familiar with the theory before starting the project. Finally, each student must
have an individual computer and have basic computer skills.
To get a head start on the project, the students are given homework, where they have to
measure their room. This includes making a sketch of the room with all measurements of
the walls, floor, and placement of doors and windows. The homework description given
to the students can be found in appendix D.
To avoid disrupting student focus through the design process, all necessary plenary in­
structions are planned in the beginning of Lesson 1. This includes an introduction to the
theme, an overview of the activities, how to use the equipment, and a presentation of the
learning objectives. The mathematical theory about scaling and unit conversion are intro­
duced through a shared example relevant to the theme to refresh student knowledge of
the subjects. Finally, a plenum brainstorm of possible furniture to include are conducted to
initiate the creative process. Then students will select three items they consider essential,
serving as their individual success criteria.
Detailed written guides were made to support individual pacing and allow for student au­
tonomy in the process. The 17 activities were grouped in four categories; 1) introduction,
theory, and accessing TinkerCAD, 2) Selecting furniture, creating a budget, and making
3D models to scale, 3) Creating a blueprint of the room and transferring it into Tinker­
CAD for laser cutting, and 4) Designing how wall should be painted, selecting paint, and
calculating the cost to paint the walls. The full student guides can be found in appendix
E.
Based on the UDL principles (Rose, 2001), the instructions includes both written and
visual cues. An example can be seen in Figure 6.17. The initial draft layout for the student
material was developed by Eriana Panopoulou who is a part of the Gnist Team.
140
==PAGE:154==
Figure 6.17: Example of student guide explaining how to get access to TinkerCAD.
To ensure the clarity of the written guides, they were tested with a dyslexic user. This led to
minor improvements in phrasing and formatting to support the readability and accessibility.
This curriculum example reflects the core design principles identified through the co­
creation workshops and testing sessions with students. It supports student autonomy, en­
ables differentiated pacing, and aligns hands­on activities with personal, meaningful, and
tangible outcomes. Each activity can be substituted with another, depending on teaching
preferences and class prerequisites. Supplementary teacher guides will support scaffold­
ing strategies, suggest assessment methods, and propose alternative structures to help
balance creative freedom with instructional clarity.
6.4.9
Outline of the Professional Development Course
The curriculum example is designed not only for direct classroom implementation, but also
as a core component of the professional development course. As outlined in subsubsec­
tion 6.4.3, the professional development course spans over two full days and a follow
up meeting, with a focus on hands­on experience from a student perspective as well as
on pedagogical strategies. Due to these time constraints, teachers will not be able to
complete the full curriculum example while also learning to operate the equipment and
reflecting on implementation strategies. Additionally, during the initial co­creation work­
shop, teachers expressed a desire to gain experience not only from a student perspective,
but also as facilitators of makerspace activities.
The curriculum example is split into two main paths, building furniture for 3D printing and
making walls and floor with the laser cutter. This structure allows course participants
to be divided into two groups, focusing on each path to gain experience from a student
perspective and reflect on the associated pedagogical strategies. Participants then switch
to a facilitator role by pairing with someone from the other group, taking turns guiding the
other through the activities. This dual perspective reinforces their understanding of both
the student and facilitator roles. This provides practical experience with the full curriculum,
which builds teacher confidence in a structured and supported way. Finally, at the end of
the course, the teachers will be presented with the full resource bank based on the 40
proposed activities. They can then pick and choose the activities and materials they want
to include, thereby tailoring the curriculum to their specific teaching context. The course
structure is outlined in Table 6.5 below.
This practical insight helps build teacher confidence, clarifies how to scaffold open­ended
tasks, and demonstrates how makerspace activities can meaningfully align with subject
goals. Integrating the curriculum into the course enables teachers to adapt, implement,
and reflect on their own use of makerspaces with the support from colleagues and the
course facilitator.
141
==PAGE:155==
Table 6.5: Outline of the professional development course based on the defined time frame
Time
Description
Day 1: 8–12
Participants are divided into two groups. One group works with
designing and 3D printing furniture, while the other focuses on cre­
ating room layouts for laser cutting. Each group completes their
assigned task using the student materials to gain hands­on ex­
perience from a student perspective while guided by the course
facilitator.
Day 1: 13–17
The course facilitator guides participants through the operation of
the 3D printers and laser cutter to produce their created artefacts.
Participants explore potential challenges and teaching points when
working with students. Afterwards, a discussion of classroom man­
agement, equipment supervision, and typical troubleshooting sce­
narios will help participants reflect on how to implement the activi­
ties in their own practice.
Day 2: 8–12
Participants pair up with someone from the opposite group and
take turns facilitating their activity. Each participant plays the role
of both facilitator and student. This gives them practical experi­
ence with guiding others, answering questions, and adapting in­
structions in real time. This will ensure all participants will gain
experience with the full curriculum.
Day 2: 13–17
Joint reflection on the facilitation experience, including what
worked well and which situations were challenging. Participants
are then introduced to the full activity bank and begin adapting the
curriculum example to their own context, choosing relevant activi­
ties, reordering stages, and identifying opportunities for differenti­
ation.
Follow­up: 4 hours
A follow­up session (virtual or in­person) after classroom imple­
mentation. Participants share how it went in practice, reflect on
successes and barriers, and identify changes they would make in
future iterations. This session also supports continued community
building and knowledge sharing.
Further Course Possibilities to Sustain Teacher Engagement
The proposed professional development course is based on insights from teachers with
no or limited prior makerspace experience. It is therefore particularly suited to support
teachers who are new to maker pedagogies. However, to ensure continuous and scalable
development, additional courses may be needed to support teachers with varying levels
of experience.
Tier 1
The proposed professional development course is well suited for an introduction
to maker activities. Teachers will select a pre­defined curriculum they want to implement
in their own practice, and the course gives them the required confidence for execution.
When booking the container, a Gnist employee familiar with the curriculum will be present
142
==PAGE:156==
to support the teacher, particularly to assist with equipment use and maintain smooth
student flow in the makerspace.
Tier 2
This course targets teachers with limited makerspace experience who are curious
on the possibilities and wants to design their own curriculum tailored to specific subjects,
grade levels, or student needs. Rather than implementing a pre­defined set of activities,
participants are introduced to the full Gnist resource bank and supported in selecting,
modifying, and combining activities into a curriculum that fits their own teaching context.
The course focuses on practical co­creation to define learning objectives, select relevant
technologies and structure student progression through the design process. This course
empowers teachers to take ownership of the integration of makerspaces in their teaching
and enables long­term scalability across different subjects. When booking the container,
a Gnist employee will be present in the makerspace to support proper and safe use of the
equipment but the regular teacher will facilitate the curriculum independently.
Tier 3
This series of courses is designed for teachers who wish to gain full autonomy in
operating the makerspace equipment. Each course focuses on a specific type of equip­
ment and provides in­depth, hands­on training aimed at building technical proficiency and
confidence in setup, troubleshooting, and safe use. Upon completion, participants receive
a Gnist certificate verifying that they are qualified to operate the equipment independently.
This certificate is a prerequisite for booking the makerspace without Gnist staff support.
Teachers can return for additional courses to gain certification in other types of equipment.
Together, these three tiers form a scalable progression model that supports teachers from
initial exposure to full independence. This structure allows schools to build internal capac­
ity over time and sustain meaningful makerspace integration across subjects and grade
levels.
6.5
The Development of the Business Model and
Implementation Plan
This section explores the challenges, related to the ’Business Model and Implementa­
tion Plan’ of Gnist Education and the development thereof. The development make use
of strategically selected design methods and tools, in the hopes of designing not just a
plausible framework, but an instrumental and valuable framework for the business model
and implementation plan. DS­I revealed that schools are a complex size to address. The
public schools are governed and financially depended of the municipality, by which the
decision making of any large investments are undertaken solely by the internal mecha­
nisms within the municipality. Furthermore, the timing to address; the value and ethical
alignment; the adherence to specific procurement hierarchies makes the Municipality a
customer a direct implementation barrier. Private schools on the other hand, have differ­
ent autonomous leavers, which makes their decision­making procedure and action­taking
speed a more favourable target as a first point of contact to the world outside the incu­
bation nest of DTU Skylab. Strategically sourced private schools can act as a foundation
and launch pad for real user testing and core­product validation in an environment man­
aged by a relatively small number of people with a targeted focus. Moreover, the current
situation of Gnist Education demands clarification of the business model. DS­I can inform
the development of this aspect with findings from the literature, that can potentially shift
the narrative of Gnist Education’s main offering.
The ’design brief’ specifies the requirements and success criteria for the development of
143
==PAGE:157==
the business model and implementation plan. In alignment with these, the design of this
aspect will be developed, with the focus of addressing the gaps, barriers and opportunities,
that were found and synthesised in DS­I. As an initial step towards a holistic and coherent
output that encompasses this aspect, the business model will be addressed first and the
implementation plan last.
6.5.1
Gaps, Barriers and Opportunities - A starting Point for developing the
business model
The development of the business model and implementation plan, will make use of ele­
ments from the Soft Systems Methodology. The method will be used as an action research
and development method in a case study of Gladsaxe Privatskole. The case study will
inform the development and Gnist Education with insights regarding a specific business
model type, targeted towards the Danish private school sector.
6.5.2
Analysing the As-Is state of the Business Model
There is a beginning to everything. The business model has seen only incremental im­
provements, since its initial formulation in the first funding applications developed in the
early stages of the InnovationContainer era. The focus here was primarily pointing to­
wards the physical space, the makerspace in a container. The assumption was, that
the concept of the ’modularised makerspace in a container’; with a build in set of cur­
riculum; and targeting ’the girls’ roles in STEM education’, withheld a powerful enough
value proposition, that also largely based itself on the previous work of the DesignPark
team. However, this became evident, when two declined funding applications cemented
that. Through the fall of 2024, InnovationContainer, while working primarily at finalising
the details of the containers modular interior design, the team became aware of, that a
servitisation­model could enhance the value delivered for the end­customer, but also that
our target segment was not only about strengthen girls opportunities later in life, but every­
one, who might not have direct access to makerspace­led learning experiences, but also
those ones who already had, but which access was not sufficient and effective enough. At
the end of 2024; a couple of months before our project commenced, the business model
was framed at a board meeting as such.
An outline of the entire business model is needed to promote a solid foundation for the
entire business model development. In an effort to do that and systematically outline the
missing elements of the business model, the development deploys the tool and associated
method of the lean business model canvasMaurya (2010). The lean business model
canvas is one of a number of ’canvases’ or mental models used to analyse and project a
business model for new business or with the aim to make alterations and changes to an
existing business model and the lean canvas is especially suited for business in the lean
start­up phase, making it ideal for this purpose. In Figure 6.18 the former business model
is analysed in relation to the As­Is situation. The analysis elaborates and highlights (from
left to right) Gnist Educations; existing and substituting alternatives, key metrics, unfair
advantage, channels and high level concept. These elements are on their own typically
not mentioned in much details, however the small things to the business model can be
defining points on how Gnist Education positions itself in the market in relation to it’s
alternatives, how the impact of the value proposition is visioned to be measured, what the
unfair advantage is, that enables Gnist to do this, and not somebody else, how unique
value can be realised through the high­level concept and what the engagement channels
with the defined users and customers might be.
144
==PAGE:158==
Figure 6.18: Business Model Canvas ­ Lean Canvas As­Is(see appendix PS14.2).
From the As­Is business model situation, a To­Be model can be designed. The DS­I,
alongside discussions bigger team meetings with related Gnist Education members and
internal team conversations have informed the new initial To­Be lean business model. The
new iterations to the To­Be model is seen in Figure 6.19, where an ’early adopter’ has en­
tered. Additionally, a first projection of the Gnist concept, as to be understood through the
element of the ’high level concept’ has also been found and a new customer acquisition
channel has been found. The element of the early adopters relates to the schools with­
out current access to makerspace, either public or private. Through this iteration, these
school’s are now the primary target point for Gnist and should align with the development
plan.
145
==PAGE:159==
Figure 6.19: Business Model Canvas ­ Lean Canvas As­Is 2 After DS­I(see appendix PS14.3).
The new channels, that were discovered, were festivals, such as Folkemødet and Roskilde
Festival, which aligns with value of Gnist Education. Roskilde Festival is a big DIY fes­
tival in it’s essence. Roskilde Festival has a strong presence within the general Danish
public, why placing the Gnist Container there would most likely attract customers from are
targeted user­group, among many other interests, that could open opportunities for col­
laboration. Folkemødet is a political festival, where people from the highest political level
are mingling in an out with other festival attendances. Both Folkemødet and Roskilde
Festival could be physical platforms for attracting positive attention. Also festivals, such
as maker­festivals and childrens festival could also be channels, that Gnist could explore.
These festival aligns more linearly with the value proposition of Gnist Education, but are
also more niche.
6.5.3
The second To-Be business model - A Co-creation exercise with Silas
Korndrup
The third iteration of the business model is iterated through a co­creation session with
Silas Korndrup from Next Arc Venture Capital(see appendix PS14.1). Next Arc Venture
is a venture capital, which screens business for big individual business angels, that uses a
strategy of aiming big in hopes of profiting form a Unicorn start­up. Silas has knowledge of
funding and their internal mechanisms and decision­making process. In the development
of the To­Be model, the co­creation session revealed a new take of on the business model
entirely. Silas denotes that recurring revenue must be considered into the business plan
in a more strategic sense. He urges the notion of the platform business model, which we
have already familiarised ourselves with through the DS­I. A new channel covering the
platform aspect is introduced along with a new problem, a corresponding solution, and
value proposition. The platform aspect introduces actors such as industry partners as
146
==PAGE:160==
content­creators, in exchange for publicity, and access to school­market.
Figure 6.20: Business Model Canvas ­ Lean Canvas To­Be After DS­I (see appendix PS14.4).
6.5.4
Business Economics - Integrating the business model
The business model canvas is a good tool for visioning, set a business direction and act
as a boundary object to build mutual understanding between to actors, however, it also
has it’s limitation. As Teece (2010) also mentions, the business model is not an excel file
with forecasting revenue numbers, but the business model can be integrated in one, to
create an even better alignment of reality between the ’visioned future’ and a ’potential
future’.
From the To­Be1 lean canvas Figure 6.19, the spill­over of potential revenue streams and
cost structure alongside the findings of potential early adopters, can narrow down the
scope of, how the business model and a realistic implementation plan, can play together.
On the revenue streams, there is the container, the curriculum and the added teacher
training courses, that has through this study also seen further development. There is also
the cost­structure, which is divided into to two sub­groups, the ’fixed costs’ and the ’vari­
able costs’. The container is in this model described under variable costs, and not a fixed
costs, as it can be argued, that it is not a yearly recurring cost, although it is considered
to an large start­up investment. Rather, in reality it is more directly tied to the operations,
and the depreciation (decrease in value asset), that goes into every deployment. The
same applies to the equipment.
On the revenue streams, it is stated, that the revenue model could either be ’subscription
based’ or ’pay­per­use’ or a ’one­time­contract­payment’. This ties to the overall pricing
strategy, and in the current situation, it might tell, which of the three, that will suit the
situation best, as it depends on who the customer is. As it is noted in the ’target customers
147
==PAGE:161==
and users’ column (see appendix PS14.2), these can either be the municipality on behalf
of of the public schools, the public schools themselves and/or the private schools. The
private and public schools falls under the same category, if it is subscription­based, and
the price is kept below DKK 10.000 Table 5.4(Brix, 2024). The municipality takes over,
when it goes above that threshold, and then the situation is rather different, as stated
in DS­I. A price, that fits within the legal framework of the public school, will be hard,
considering the investment, Gnist Education is one day hoping to cover. For this reason,
the focus will from here on be on the private schools, as they also have quicker and more
direct decision­making processes, and thus a lower barrier of entry. It is also noted, that
it might be strategically wise to target the private schools first, and get traction, that can
then flow into the public­sector and the municipalities.
Deploying the Competitive-based Pricing Strategy
Current prices of competitors, that also offers teaching material, either physically or through
a learn portals or both are either non­existing, as in free, like LIFE FondenLIFE Fonden
(2018b) or have very competitive and flexible pricing models like Alinea or Gyldendal (ap­
prox. DKK 900­1200 per class) Gyldendal (2025) Alinea (2025), other vendors like LEGO
Education LEGO Education (2025) is partially free, meaning the the portal is free to ac­
cess, but the LEGO sets themselves has to be paid for. This pricing model makes use of
a one of payment, that grants the school access to learning materials covering all grade
levels for a reasonable investment (approx. DKK 2000 ­ 30.000) Lekolar (2025), where
the upper bound DKK 30.000 is the price for class with 20 students. Such a class set
can be applied to cover an entire school for years. Considering a class size of 20 stu­
dents corresponding to the national average from 2024 Danmarks Statistik (2025); a Lego
Education set to last 5 years, covering one track of classes from grade 1­6 (Lego Spike
is aimed at these grade(LEGO Education, 2025)), and LIFE to be an equal substitute in
half of the cases, the average price per student amounts 47 DKK per student (see exact
calculation in appendix I (Data)). The price of Gnist Education should reflect this number,
if considering a competitive­pricing model, but it should also reflect it’s current place and
time in the market. Using the ’price per student’ metric is in general considered ’standard
practice’, as it is unanimously understood across multiple sectors.
The current state, of Gnist Education cannot rely solely on renting out the container, as this
could never cover the cost of the investment and the fixed costs related to administration
and variable cost related the maintenance of equipment. This was already discovered
in the fall of 2024, when InnovationContainer unless prices are aimed so high, that it is
only reasonable for the municipality to consider, and they would expect traction to some
degree. For further reference to the business economics see (appendix I).
6.5.5
The Development of the Implementation Plan
In addition, the business model and business economical aspects will also be integrated,
to not only inform the targeted implementation plan from a holistic actor­oriented point
of view, but also from a user/customer needs and requirements point of view. Here, the
perceived value of Gnist Educations entire (almost) conceptual offering in an MVP­format.
Consequently leading to an even better pricing­model, now that value­based pricing from
a realistic standpoint, cal also be considered and potentially utilized. Moreover, the As­Is
and and current To­Be value proposition is also to be validated, not only externally but
also internally, to see if the proposed offering is both feasible and viable.

6.5.7
The Implementation Plan - Identifying controversies, negotiation
points and obligatory passage points at Gladsaxe Privatskole
The already mentioned first meeting with Rune, clarifies overall controversies and negoti­
ation points at the school. The identified controversies relates to the school’s economical
situation and their general makerspace familiarity at private schools (and as their has not
been any proof of investigation to clarify this point, we were inclined to ask). The identi­
fied negotiation points focus on their procurement capabilities and practical and logistical
capabilities. As a result of these, they can either form new controversies, such as non­
aligning price points, or obligatory passage points, that both parties make an effort to take
action on. As an example, the school did not have any outdoor power outlet, which is a
149
==PAGE:163==
big controversy for the Gnist Education concept. The container makerspace is designed
to run over­night, why cutting the power supply at midday would jeopardise, how the 3D
printing process could work in a limited time frame.

The economical challenge
Gladsaxe Privatskole has in recent years been through an economically rough stage. The
school was in a dire state economically, when hardcore inherent Private school values
and principles collided with the values of the parents, resulting in an net loss of DKK
800.000 in the school year 2021/2022 (see appendix PS13.1). Today, the school is in a
much healthier place both financially and the schools core values, however the school
is still operating on a very low budget. Out of all their expenses in 2024, totalling at
DKK 14.500.000, DKK 11.500.000 were spend on everything related to teaching including
teacher salary, an only 200.000 was spent on materials covering everything from new arts
supply to to books and learning portals (see appendix PS13.1). Rune, appendix PS13.1,
denotes, that some schools have a significantly higher budget, than that of Gladsaxe
Privat skole.
The logistical challenges
The logistical challenges at the school can be more or less case specific to the school’s
own situation, why in the future, it will be crucial for a successful container makerspace

deployment and it to be fully functional at a school’s premises, that the requirements are
investigated locally or notified with the school in advance.
