6.7
Evaluation Plan
To conclude the Prescriptive Study phase, a evaluation plan has been developed to deter­
mine whether the proposed solutions are ready to be tested in practice, and which parts
will be tested. Further, the plan will help outline the structure for the subsequent actual
evaluation as part of DS­II.
The solutions have been iteratively developed independently of each other and refined
through co­creation and expert feedback. This evaluation plan helps determine if the
solutions are coherent and ready for a real world MVP test. The final test will take place
at Gladsaxe Privatskole and involve two school classes over a limited time period of one
week. The goal is to assess both usability and impact from a practical, pedagogical, and
organisational perspective.
The following aspects will be evaluated during the MVP test, and form the basis for the test
in DS­II, where the solutions’ real­world impact will be assessed and further improvements
identified.
• Functionality: Is the physical mobile makerspace operational and easy to use for
teachers and students and can the developed solutions for the 3D printers optimise
the user experience and usability of equipment to avoid bottlenecks?
• Teaching: Can the developed curriculum function as the foundation for a profes­
sional development course, where teachers gain confidence in facilitating makerspace
153
==PAGE:167==
activities and prepare them for integrating it in their daily practice to teach Technol­
ogy Comprehension?
• Implementation and logistics: Is is possible to move and place the container at
the school and will the real life interaction with the makerspace help convince the
school in investing in the makerspace?
154
==PAGE:168==
7
Descriptive Study 2
Descriptive Study II aims to explore the practical and contextual conditions surrounding
the implementation and use of the Gnist Makerspace in specific school setting. While
DS­I focused on identifying general needs, barriers and design requirements, DS­II in­
vestigates how the proposed solutions interact with real­world environments. Through
observations, interviews, and feedback from MVP implementations, this phase seeks to
validate assumptions, uncover new challenges, and inform further refinements of the so­
lutions before final evaluation.
Overview of Methods and Activities
The following methods were applied in DS­II to investigate how the Gnist Makerspace
functions in a real school setting to uncover unforeseen challenges, user experiences,
and contextual dynamics.
• MVP implementation at Gladsaxe Privatskole
• Teaching sessions with 5th and 7th grade
• Empirical data collection
• Evaluation of technical and logistical performance
Research Questions Covered in this Phase
RQ3: How can the developed solution(s) be assessed and validated to strengthen
the integration of the Gnist Education concept and what significance will it have for
the startup.
155
==PAGE:169==
7.1
Test Setup
During the development of the solutions, a close contact was established with Gladsaxe
Privatskole who agreed to being part of a final Minimum Viable Product (MVP) test setup
to aid the development and assess the solutions. This created the possibility of moving the
entire makerspace container to Gladsaxe Privatskole, ensuring a real­world test scenario
within a school context.
The MVP test would be conducted over the span of one week, with the primary user groups
being 5th and 7th grade and their two respective teachers. Insights would be gathered,
using multiple data collection methods, to answer a list of hypotheses that was created
with certain success criteria in mind.
Certain requirements should be met in order to conduct the MVP test. These include
shimming the container with tiles and other materials, and making sure that power can
be provided at the chosen location. Furthermore, certain software restrictions and GDPR
rules should be considered by streamlining administrative access. This ensures that stu­
dents do not need to download or create personalised accounts in order to explore and
experience what a makerspace has to offer. Additional safety measures should likewise
be taken to ensure that the makerspace adheres to safety standards, such as using safe
materials and ventilation when needed.
7.1.1
Purpose with the Test
The overall purpose of the test is to gain enough insights to answer the third and final
research question (RQ3): How can the developed solution(s) be assessed and validated
to strengthen the integration of the Gnist Education concept and what significance will
it have for the startup. More specifically, the test will be used to evaluate how the Gnist
Education concept will function in a real­world elementary school context, to reveal certain
strengths and weaknesses.
Insights will be gained by testing a set of hypothesis, which have been created on the
basis of the three developed focus areas: physical makerspace, curriculum, and opera­
tional business standpoint, which can be seen in Table 7.1. The hypothesis reflect the
expectations regarding functionality, usability, and educational impact of the makerspace
and related workflows. Corresponding success criteria from the design specification have
been tied with each hypothesis to evaluate whether these expectations are met. The suc­
cess criteria was first described in DS­I but have since been adapted to better fit with the
developed solutions and the specific testing scenario.

7.1.2
Test Plan
To evaluate the formulated hypotheses and assess the impact of the developed solutions,
a mixed­method approach will be used to gather empirical data. This combines both qual­
itative and quantitative methods, specifically selected to provide insights for the different
aspects of the test. Figure 7.1 illustrates an overview of the planned activities. The fol­
lowing sections will describe the approach of each method and the collected data can be
found in appendix DSII.
Observation
Throughout the testing period, observations will be employed as a qualitative method to
document student engagement and interactions with the makerspace. Field notes will be
taken to continuously record contextual insights, behavioural patterns, and spontaneous
reactions. These observations will focus on how students engage with the space and
activities, including their navigation of the physical space, flow within the container, and
157
==PAGE:171==
approach to the various activities. At the end of each day, the team will conduct internal
debriefings to reflect on the day’s events. During these sessions, preliminary field notes
will be elaborated upon with general impressions, discussions, and any emergent themes
to keep an eye out for.
Measures
Quantitative measurable data will be collected to supplement the qualitative insights and
allow for a systematic evaluation of the technical performance and material usage in the
makerspace. Throughout the test period, data from 3D printers will be logged through
SimplyPrint. Additionally, any failed or incomplete prints due to student mistakes in the
model will be documented before modifying and reprinting them, to assess the usability
and completeness of student guides. Finally, the time to conduct the laser cutting activity
will be measured, to quantify the active student time in the makerspace.
Interview
Semi­structured interviews will be conducted with selected teachers and school manage­
ment. These interviews aim to explore perceptions of the makerspace’s educational value,
usability, and potential for integration into existing practice. Additionally, the teachers will
be interviewed to assess their level of confidence in facilitating the activities, assuming
the curriculum was used in a professional development course. The broader institutional
value and the impact for Gnist will be assessed by interviewing the school principal.
Survey
A survey will be administered to students in 7th grade to gather data in their overall experi­
ence and perception of the makerspace and the curriculum. To minimise bias and ensure
authenticity in student responses, the survey will be completed independently, without the
presence or influence of the project team. The Questionnaire will be designed to assess
students’ engagement in activities, perceived learning outcomes, and attitudes toward the
makerspace. The survey will primarily consist of open­ended questions to allow students
to provide nuance to their responses.
Figure 7.1: Overview of the planned activities in the test week.
Plan for 7th grade: Design Your Dream Bedroom
The plan for 7th grade curriculum have been developed through PS, as a possibility for a
modular curriculum easily adaptable based on specific context. In the curriculum, the stu­
dents will design their own dream bedroom through a set of activities, including 3D mod­
158
==PAGE:172==
elling walls and floor for laser cutting and furniture for 3D printing. Examples of students’
results are shown in Figure 7.2. The students will be presented with guides, scaffolding
their process and ensure possibility for students to work in their own pace. The full lesson
plan can be found in appendix C and the guides can be found in appendix E.
Figure 7.2: Examples of the end results produced by 7th grade students.
Plan for 5th grade: Develop a Class Chess Game
During the test period, four lessons are planned for 5th grade. This curriculum includes
the entire class to develop a chess game collaboratively. The full plan can be found in
appendix F and the student material can be found in appendix G. The curriculum was in­
troduced with an inspirational slide to show different design possibilities for chess pieces.
Afterwards, students were paired and the different tasks were distributed, defining which
chess piece they should design including requirements for dimensions. The students
would then brainstorm different design ideas, analyse the shapes required for 3D mod­
elling, and digitalise the design in TinkerCAD. As an extra activity, the students could
make a personalised model that would be printed, if time allowed for it.
159
==PAGE:173==
Figure 7.3: The end results produced by 5th grade students.
7.2
Analysis of Test Results
To assess each hypothesis, the findings and results will be analysed in the following sec­
tions. This will include the insights gained through multiple methods, and each section is
focused on the individual hypotheses.
7.2.1
Securing Equipment During Transportation of Container (H1)
As moving the makerspace is crucial for recognising a successful MVP that has the ability
to be deployed at different locations, the test needed to involve the transportation of the
container from DTU to Gladsaxe Privatskole. In addition, the test should show the pos­
sibility of moving the container with the needed equipment, as the concept is designed
to be operational soon after deployment at the school. This meant that the solution for
securing the equipment with straps and foam developed in PS would have to be tested to
determine if it would be viable or not in the future.
The test was created to show the state of the equipment before and after the container
were transported from DTU to Gladsaxe Privatskole (as shown in Figure 7.4). Through
observation, it was possible to evaluate the impact of the solution and to determine if the
equipment was moved and/or damaged as a result of the transportation.
160
==PAGE:174==
Figure 7.4: The container at DTU before transportation (Left) and at Gladsaxe Privatskole after
transportation (right).
Observations of the container and more specifically the equipment after transportation
revealed two interesting findings. Firstly, that the straps used to prevent the equipment
from moving during transport were successful as there were no sign of movement at all
when inspecting the container after transportation as seen in Figure 7.4. Secondly, that
the foam placed under each piece of equipment was sufficient at absorbing the vibration
during transport, meaning that the build­in auto calibration programs were all that was
needed to prepare the equipment for usage.
In general, this test confirms Hypothesis 1, listed in Table 7.1. However, since the trans­
portation was only a 15 minute drive from DTU to Gladsaxe Privatskole, further investi­
gation would be recommended to see how the solution would be affected during longer
trips.
7.2.2
The Influence of Guides on Successful Digital Fabrication (H2)
To ensure that students can successfully complete activities in a makerspace, it is essen­
tial to provide clear instructions outlining both the process and the intended outcomes.
The format and degree of structure within these guides can vary significantly depending
on the complexity of the task ans the students’ prior experience. In this test, 7th grade stu­
dents was given both a detailed, step­by­step guide and a more open­ended guide. The
step­by­step guide was used for modelling the walls and floor intended for laser cutting,
which have strict requirements and specific steps to ensure the model can be assembled.
The furniture modelling activity was supported by a more open­ended guide, giving stu­
dents greater creative freedom. The time constraints of the test did not allow for iterative
experimentation when using the laser cutter.
To assess the extent to which students were able to follow the provided guides, the amount
of models that required modifications for successful manufacturing was noted, see ap­
pendix DSII4.1. This was done for both 5th and 7th grade students, although only 7th
grade received the guides. For 3D printing tasks, this evaluation was carried out sys­
tematically, whereas for laser cutting tasks it was not formally documented as most of
the models required editing and therefore took a lot longer than expected. Among the
7th grade students the results indicated that most students were able to create printable
models. Out of 85 models, only 9 required adjustments before they could be printed. In
contrast, the outcome was significantly different for the 5th grade students, where 7 out
of 23 models required adjustments. These students did not get a written guide but had an
161
==PAGE:175==
introductory exercise focused on analysing geometric shapes. Common issues included
floating objects, thin elements, and excessively fine details.
While there appears to be a correlation between having a guide and the success rate
of the models, it cannot be concluded that the guide alone was the determining factor.
There is a significant developmental difference between 5th and 7th grade, particularly in
terms of spatial comprehension and basic computer skills. Both group had participated
in prototyping sessions using TinkerCad prior to the test, however only the 7th graders
had previously seen their models physically printed, which may have contributed to their
better understanding of design constraints in 3D modelling.
To gain further insights into the effectiveness of the guides, the 7th grade students were
asked to reflect on both types in the final survey, see appendix DSII2. Overall, students
expressed limited enthusiasm for the guides, and several reported to not having used
them at all. This aligns with the observations of students approach to the activities, where
many students were seen requesting assistance before attempting independently. Most
of the times, the answer could be found in the guide. Only one student explicitly mentioned
appreciating having the guide physically printed and made active use of it. Most students
stated that they could complete the task without consulting the guide, which may have
caused the numerous issues with the models for the laser cutter, as this required students
to follow specific steps.
Based on the results of this test, it is not possible to conclusively confirm Hypothesis 2
(see Table 7.1), as only a minority of students actually made use of the guides. The find­
ings suggests that written instructions may not be the most effective support for students
in this context, and alternative formats may be worth exploring in future iterations. Fur­
ther, if specific steps are necessary for successful manufacturing, some sort of guide is
necessary, or students should have time to discover the steps themselves.
7.2.3
Student Motivation and Engagement with Activities (H3)
Makerspace activities are generally known for their possibility for personalised entry points,
which provides differentiated participation opportunities and strengthens student motiva­
tion. However, this will only be the case if students are able to complete the activities
within the allocated time.
The student engagement and motivation was assessed though observations and in the
final survey for 7th grade, see appendix DSII2. The general impression was positive as
both 5th and 7th grade students continued working on their tasks during breaks. Figure 7.5
shown some of the students deeply engaged in the activities. Further, some of the 7th
grade students was continuing to work on their furniture models after school. Especially
one student stood out. The teacher described him as someone who generally takes a long
time starting on assignments, and he did not bring his computer on the first day. However,
he still participated in the activities by helping peers and thereby gaining experience with
the activities. After school, he created all of his models and the next day, he happily
explained how he had made everything. In the 7th grade survey, most students described
the curriculum as fun, especially highlighting how it was a different way to learn. Further,
they wished for more time, not because they did not finish, but because they liked the
theme and wanted to work more experimental with the laser cutter.
162
==PAGE:176==
Figure 7.5: Students deeply engaged with activities.
To assess the time spent in the makerspace, it was planned to measure how long it would
take to complete the laser cutting activity. However, due to the many issues with preparing
the files, this was not measured. The first student who started this activity spent approx­
imately an hour in the container, delaying others from accessing the laser cutter. It was
expected that the task would take 10 minutes to complete, and would require further test­
ing of the activity to make a better estimate. To ensure all students would end up with a
model of their bedroom, most of the files was produced after school hours by the team,
except three sets, so students could still experience the process in smaller groups.
Based in these insights, it is partially possible to confirm Hypothesis 3, described in Ta­
ble 7.1. Even though the test is limited to two classes, the general observations of stu­
dents’ engagement and motivation corresponds to the expectations based on the research
in DS­I. However, it was not possible to confirm that the activities could be completed by
the students within the allocated time as the team had to do most of the laser cutting
without students.
7.2.4
3D Printer Efficiency (H4)
Efficiency of the equipment used in the makerspace is closely related to how usable is is,
as stated in DS­I. More specifically, equipment bottlenecks and downtime should be min­
imised in order to improve the general user experience and utilisation rate. As 3D printers
are the most likely source of bottleneck due to slow print times, a swapmod solution com­
bined with SimplyPrint was developed and described in PS. To finally test this solution in
a real­world school setting, the A1 mini swapmod combination should be compared to a
different 3D printer in order to determine the overall impact the solution would have for the
final concept. This should reveal certain recommendations or crucial findings associated
with selecting the correct set of equipment used in a school context.
Two sets of A1 mini swapmod combos and four P1S 3D printers was used to conduct the
test at Gladsaxe Privatskole. Each of the six printers was connected to SimplyPrint, allow­
ing both optimisation of the workflow and the ability to collect data measurements about
the usage and efficiency of each printer (see appendix DSII1). These data measurement
was collected on the fifth day of the test week and were a combination of the 3D mod­
163
==PAGE:177==
els created by 5th and 7th grade. Finally, two stacked bar charts were created, based on
these measurements, to show the efficiency and filament usage of each individual printer,
which is displayed in Figure 7.6, and Figure 7.7.
Figure 7.6: Individual 3D printer status showing number of print jobs performed by each printer
over the duration of the test week (see appendix DSII1.1).
Figure 7.6 clearly shows that both A1 minis used in the test completed far more prints
than all the P1S’s combined over the test duration. Additionally, the A1 minis had a higher
average success rate of 90.49% against the P1S’s 75.18% (see appendix DSII1.1). The
reason for this difference can overall be explained by the fact that one of the four P1S’s
broke down multiple times during the test, lowering the overall average success rate.
However, as seen in Figure 7.6, the P1S’s were the only printers that experienced direct
print failures, opposed to the A1 minis which were only stopped due to cancelled jobs
or user errors. These findings suggest that the A1 minis is generally more efficient and
reliable. However, to understand the full picture, filament usage was also analysed (see
Figure 7.7 and appendix DSII1.2) as the P1S’s allowed for larger prints.
Figure 7.7: Individual 3D printer status showing filament usage, divided into their respective
colours, over the duration of the test week (see appendix DSII1.2).
164
==PAGE:178==
Due to the greater size of the print plate dimensions on the P1S’s, larger prints could
be achieved. This is indicated in Figure 7.7 by the amount of filament used by each
printer, where the P1S’s are more closely matched up against the A1 minis. Looking at
the collective filament usage of the A1 minis versus the P1S’s reveal a total difference of
only 5 grams (see appendix DSII1.2). These numbers indicate that the A1 minis with an
autoprint solution, (such as the swapmod and SimplyPrint combination) that allow printing
overnight, can double its usability rate compared to a P1S printer. This usability rate,
might even be higher compared to the P1S’s, than indicated by these numbers. This can
be explained by certain individual print jobs being combined into larger files, due to time
constraints, and were run by the P1S’s. These larger files meant that the overall usage of
specifically the P1S’s were being inflated by using manual work combining multiple print
jobs. If the print jobs had not been combined, the utilisation rate might had indicated an
even bigger difference between the printers.
Overall, the test confirms Hypothesis 4, that an A1 mini printer with the autoprint solution,
is generally more efficient compared to a P1S printer. This furthermore, proves that sim­
ilar solutions should be considered in order to improve the overall user experience and
usability rate of the 3D printer in a makerspace setting.
7.2.5
The Makerspace as a Welcoming Environment (H5)
In order for the makerspace to be a desired expansion of the schools current teaching
methods, the space needs to feel welcoming for all, to foster inclusivity and spark curiosity.
It is important, that it does not feel like a pure production space, but as a place you want to
be. Otherwise, it will not be integrated in teaching or empower both student and teacher
agency.
To test if the space feels welcoming, the general attitude towards the container will be
observed, as well as their interest in what is happening inside the container. It was clear,
that many students was interested in the container, especially the 2nd graders, who came
into the container at all possible times as shown in Figure 7.8. Further, the makerspace
attracted a lot of positive attention from both teachers, students, and parents. During the
testing period, 9th grade was conducting their science exam, where one group asked if
they could print a blue mussel shell for their presentation, and the censor asked when
we could come to her school with the container. This indicates, that there is a general
positive interest in the makerspace.
Figure 7.8: Students observing the equipment in use. Especially the A1 mini with the swapmod
got a lot of attention.
To further support the observations of interactions with the space, and to get a deeper
165
==PAGE:179==
understanding of what makes the container welcoming, a few questions was included in
the survey for 7th grade, see appendix DSII2. Here, the students mentioned the indoor
climate as a positive aspect. Even though the space is small, it does not feel confined
due to the natural light from the windows and the good air quality. Further, the students
mentioned the modern interior as a factor that made it feel more like a home and less like
a workshop. The only negative comment the student had about the space, was that it was
not bigger, as they wanted to have more equipment available.
7.2.6
Flow in the Container (H6)
Due to the limited space in the container, it is essential to ensure a smooth flow of stu­
dents to enhance the user experience. To support this, the interior, spatial layout, and
curriculum should be designed in a way, that allows all equipment to be accessed and
used simultaneously. The PS shows how this layout changed through multiple iterations
of the container to optimise the general flow. The curriculum has likewise been designed
in a way to encourage students to start on different tasks, to distribute the load on the
equipment.
To test the general flow inside the container in a school context, multiple sets of obser­
vations were made at Gladsaxe Privatskole. These observations was made during the
lessons were 5th and 7th grade used the makerspace equipment inside the container as
seen in Figure 7.9.
Figure 7.9: Container makerspace in use, multiple students interacting with multiple equipment
simultaneously.
Although the students had the opportunity to start on both activities (furniture and walls),
the majority chose to start with creating walls and floors so they could use the laser cutter.
This created a bottleneck for the laser cutter, due to the faulty designs, which ultimately
caused long queues to be formed. Additionally, due to the general excitement from other
students, specifically the 2nd graders, meant that the container at times could be over­
crowded which caused disturbance in the overall flow. Even with these complications, the
general observations highlighted, that both the 3D printers and laser cutters were used
simultaneously, as seen in Figure 7.9, which confirms Hypothesis 9. However, further
166
==PAGE:180==
exploration should be done in the future to determine if alternative interior and curriculum
designs can improve flow even further.
7.2.7
Value Creation and Confidence in Integration (H7)
The Gnist Education concept, encompassing all three elements tested at the school,
needs to be a viable solution, that the school will find attractive to adopt. In relation,
that is why, it is important to assess how the value is perceived by the teachers, students,
and school management, but also be the parents hereafter. Further, in order to be able
to implement the makerspace in a school setting, the teachers needs to feel confident
in facilitating the activities. The value­proposition from the To­Be2 lean business model
canvas, relies heavily on the interaction and the teachers over­all excitement, understand
and confidence of makerspace­led teaching. The teachers have the possibility to get
professional training directly from Gnist Education as a part of their confidence building.
With confidence comes also th freedom to explore other paths, that can lead to new ideas
creating own personal learning materials.
However, not only the teachers showed the appreciation, but also the students, and es­
pecially the students, that were not directly involved in the activities we facilitated, which
is also noted in H6. The students excitement holds immense power, than what might be
obersavble at first sight. Even more so, that the excitement from the teachers is a direct
reflection on how the students are perceiving the Gnist Education makerspace. Parents
equally reflects this excitement, which is again paramount to consider, when Gnist Edu­
cation is planning for innitial implementation
The overall perception of the the entire concept including the makerspace container’s
value have been observed through informal conversations with students and teachers,
as well as observations of their general interactions with the makerspace. Occasionally
parents have also visited the container, and have in general shown excitement to the pro­
posed solution to the concept. Here, it was clear that teachers could see the educational
value of the container, and multiple teachers asked if it was possible to get the container
back in the future, so other students also could experience the makerspace in action. Fur­
ther, numerous student, not involved in the curricula­activities, expressed a desire to also
try to use it actively as a part of their regular classes, a few even mentioning it as unfair
that they did not have planned lessons. However, not all teachers was equally excited
about the makerspace. One teacher indicated her disapproval of the concept, and did not
show any interest in the activities.
In a more formal evaluation, an interview with the two teachers involved in the test was
planned to evaluate the curriculum, value, and future possibilities. However, due to un­
foreseen circumstances this was not possible to conduct during the test. Instead, the
planned interview questions was answered over email, see appendix DSII3. Both teach­
ers could see the value of integrating the makerspace, noting the students learned a lot
more than with regular teaching methods, as the practical experience enhanced their un­
derstanding. However, in order to compete with current teaching material providers, it is
necessary that the available curriculum will cover all learning objectives within a subject.
After the test, both teachers emphasised that they would feel confident in facilitating the
activities themselves, but would require more experience with the software and especially
with the equipment. Furthermore, they noted that the two curricula would be beneficial
for a professional development course, where the 5th grade activities would be useful
to quickly learn the software, and the 7th grade curriculum would be useful for reflecting
upon the underlying pedagogies.
167
==PAGE:181==
Based on these findings, it is possible to confirm Hypothesis 7, described in Table 7.1.
Even though some teachers did not see the value of the makerspace, the general percep­
tion was positive. Further, the teachers reported feeling confident in facilitating teaching
activities, however, further testing is needed to assess whether they can be fully in charge
of the teaching or if co­teaching options are necessary.
7.2.8
Perceived value - elaborating on the pricing strategy (H8)
Coming from the value creation and touching base on the perceived value of the offering,
that were tested. The pricing strategy can be extended from the pricing strategy, that
were used to prior to the validation, to competitive­based pricing. Value­based pricing is
considered to be the best and most reflective pricing strategy to consider. The perceived
value, will for the users naturally fall in some hypothetical line of reference to the offerings
of substitutors and their pricing, thus being able to more closely articulate the right price for
them, rather the price, internally created price, boasted by the owners biased connection
to the offering.
The school principal were proposed in order to confirm the perceived value creation of
our offering and validate the economical aspects of it. With considerations to their current
payments to curricula, and the perceived value, acknowledgements and critique points,
the principal stated, that their is a bit of way before, it can be considered as a stand­alone
offering, however if the curriculum were of the same fidelity as what is on the market
currently, the container could be a complimenting add­on to schools such as Gladsaxe
Privatskole. A point, worth noting, that also came up, was the notion of the perceived ad­
ditional value, that Gladaxe Privatskole for good reasons cannot recognise. The school
principal were not fully convinced, that the additional service and tendering to the equip­
ment was worthwhile, hinting at the extra cost associated with such a service, and implying
the ever so obvious question, ”how easy can this be substituted, if space available for the
equipment were not factor to be considered”. Shifting the perspective back again on the
perceived value creation.
In terms of actual pricing, the school principle started out by outlining the typical prices of
the competitors (Alinea, Gyldendal), that were also covered in the PS. He mentions, that a
curricula covering all classes from grade 4­9 in respect to the science subjects, including
’Natur og Teknik’ alignes with a price close to DKK 15.000 per year , and denotes, that
if Gnist Education were a standardised offering on a learning, where curricula could be
paid for for a year, the container could be an addition, totalling to the full amount of DKK
15.000. Furthermoe, he continues and mentions, that there might be times of the year,
where the offering is not as applicable than others, e.g. June as all the 9th graders are
busy reading for the exam. Gnist Education must be aware of the total number of weeks
of 40 weeks can shrink to 35. This is worth noting, as it limits the actual deployment time
for the container at the school.
On a last note, while team were packing up the container on the last friday, Martin Steno
and Hans Ubbe Ebbesen from the Gnist Education Advisory board paid us a visit at Glad­
saxe Privatskole. They both showed excitement to what had been achieved.
Following the test at Gladsaxe Privatskole the previous business model iteration involving
co­creation with Silas Korndrup and the research from DS­I, the platform business model
canvas modelled as a last iteration of the business model development, see Figure 7.10.
The platform business model is different from the lean canvas, as it deals with consumers
and producers and not customers.
168
==PAGE:182==
Figure 7.10: Business Model Canvas ­ Platform Canvas To­Be.
The platform canvas delivers a new perspective and a way forward for Gnist, that might
be valuable to explore further.
