==PAGE:184==
8
Discussion and Future Work
In the beginning of this project, Gnist Education had just been registered as a startup with
the basic concept defined through previous student projects. The focus was on promot­
ing Technology Comprehension in elementary schools by providing a mobile makerspace.
Compared to current makerspaces, this would solve the accessibility limitations and en­
sure seamless integration with school curricula. However, this was still on a concept
basis, with just initial plans outlined.
Throughout this project the Gnist Education concept and makerspace have been trans­
formed on multiple levels. More specifically, the physical container has evolved from a
conceptual design into a functional makerspace ready for student use. Furthermore, the
curriculum has undergone significant development, to allow both the students and teach­
ers to feel empowered and overall build confidence in engaging in makerspace activities.
Finally, on a business level, Gnist Education has progressed from an early stage business
concept to an validated viable business model with a clear and actionable plan. These
overall changes and development on the three focus areas will be discussed further in
the following sections.
8.1
Creating a Successful Detailed Mobile Makerspace for a
Classroom Setting
When designing a makerspace, multiple factors, such as choice of equipment, interior de­
sign, and spatial configuration, should be carefully considered, as they can significantly
impact the educational outcome, user engagement, and overall functionality. These find­
ings were made clear in DS­I where multiple studies argued the importance of having a
balanced and effective selection of makerspace equipment that supports group use. This
was furthermore made clear by teachers that in general value user­friendly equipment that
supports pedagogical scaffolding and a wide area of learning possibilities. In addition, the
interior design and spatial configuration should create a safe and welcoming space that
help empower both students and teachers and foster creativity for all. Along with these
considerations, certain barriers were also presented that a makerspace should overcome
if hoping to be successful. Specifically workflow and equipment bottlenecks were shown
to have significant impact on overall usability and efficiency.
These barriers were especially apparent for the 3D printer, because of the slow manufac­
turing speed. The 3D printer was for that reason also chosen as a key focus area for the
development, as it indicated most areas of improvements. By combining the modified 3D
printer (A1 mini) with the implementation of a service platform (SimplyPrint), allowed for
certain automated features, which was shown to have positive impact on the overall us­
ability, and user experience, throughout the makerspace. This was validated, both in the
development phase, and again in the MVP test in DS­II, where the difference in utilisation
rate between the two 3D printers (A1 mini and P1S) was especially apparent. However,
by focusing on one specific type of equipment also meant that other potentially impactful
tools or equipment might have been completely overlooked. Moreover, it would be inter­
esting to investigate if similar solutions to the combination of Swapmod and SimplyPrint,
might exist for other equipment, and show the same level of overall improvements. For­
tunately, as the mobile makerspace is designed to be modular, exploring different setups
and equipment is relatively easy, as reflected in the different makerspace iterations. This
171
==PAGE:185==
allows for new opportunities to be explored in the future iterations.
As the development in this project was focused on improving the overall usability through
efficient equipment, also meant that some areas, regarding the students overall learn­
ing opportunities, might have been overlooked. An example of this, was shown with the
integration between Bambu Lab and SimplyPrint, as the chosen slicer could not be down­
loaded on the students personal computers. This meant that some hands­on experience
were left out, in order to streamline and create a more efficient integration of the curriculum
activities throughout the user tests. It can be argued that future iterations of makerspace
activities, should try to incorporate the process of slicing the 3D models, allowing students
to gain a deeper understanding of the entire 3D printing process. This might even be pos­
sible to incorporate in the next iteration of the mobile makerspace. As SimplyPrint are
currently developing a cloud based slicer that is compatible with Bambu Lab 3D printers.
It should be stated that the mobile makerspace developed in this project, was a first edi­
tion, with the purpose of validating the Gnist Education concept in a real schools setting.
This meant that certain solutions described throughout, would benefit from additional and
future iterations, to further improve makerspace usability and safety. For example, venti­
lation cabinets for the 3D printers were originally planned to be implemented in the mak­
erspace, see appendix PS11.1. However, as they are not required when using PLA, and
due to time constraints, they were left out of the final iteration. They would however be a
valuable addition to a makerspace, as they allow for better print quality, different types of
material use, and provide more safety in a school setting. Likewise, a more permanent
solution for securing equipment during transportation, would be beneficial in a future it­
eration, allowing a faster preparation. In general, further exploration and evaluation are
needed to refine the mobile makerspace to increase the opportunities within it.
8.2
Empowering Teachers to Integrate Makerspace Activities
in Practice
For makerspaces to be meaningfully integrated into everyday teaching practice, it is es­
sential that the teachers feel confident and capable of facilitating maker­based activities.
As identified in DS­I, the teacher­student relationship plays a central role in motivating
students and enabling differentiated instructions. Several teachers emphasised that the
regular classroom teacher is best positioned to adapt activities to the specific needs of
their class. However, external teachers still provide valuable contributions by bridging
real­world perspectives with school context, making the curriculum more meaningful for
the students. This dual perspective suggests a hybrid model, where a Gnist employee
support the regular classroom teacher in using the equipment, while the teacher remains
primarily responsible for planning, adapting and facilitating the activities.
To support this approach, the developed curriculum in PS is intentionally modular and
adaptable, allowing teachers to tailor activities to their context. It explicitly targets the
identified barriers for integrating makerspaces in teaching practice. By providing a struc­
tured, yet flexible framework with embedded scaffolding, clear links to design models, and
opportunities for differentiation, the curriculum holds strong potential as a tool in a pro­
fessional development setting. It also supports the development of a professional “maker
teacher” identity, which is an important motivational driver for adopting new technologies
and pedagogies. The curriculum itself is intended as a part of the professional develop­
ment course and designed to strengthen both technical knowledge and confidence in the
pedagogical approach in maker activities. A central question is therefore whether the de­
veloped curriculum can build the foundation for the professional development course and
172
==PAGE:186==
whether the practical involvement of teachers in the MVP test improved their confidence
in facilitating makerspace activities.
Findings from DS­II indicate that teachers recognised the value of using the makerspace
as an alternative teaching method, and students responded positively to the activities. A
teacher pointed to the practical work with the subject theory have enhanced the students
understanding of the theory, even though they might not even have realised the learning
aspect of the activities. Further, the learning related to trial and error is especially for
students to understand the overall picture. This was revealed in the final test, where
students expressed, that they did not understand the purpose with all of the steps in the
written guide. However, this approach requires more time as students have to make many
iterations. When planning the curriculum activities, it is therefore important, to reflect upon
the available time frame and the possible learning outcome.
The teacher confidence level is essential for successful facilitation of the activities. The
two teachers involved in the MVP test had also previously been a part of a workshop,
where they stated their prior confidence level as makerspace facilitators as low. Their
feedback after the test revealed significant progress. Both teachers stated, that they now
felt confident in facilitating the activities in the classroom, although they would still need
support with the technical equipment. This was likely due to the fact that the teachers
did not get any experience with the equipment themselves but stayed in the class to sup­
port the students. One of the teachers also noted a desire for further experience with
TinkerCAD before taking full ownership of similar lessons. Both teachers agreed that a
professional development course would still be necessary to fully prepare them for inte­
grating makerspace activities, even though the practical experience during the test was
enough to strengthen their overall confidence in their own capabilities.
In terms of curriculum usability as a framework in the professional development course,
the teachers noted that the 5th grade curriculum on designing chess pieces, was concrete
and immediately applicable, making it ideal as an introductory tool for TinkerCAD and
3D printing. In contrast, the more complex curriculum for 7th grade on designing their
dream bedroom, was seen as a pedagogically rich and well­suited for reflecting in subject
integration and makerspace didactics.
Based on the development and testing process, the dream bedroom project is consid­
ered to have strong potential as the foundation for a professional development course.
However, since it has not yet been tested in that specific context, further exploration and
evaluation are needed to validate its effectiveness as a training format.
8.3
The Business Model and Implementation Plan - Coherence
and Viability
The role of the business model is important to consider for any business hoping to suc­
ceed in delivering, their value proposition to their target customer or user. Often times a
business will go through several iterations, and still not end up at figuring out, what the
missing piece to the puzzle might be. In relation to Gnist Education, the business model
had up until the start of this project seen changes to it’s value proposition, however only
with smaller incremental changes.
The business model has throughout this study seen four iterations with the use of the
lean business model canvas and the Platform canvas. One of the main reason for using
a business model canvas to develop the business model, was to have a strategic outline
of all the elements, that encompasses a business model, varying from the more typical;
173
==PAGE:187==
’Problem’, ’Value Proposition’ or ’customer’, to the more niche, that often takes a more
thorough analysis and broader perspective, e.g. Unfair Advantage, ’Early Adopters’ or
’Existing Alternatives’. By covering all the elements, a better understanding of the busi­
ness situation can be achieved and levers for change are easier discovered. The DS­I
informed the business model development with valuable insights to how Gnist Education
could make a good argument to leverage the already stated platform­aspect.
As of yet, the core offering has been centred around the container and it’s core offering
of providing the space to those who need it. However the space in general might not be
Gnist Education’s biggest problem to solve. To further clarify the cost associated with the
space itself is so significant, that more obstacles are appearing on the road ahead. From
the test at Gladsaxe Privatskole, it became more evident than ever, that the container and
the makerspace inside attracted the wanted attention from both in and outside the school
premises. The interactions from users, offers Gnist the uniqueness, that others don’t
have, and for that reason it is a stronghold and a core part of the identity tied to Gnist.
The recognition an the uniqueness factor plays a huge role, especially in the beginning,
where setting yourself apart from the common and established substitutes can drive the
business forward and open up new possibilities. However, the cost associated is also very
high in comparison to the offerings of competitors, making our chances at succeeding with
just the makerspace as the core offering limited.
The curriculum and teacher training modules are offerings, that ties well together with
the makerspace container. The teacher training can be tier­based, offering the chance to
customers, that wish to develop their skill level to come back into the loop. The curriculum
is there as the element that binds it all together. The curriculum is also the element, that
currently has no variable costs tied to it, but is purely fixed. That means, that when it
is developed, Gnist can start to monetize on the assets, that they’re are not physically
working on. Realising this, will be an essential element for Gnist, and could be the make
or break point. In a situation like this, the container and the training modules become
add­ons, however still defining elements of the entire concept. Taking it a step further,
Gnist Education, can deploy its notion as platform, not only in the physical makerspace
sense, but also in a business logic sense.
Adopting a platform structure, although difficult, will be a disruptive factor in the current
elementary school ecosystem among teaching material portals. Leveraging co­creation
process on both the consumer and complementor side can potentially define a turning
point of how teachers adopt new teaching materials in the engagement with the platform
offering, but at the same time, takes care of all the busy work of developing, while Gnist
Education facilitates and orchestrate new collaborative partnerships and manage the dis­
tribution channel. Furthermore the platform business model also gives Gnist Education
the opportunity to subsidise one side of the platform, which can be a strategic clever
choice, when the consumer side has less to offer than the complementor side.
To conclude the aspect of the business model, Gnist Education should consider different
types of revenue models, where the platform business model can offer an alternative
to existing thinking. Cost and revenue streams, do not necessarily have to be linear,
when two­ or multi­sided markets can leverage both sides of the value chain to deliver the
primary offer of good teaching.
8.4
Assessment of the Combined Solutions for Gnist Education
The combined solution developed for the Gnist Education concept integrates three core
components that were designed to compliment one another, thereby forming a holistic
174
==PAGE:188==
solution. This solution provide a framework that offers a flexible learning environment
to meet different user needs for both students and teachers, as well as being financially
sustainable.
Throughout this project a mobile makerspace container was developed along with sup­
porting curriculum, and a business plan that support the implementation of the concept.
Currently, one functional makerspace container has been successfully built and tested
throughout this project. To assess the implementation and impact of the solution, a MVP
test was conducted in collaboration with Gladssaxe Privatskole, with overwhelmingly pos­
itive feedback. This test demonstrated both the practicality of a mobile makerspace sit­
uated in a container and the strong potential to foster Technology Comprehension for
students. These results present clear opportunities in the continuation of the concept and
to develop and refine the solution even further.
The makerspace face some limitations in the spacial layout, making it challenging for all
students and teachers to make meaningful use of it. This is however a necessary sacrifice
for keeping it mobile. Specifically the mobility of the makerspace, is what makes the
combined solution valuable, at is it easy for schools to gain access to expensive equipment
without having large up­front costs, making it a viable solutions for schools with limited
budgets. In contrast, the mobility also means that the makerspace will only be available
for a limited time, giving logistical challenges for making the most out of it. This sets a
limiting factor for the possibilities of curricula, however, it is possible to begin the digital
fabrication before the container arrives and limit the time to production.
8.5
Limitations of Study
Although the project successfully met its primary objectives, several limitations should be
acknowledged, as they can have general implication on the findings and development
throughout the study.
8.5.1
Development and Sampling Limitations
First of all, the limited time frame of a master thesis can be seen as a general limiting
factor due to the size and overall complexity of the project. Because this project follows
the research structure of a type 5 (Blessing & Chakrabarti, 2009), means that the project is
restraint to current understanding of the scope when starting the development of solutions,
whereas a type 7 research will continuously integrate new knowledge. This means that
the development of solutions is limited by the initial findings, even though new insights
might occur along the way.
Developing a makerspace involve complex systems that require iterative testing and long
term observation. In addition, the findings from the literature and empirical studies, greatly
impact and help shape the overall design specification. The requirements and criteria,
might have looked different had other field experts and stakeholders been involved in the
DS­I analysis. Since the requirements and criteria aim to address challenges and oppor­
tunities specific to integrate a makerspace in a schools setting, they provide a valuable
starting point for the Gnist Education concept. However, it is important to note that the
findings used are not universally applicable, and that certain modifications or adjustments
might be necessary to suit different contexts or environments.
Furthermore, the limited sample size during the development can likewise be seen as a
limiting factor. Field experts, stakeholders, teachers and students that helped with the
development and testing were limited in numbers, and might not reflect the needs of all
175
==PAGE:189==
users that intent on using similar solutions. It should also be noted that the Gnist Education
concept developed in this project is created to suit the Danish school sector. This is
reflected by the interviews, user tests and co­creation methods used for the development,
as certain touchpoints are directly linked to the the school setting in Denmark.
8.5.2
Contextual Limitations of the Final MVP Test
The final test conducted as a part of this project was limited to a single school. In order to
draw broader conclusions about the success and applicability of the developed solutions,
further testing is needed across a larger amount of schools, representative of the diversity
of school types and contexts. While the results from Gladsaxe Privatskole represents only
one case, they nonetheless provide a valuable indication of what might be expected on
a larger scale. However, as a small school, Gladsaxe Privatskole may not reflect the
logistical complexities that could arise in larger institutions, where coordinating parallel
use of the makerspace across multiple classes may present new challenges in terms of
scheduling and equipment management.
Both students and teachers expressed enthusiasm for the makerspace, which aligns with
findings from DS­I. It is therefore reasonable to assume that similar responses could be
expected in comparable contexts. However, when it comes to the development of teach­
ers’ maker identity, no general conclusions can be drawn. The project primarily involved
STEM teachers, and only teachers from this subject area participated in the final test.
As such, it remains uncertain whether similar outcomes would emerge among teachers
from other disciplines, where didactic approaches and pedagogical traditions may differ
significantly. That said, there was a notable interest from teachers across other subjects,
who was curious about how the maker activities could be applied within their fields. Es­
pecially the music and visual art teacher showed high interest. The modular design of
the container allows for switching the equipment to more suited alternatives, but further
research would be needed to determine which type of equipment and activities are most
appropriate. Further investigations should include the development of modular curricula
applicable for other subjects and involve a broader range of teacher profiles.
Because the MVP test was conducted at an independent school, the findings cannot be
generalised to public schools. There are significant differences between the two school
types in terms of decision­making processes and available budget. Although Gladsaxe
Privatskole is a small institution with limited financial resources, the final test demonstrated
that Gnist can develop a viable business proposal suited to this context. This suggests that
the independent school sector holds strong market potential. However, no conclusions
can yet be made regarding public schools. Further research into municipal decision­
making structures and regulatory frameworks would be necessary before assessing the
sustainability of the current business model for public school implementation.
8.6
Actionable recommendations
Based on the results of the MVP test and considering the contextual limitation, it is rec­
ommended that Gnist will continue the work and do a full pilot study. This would include
involving diverse schools to cover the different contexts related to size and economic
foundation. To be representative for all elementary schools in Denmark, both private and
public schools should be involved, as well as teachers with diverse subject profiles. This
would include different configurations of the container, investigation of different equipment
packages, and further development of curricula, tailored to cover multiple subjects. Fur­
ther, the business proposal for private schools would need further testing and adaption to
176
==PAGE:190==
be viable in the public sector.
The next step for Gnist Education would be development of an online platform, where
schools are able to book the container and sign up for professional development courses.
The platform should accommodate the diverse needs and allow schools to adjust the
duration of the container at the school, select equipment and configure the interior, and
find multiple curricula examples.
8.7
Future opportunities for Gnist Education ApS
This study have shown great success for integrating the makerspace in a school context,
and there is a possibility to expand the current solution to also include after­school ac­
tivities. Since the makerspace is already positioned at the school, this would maximise
the utility of the makerspace, without requiring extra costs for transportation. Students
in the lower grades showed high interest in the space during the MVP test, and expand­
ing the offer to include after school activities as well, will add extra value for the schools.
However, this would require further exploration of how this would be operated to ensure
meaningful implementation and student safety in using the equipment.
Furthermore, the MVP test have proved that it is possible to place the makerspace on­
site for students to create prototypes, and have meaningful learning experiences, which
is also stated in a testimonial from the School Principal, see Figure 8.1. It is not only stu­
dents in elementary schools, that benefit from the learning opportunities in a makerspace.
The mobility of the container would make it possible to place it at high schools and uni­
versities to implement the prototyping possibilities. However, this would require further
explorations, as these institutions are significantly different than elementary schools, hav­
ing different requirements for learning activities and high amount of students in courses.
Therefore, this would give logistical challenges to ensure smooth flow in the container for
optimal usability.
Figure 8.1: Testimonial from Rune Norman Svendsen, School Principal at Gladsaxe Privatskole.
177
==PAGE:191==
9
Reflection of Work Process
Writing a master thesis do not come without its complications. This chapter will reflect
on the work process with a particular focus on the practical and collaborative dynamics
that have shaped both the outcome and the experience. The reflections are structured
around three key themes, and across all sections, both the challenges but also the unique
opportunities that extended beyond traditional academic settings are reflected upon.
9.1
Implications of Working Alongside a Startup
One of the most defining characteristics of this thesis project was its close collaboration
with the early­stage startup Gnist Education ApS. Rather that operating solely within an
academic framework, we became deeply embedded in the startup’s development trajec­
tory. From the very beginning of the project, our motivation extended beyond academic
inquiry. We were personally invested in seeing Gnist Education succeed, and this in­
evitably influenced how we prioritised our time and focus. This dual role introduced both
unique opportunities and significant tensions between methodological alignment and fast­
paced, opportunity­driven nature of startup environments.
In the early stages of the project, a considerable portion of our energy was spent on non­
thesis related tasks, such as preparing funding applications and participating in external
events. However, these applications formed a finishing goal early on in the project, which
helped direct our focus on what we wanted to achieve during the development. Our close
relationship with Gnist positioned us as de facto representatives of the company, particu­
larly in our affiliation with DTU Skylab. This gave us access to startup resources, mentor­
ship, and professional network, opportunities typically unavailable to academic projects.
However, it also demanded balancing competing expectations from both academics and
entrepreneurial domains.
The interactions with a functional startup also shaped the direction of the project in certain
ways. On several occasions, we were put in a position to make fast real­world decisions
that would have long­term implications. This have been an opportunity to experience how
decision­making processes function in the real world, but have been challenging in terms
of adhering to academic design processes. Critical decisions have occasionally been
based on immediate feasibility and intuition rather than academically grounded reasoning.
While this process compromised ideal design methodology, it allowed us to move beyond
the expectations and make a full MVP test at Gladsaxe Privatskole, something that would
not have been possible without startup funding and momentum.
Overall, the collaboration with Gnist Education enriched the project and brought it much
closer to actual implementation than most academic theses can hope to achieve. At the
same time, it placed us under pressure to act swiftly and often intuitively, sometimes at
the expense of academic depth. This reflects a broader challenge when design research
intersect with entrepreneurial practice in terms of balancing the need for thoughtful inves­
tigation with the demands of real­world progress.
9.2
The Scope and Coordination of Three Focus Areas
A key challenge throughout this project was navigating the complexity of working across
three distinct yet interrelated focus areas: the physical design of the makerspace, the
178
==PAGE:192==
development of curriculum and teacher support structures, and the formulation of a viable
business and implementation strategy. Rather than dividing the work into three separate
theses with narrow scopes, we made a deliberate decision to to write a joint thesis. This
approached allowed us to maintain a shared design vision and develop a fully integrated
solution that would not have been possible through isolated efforts.
In general, there was a close collaboration to cover all aspects related to integration of
makerspaces, though we always had our individual focus in mind. During the prescriptive
study, the team work became more fragmented, when developing individual solutions,
however close coordination was still in place to ensure a full solution for the final test. As
the project evolved and more knowledge was gathered, so did the scope of the project.
If this had happened in three separate theses, it would have required significant efforts to
update each other to ensure all solutions would fit together in a full package makerspace
concept.
In retrospect, our decision to work as a group, allowed us to achieve significantly more
than would have been possible through separate efforts. The complexity of designing an
educational platform, demands considerations of all aspects, including physical space,
pedagogy, and business. Although this approach required more coordination to integrate
the different areas in one final package, it proved to be one of the greatest strengths.
9.3
Time Management
Time management has been a challenge throughout the project. From the start, an ini­
tial timeline was created based on the DRM framework (Blessing & Chakrabarti, 2009),
allocating time to each phase with the understanding that adjustments would likely be
necessary as the project evolved. While this structured approach offered initial clarity,
it quickly became evident that the scope and complexity of each phase were difficult to
estimate in advance. In addition, several time estimates for activities proved to be overly
optimistic, as these required significantly more effort and iteration than anticipated, mak­
ing it difficult to adhere to the initial time plan.
In the beginning of the project, a lot of time was spent on startup related activities, driv­
ing focus away from the thesis. Although valuable, this diversion affected the early mo­
mentum of the academic work and created time pressure that persisted throughout the
process. To compensate for the delayed process, selected tasks were time­boxed, ac­
cepting that this would be on the cost of analytical depth. Even so, the research extended
well beyond the adjusted time frame, causing challenges in terms of integrating insights
across focus areas. One of the reasons herein, was difficulties in defining the point at
which the research was sufficiently complete to move forward. As understanding of the
scope deepened, new insights continuously emerged, leading to further adjustments in
both direction and content. This had further influence on the collaborative process, and
uneven progression, caused alignment difficulties in general. This highlights the impor­
tance of coordination in large collaborative thesis projects, particularly those that span
multiple focus areas and are situated within dynamic external contexts.
179
==PAGE:193==
10
Conclusion
Throughout this project the Gnist Education concept have been developed and refined to
fit a Danish School setting. This development represents a comprehensive and iterative
design process that integrates three key areas: The physical makerspace, a curricular
framework, and a business model that support the implementation. Together these ele­
ments form a unified and holistic solution that brings accessible, engaging, and scalable
maker activities to schools. This solution was shaped through the four research phases
of the DRM, Research Clarification (RC), Descriptive Study I (DS­I), Prescriptive Study
(PS), and Descriptive Study II (DS­II). The RC was used to scope the project and define
key assumptions and problem area. DS­I unfolded this problem area and transformed
assumption into knowledge point that shaped a design specification via requirements and
criteria. In the PS, the three overarching solutions were developed based on the design
specification, and refined using iterative prototyping, user testing, and co­creation. Lastly,
the DS­II validated the combined solution through a MVP test that was based on multiple
hypothesis and success criteria.
Three overarching research questions were defined in the RC to further guide the re­
search. These research question are addressed below.
RQ1: What factors are influencing a school’s decision on integrating a mak­
erspace in the Danish elementary school and how is this affecting Gnist Edu­
cation?
Based on the findings from the systematic literature review, empirical study and desktop
research, several key factors have been identified that influence a school’s decision to
integrate a makerspace. From the teacher’s perspective, a central factor is the teacher’s
sense of self­efficacy in planning and facilitating makerspace activities. This involves both
technical competencies and knowledge of pedagogical and didactic principles. Further,
it requires a shift in the teacher’s role from being the expert in the room to becoming a
facilitator who explores and experiments alongside the students.
Makerspaces are not a one­size­fits­all solution. They must be adaptable to the specific
context of each school and evolve over time. This requires a balanced mix of accessible,
flexible technologies that support both the educational value and the user experience.
Technical challenges, particularly related to 3D printing, can act as significant bottlenecks
in daily use. Streamlined workflows and smart, user­friendly solutions are therefore es­
sential to minimise these obstacles and enhance usability.
However, technical solutions alone are not enough. Teachers operate under significant
time pressure, and makerspace integration must not feel like an additional burden. Flexi­
ble and adaptable curriculum materials, opportunities for collaboration and visible recog­
nition of teacher’s work can increase both motivation and ownership. When teachers feel
supported, valued, and confident, they are more likely to adopt new practices.
Makerspaces hold great potential for inclusive learning, offering differentiated entry points
for students with diverse needs. This makes it even more important to design spaces and
activities that are accessible, meaningful, and responsive to classroom realities.
These findings carry direct implications for Gnist’s business model and implementation
strategy.
While independent schools offer a natural entry point to the market, public
180
==PAGE:194==
schools require alignment with municipal processes and longer decision cycles. A phased
strategy that begins with price­sensitive private­sector adoption and gradually builds cred­
ibility for broader integration is therefore recommended. Successful implementation de­
pends not only on the product itself, but also on how well it fits within the institutional,
economic, and relation realities of Danish schools.
For Gnist Education, this means that their offering must not only provide educational value
but also support practical implementation by reducing complexity, saving teachers’ prepa­
ration time, and enabling meaningful teaching experiences. The more confident and well­
supported teachers feel in using the makerspace, the more likely it is to be successfully
adopted within the school.
RQ2: How might a detailed design of the Gnist Education concept be devel­
oped to integrate the makerspace in a Danish school setting?
The Gnist Education concept has been developed through an iterative design process,
combining user­centres prototyping, business modelling, and real­world validation. At its
core, the mobile makerspace is built into a container, which has been tested and refined
throughout the design process. The physical design underwent several iterations with a
focus on equipment usability, spatial flow, and transportability, resulting in a classroom­
ready solution that can be deployed directly at school premises.
To address known bottleneck in makerspace activities, particularly around 3D printing, a
smart setup was implemented using Bambu Lab printers, some with the Swapmod modifi­
cation, and using the SimplyPrint platform. This enabled features such as remote access,
queue management, and overnight printing, significantly improving the overall user expe­
rience.
Recognising that technology alone is insufficient, a modular curriculum have been de­
veloped as the foundation for a professional development course for teachers. These
support both pedagogical integration and technical skill building, helping teachers feel
confident and capable in using the makerspace meaningfully in their teaching practice.
From a business perspective, a lean business model was iteratively developed and val­
idated through tools like the lean canvas and co­creation sessions with external stake­
holders, such as venture capital advisors. Early implementation efforts strategically target
independent schools, due to their more agile decision­making processes. This provides
a viable entry point before scaling to the public sector.
The final concept is therefore not just a physical product, but a service­based, scalable
learning platform. It balances pedagogical goals, teacher support, technical feasibility, ad
business viability, positioning Gnist Education as a robust offering for bringing makerspace
teaching into everyday school practice.
RQ3: How can the developed solution(s) be assessed and validated to strengthen
the integration of the Gnist Education concept and what significance will it have
for the startup?
A MVP test was setup and conducted at Gladsaxe Privatskole, where multiple areas and
solutions of the developed Gnist Education concept was assessed and validated. Multi­
ple hypothesis were created with corresponding success criteria, which were evaluated
through a mixed­method approach.
The MVP test served as a critical validation of the full makerspace concept, across tech­
nical, pedagogical, and organisational dimensions. The test confirmed the feasibility of
181
==PAGE:195==
developing a fully equipped mobile makerspace in a school setting. The container setup
with pre­installed equipment ensured minimal setup time and demonstrated the concept’s
potential as a plug­and­play solution.
The test also validated the impact of smart 3D printing solutions, specifically the integration
of Swapmod and SimplyPrint, which significantly improved the usability, efficiency, and
flow in the makerspace. These findings highlight the importance of technical reliability in
reducing teacher workload and enabling and enabling smooth classroom integration.
The developed curriculum modules proved effective in supporting student engagement
and served as a strong foundation for a future professional development course. While
the course itself was not tested, the MVP test confirmed the curriculum’s potential to
strengthen teacher confidence through hands­on experience. However, further testing
is necessary to evaluate the full impact of the training approach and to determine whether
teachers can independently facilitate makerspace activities or would benefit from co­
teaching support.
For Gnist Education as a startup, the test provided essential validation of the concept’s
value proposition. It demonstrated a viable delivery model, helped clarify pricing expec­
tations, and generated valuable user feedback. This positions the company to refine its
offering and move toward broader implementation, starting with independent schools and
potentially scaling to the public sector.
Throughout the project, several discussion points have emerged that inform both the cur­
rent solution and future refinement, development, and implementation strategies. These
include considerations related to spatial usability, teacher empowerment curricular adapt­
ability, and business feasibility, each offering clear direction for future iterations.
Overall, a detailed and multi­faceted design of Gnist Education concept has been suc­
cessfully developed and partially validated through a structured research­driven approach.
The project demonstrates the value of combining physical infrastructure, pedagogical
tools, and a sustainable business model into a cohesive offering. Notably, the MVP test
at Gladsaxe Privatskole provided promising initial insights into the concept’s viability and
educational potential.
However, further pilot testing is necessary to evaluate the concept in diverse school con­
texts and to ensure broader applicability across different teacher profiles, school types,
and subject areas. As such, the work conducted in this thesis lays a strong foundation,
while also highlighting the need for continued development and evaluation to realise the
full potential of the Gnist Education initiative.
182
==PAGE:196==
11
Learning Objectives
General Learning Objectives for a Master Thesis
1. Can identify and reflect on technical scientific issues and understand the in­
teraction between the various components that make up an issue.
Through the RC and DS­I phase, key technical and contextual challenges were
identified and mapped with multiple stakeholders and system components. Devel­
opment arenas were furthermore used to describe how these actors interacts.
2. Can, based on a clear academic profile, apply elements of current research at
an international level to develop ideas and solve problems.
Insight from multiple research touchpoints in DS­I were analysed, resulting in the
design specification based on requirements and criteria, which helped to further
develop and structure the solution.
3. Masters technical scientific methodologies, theories, and tools, and can take
a holistic view of and delimit a complex, open issue, put it into a broader aca­
demic and societal perspective, and, on this basis, propose a variety of pos­
sible actions while considering sustainability.
A holistic approach have been used to analyse the problem area, and solutions were
developed using a range of design methods and theories that address these issues.
4. Can develop relevant models, systems, technologies, and processes aimed
at solving technological problems.
A range of approaches such as iterative prototyping, user tests, and co­creation,
were used to find solutions, in the PS, to address the identified challenges and
barriers.
5. Can communicate and mediate research­based knowledge both orally and in
writing is familiar with and can seek out leading international research within
their specialist area.
A systematic literature review (SLR) was used to identify and analyse relevant and
current international academic research within the scope of the project.
6. Can work independently and reflect on own learning, academic development,
and specialisation.
Independent learning objectives were defined, and the scope of the project allowed
for in­depth exploration and solution oriented development.
7. Masters technical problem­solving at a high level through cross­disciplinary
teamwork, and can work with and manage all phases of a project – including
preparation of timetables, design, solution, and documentation.
A detailed timetable was created in collaboration in the team to ensure that all
phases of the project (RC, DS­I, PS, DS­II) along with documentation were com­
pleted within the given time frame.
Project Specific Learning Objectives
1. Impact analysis and Metrics: Utilise evaluation methods (KPIs, data, simula­
tions) to analyse how the physical container, curriculum, and strategic plan­
ning collectively can enhance the learning experience for students in the Dan­
183
==PAGE:197==
ish elementary school and promote the STEM education.
DS­II was used to assess and validate the developed solutions through an MVP user
test in a contextual setting. The solutions were validated on their potential impact
for the learning opportunities in a Danish school setting.
2. Prototyping, testing, and iterating: Gather insights from the users through
workshops and tests with prototypes to co­develop, refine, and optimise so­
lutions.
Testing sessions and co­creation workshops were conducted during PS to help the
development, refinement and optimisations of the solutions based on user insights
and feedback.
3. Holistic approach: Develop a system­level development arena model to iden­
tify, address, and overcome potential barriers to implementation at all three
levels (artefact, complex system, and engineering system), integrating the
physical container, curriculum, and business model.
Situational mapping and development arena models were created and used to gain
a holistic overview and understand the three system levels (artefact, complex, and
engineering), stakeholders, and actor worlds. These were updated throughout the
project, to reflect the changes based on increased knowledge of the scope.
4. User­oriented Industrial design: Apply user centric design principles to de­
velop and optimise the makerspace in the container to be intuitive and user­
friendly while maximising the functionality.
The makerspace were developed based on relevant literature findings, empirical
studies and user­test feedback to create a welcoming environment that allow greater
usability and user engagement. Critical touchpoint such as equipment bottleneck
were also optimised to significantly improve the overall user experience in the mak­
erspace.
5. Product­service system: Design a PSS framework that allows for value cre­
ation of both the physical and service components in the makerspace to meet
the diverse needs of its users.
Certain technical barriers and overall challenges were identified that impacted the
usability of the makerspace equipment. A solution that combined the physical el­
ement such as 3D printers and service platforms (SimplyPrint) were developed to
improve overall user experience and address general user needs.
6. Entrepreneurship and Business model: Design and develop a business model
ensuring long­term financial and operational viability, evaluate potential risks
and make strategies to mitigate them effectively.
A business model canvas and implementation plan were developed to address how
the Gnist Education concept can be implemented on independent schools.
7. Systems design engineering: Overcome logistical challenges in the opera­
tional workflow, supply chain, and implementation strategy for the business
case, ensuring seamless integration at schools.
An implementation strategy and supply chain overview were developed to enable
the effective rollout of the Gnist Education concept to more schools in the future.
8. User involved co­creation: Develop and facilitate co­creation workshops with
teachers to collaboratively design curriculum materials, ensuring alignment
with their needs and teaching practices.
Several co­creation workshops were conducted with teachers in PS to align so­
184
==PAGE:198==
lutions with the practical needs of practice and collaboratively develop curriculum
materials.
9. Behavioural design: Apply principles of behavioural design to develop strate­
gies and tools that encourage teachers to integrate the makerspace into their
everyday curriculum.
Throughout the project, factors influencing teachers’ willingness and ability to inte­
grate makerspace activities were examined, leading to the development of solution
that support and motivate adoption of new pedagogical approaches by enhancing
teacher confidence.
